The Consistent Criterion for Prophethood: Maturidi's Argument
Maturidi presents a methodological argument to prove Muhammad's prophethood to those who reject him but accept other prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses a universal criterion of prophethood to argue for Muhammad's prophethood against those who accept other prophets but reject him.

Core Idea
The meaning that makes a sign a sign is universal; apply it consistently.

Maturidi presents a powerful methodological argument that turns the tables on those who accept some prophets but deny Muhammad. Instead of simply listing miracles, he forces the opponent to state their own criterion for recognizing a prophet. Once that criterion is identified, it must be applied fairly to Muhammad. This passage demonstrates the rigorous dialectical reasoning that characterizes Maturidi's approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the opponent's position
The opponent accepts some prophets (like Moses and Jesus) but rejects Muhammad. Understand that the argument is designed for such partial rejecters.
3
Understand the key move
Maturidi asks what criterion they used to accept their prophets. If they give a meaning that entails prophethood, that same meaning must apply to Muhammad. The underlying meaning that makes a sign a sign is universal, even if the signs differ.
This is the core of the argument: consistency.
4
Examine the four responses
If the opponent claims agreement with Muslims on the criterion, Maturidi provides four responses. Read each one carefully, noting how each undermines the opponent's position.
The four responses are listed in the commentary.
5
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider why this approach is more foundational than just comparing miracles. It forces the opponent to either accept Muhammad or lose their basis for accepting any prophet.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القول فيها بيننا وبين المقرين بالرسل جملة والمنكرين لبعضهم على الإشارة أن نسألهم عن المعنى الذي له أقر به أو أقر به سلفهم. فإن أشاروا إلى معنى على تحقيق أنه من المعاني التي توجب النبوة، ألزمناهم في نبوة محمد صلى الله عليه ذلك المعنى بعينه، وإن كانت الآيات مختلفة بأنفسها، فإن المعنى الذي صارت به الآية آية غير مختلف. وإن تعلقوا بظواهر الآيات لن يجدوا لأحد مثل الذي لمحمد عليه السلام في الأعجوبة والرفعة، أو يخرجان على أمر واحد وإن ادعوا موافقتنا إياهم فإن جواب ذلك يخرج من وجوه. أحدها يُسأل عن علتهم قبل كوننا وظهور موافقتنا. والثاني إنا أقررنا بما ثبت لنا أن الذي أخبرنا بهم رسول وأنتم تنكرونه، سقط دليلكم، فما برهانكم؟ والثالث أن يقابلوا بالفرق الذين لم يقروا بها ادعوا. والرابع أن يقال: إنما أقررنا نحن ممن قد أقر بنبوة نبينا، فإن كان من يدعونه هو فقد ثبتت نبوة نبينا، وإن لم يكن هو فما الدلالة على نبوة من ادعيتم له النبوة ليسلم لكم ما أردتم؟ وبالله المعونة.
Then the statement regarding it between us and those who affirm the messengers in general and deny some of them, by way of indication, is that we ask them about the meaning by which they affirmed him, or by which their predecessors affirmed him. If they point to a meaning upon verification that it is among the meanings that entail prophethood, we impose upon them in the prophethood of Muhammad, peace be upon him, that same meaning, even if the signs are different in themselves; for the meaning by which the sign becomes a sign is not different. And if they cling to the external aspects of the signs, they will not find for anyone the like of what is for Muhammad, peace be upon him, in miraculousness and high rank, or they both emerge upon one matter. And if they claim agreement with us, then the answer to that proceeds from several aspects. One: they are asked about their cause before our existence and the appearance of our agreement. Second: we have affirmed what is established for us that the one who informed us of them is a messenger, and you deny him; your proof fails, so what is your proof? Third: they should be confronted with the sects who did not affirm what they claimed. Fourth: it is said: we only affirm from one who has affirmed the prophethood of our prophet; if the one they claim is he, then the prophethood of our prophet is established; and if he is not he, then what is the evidence for the prophethood of the one for whom you claimed prophethood so that what you want is conceded to you? And with God is assistance.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is a methodological argument for proving the prophethood of Muhammad to those who accept some prophets but reject him, especially the People of the Book. Maturidi instructs the reader to ask such opponents what criterion they or their predecessors used to recognize a prophet. If they identify a specific meaning (criterion) that entails prophethood, then that same criterion must be applied to Muhammad, for the underlying meaning that makes a miracle a sign is universal, even if the miracles themselves differ in form. If they instead rely on the outward appearances of miracles, they will not find anyone whose miracles and rank match Muhammad's. If they claim to agree with Muslims in the criterion, Maturidi provides four possible responses: (1) ask them what their reason was before we existed or before we declared agreement—i.e., their acceptance must have been based on something independent of us; (2) since we affirm the prophets we know from our messenger, and they deny that messenger, their argument collapses—they must provide their own proof; (3) confront them with other sects who do not accept the prophets they claim, showing the inconsistency; (4) argue that we only accept prophets from one who has affirmed our prophet—if the prophet they claim is the same as ours, then our prophet is established; if not, they must provide evidence for their claimed prophet. The argument is rigorous and dialectical, forcing the opponent to either accept Muhammad or lose their own basis for prophethood.

Key Terms
المعنى الذي يوجب النبوة
آيات
الأعجوبة
الرفعة
البرهان
العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument is merely about comparing miracles, but it is fundamentally about establishing a consistent criterion for prophethood.
Correction: Maturidi's focus is not on the magnitude of miracles but on the universal meaning that makes any miracle a sign of prophethood. The argument forces consistency.

Checkpoint
What is the first of the four responses to someone who claims agreement with Muslims on the criterion of prophethood?
A) Ask them about their cause before the Muslims' existence and before their agreement appeared.
B) Demand that they provide a miracle equal to Muhammad's.
C) Accuse them of inconsistency in accepting some prophets and rejecting others.
D) Point out that their scripture was corrupted.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's argument forces opponents to apply their own criterion for prophethood consistently to Muhammad, or lose their basis for accepting any prophet.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What criterion do you personally use to accept something as true from God? Have you applied that same criterion to the message of Muhammad, or do you hold him to a different standard?
?
If you were to ask yourself the same question Maturidi poses to the opponent—'What meaning made you accept any prophet?'—would your answer stand up to scrutiny? Or have you inherited beliefs without examining the underlying criterion?
?
Maturidi's argument forces consistency. Is there any area in your life where you accept a claim based on evidence you refuse to apply to something else, especially regarding religious truth? Will you commit today to examining your own criteria.

Consistency of Prophetic Signs
Maturidi refutes Christian argument for prophethood by consistency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes Christian claims about prophethood by using a consistent criterion for recognizing prophetic signs.

Core Idea
If a sign proves prophethood, the same logic must apply to Muhammad.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi challenges Christian reasoning about prophethood. He argues that the underlying meaning that makes a miracle a sign of prophethood is universal, not limited to one tradition. He then provides four powerful responses if Christians claim that Muslims agree with them.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: a core argument followed by four enumerated responses.
Pay attention to the phrase 'فإن المعنى الذي صارت [به] الآية آية غير مختلف' – the meaning that makes a sign a sign is not different.
2
Understand the core argument
Identify Maturidi's main point: if Christians accept miracles as proof of prophethood, they must accept Muhammad's miracles because the underlying meaning is the same.
3
Examine the four responses
Read the four numbered responses carefully. Each is a different angle to counter the claim that Muslims agree with Christians.
Try to summarize each response in your own words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنه من المعاني [التي] توجب النبوة، ألزمناهم في نبوة محمد صلى الله عليه ذلك المعنى بعينه، وإن كانت الآيات مختلفة بأنفسها، فإن المعنى الذي صارت [به] الآية آية غير مختلف. وإن تعلقوا بظواهر الآيات لن يجدوا لأحد مثل الذي لمحمد عليه السلام في الأعجوبة والرفعة، أو يخرجان على أمر واحد وإن ادعوا موافقتنا إياهم فإن جواب ذلك يخرج من وجوه. أحدها يُسأل عن علتهم قبل كوننا وظهور موافقتنا. والثاني إنا أقررنا بما ثبت لنا أن الذي أخبرنا بهم رسول وأنتم تنكرونه، سقط دليلكم، فما برهانكم؟ والثالث أن يقابلوا بالفرق الذين لم يقروا بها ادعوا. والرابع أن يقال: إنما أقررنا نحن ممن قد أقر بنبوة نبينا، فإن كان من يدعونه هو فقد ثبتت نبوة نبينا، وإن لم يكن هو فما الدلالة على نبوة من ادعيتم له النبوة ليسلم لكم ما أردتم؟ وبالله المعونة.
That from the meanings that necessitate prophethood, we made them liable in the prophethood of Muhammad, peace be upon him, that same meaning exactly. Even if the signs are different in themselves, the meaning by which the sign becomes a sign is not different. If they cling to the apparent meanings of the signs, they will not find for anyone the like of what Muhammad, peace be upon him, has in wonder and elevation, or they proceed on one matter. And if they claim that we agree with them, then the answer to that comes from aspects: First, they are asked about their reason before our existence and the emergence of our agreement. Second, we acknowledge what is established for us that the one who informed us about them is a messenger, and you deny him, so your proof falls, so what is your proof? Third, they are confronted with the sects who did not acknowledge them [the prophets] when they claimed. Fourth, it is said: We only acknowledge from those who have acknowledged the prophethood of our prophet. If the one they claim is he, then the prophethood of our prophet is established. If it is not he, then what is the evidence for the prophethood of the one for whom you claim prophethood so that what you desire is established? And with God is help.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a Christian argument regarding prophethood. Maturidi begins by stating that the meaning that makes something a sign of prophethood is consistent, even if the signs themselves differ. If Christians accept their own prophet's miracles as proof, they must apply the same criterion to Muhammad. He then presents four responses if they claim we agree with them: (1) they are asked for their reason before we existed; (2) we accept the messenger who informed us, but they deny him, so their proof collapses; (3) they are confronted with other sects who also made claims; (4) we only acknowledge prophets recognized by our prophet. If the one they claim is Muhammad, then his prophethood is proven; otherwise, they need evidence for their prophet.

الأعجوبة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi is rejecting all non-Islamic claims to prophethood categorically.
Correction: Maturidi is using consistency arguments: if Christians accept their prophet's miracles, they must apply the same standard to Muhammad. He does not reject their prophets outright but uses their own logic against them.

Maturidi’s argument shows that consistency is key. What truth have you been avoiding because it would require you to change? Are you willing to follow the logic wherever it leads, even if it unsettles your current understanding?

Refuting Miracles as Proof of Sonship: A Comparative Argument
Maturidi compares Jesus' miracles with those of other prophets to refute sonship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the claim that Jesus' miracles prove his divine sonship by showing that other prophets performed similar or greater miracles and that Jesus exhibited human weaknesses.

Core Idea
Miracles do not prove divinity; many prophets performed wonders greater than Jesus'.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi directly challenges the Christian argument that Jesus' miracles indicate his special status as son of God. By comparing Jesus' wonders with those of earlier prophets such as Moses, Elijah, and Elisha, he demonstrates that miracles are not unique to Jesus and therefore cannot prove divine sonship. Maturidi also highlights Jesus' human entreaties and the dishonor of crucifixion, further undermining the claim of exalted status.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation carefully. Notice the flow of argument: Maturidi presents a Christian claim and then refutes it point by point.
Pay attention to names of prophets and their miracles.
2
Identify the refutation structure
The passage uses a series of objections and answers. Identify each Christian claim and Maturidi's counterexample from another prophet's life.
The refutation is comparative: each miracle of Jesus is matched with a similar or greater miracle of another prophet.
3
Reflect on the key point
Ask yourself: Why does Maturidi emphasize that Jesus wept and prayed? How does the crucifixion argument weaken the Christian position?
Human weakness and dishonor contradict the idea of special divinity.
4
Summarize the conclusion
After reviewing all comparisons, state Maturidi's final point: miracles are not evidence for sonship because many prophets performed them. True honor is shown by God's protection, as with Elijah.
The argument is cumulative: no single miracle of Jesus is unparalleled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: من أن الله أظهر منه عجائب. قيل: وقد أظهر من موسى، فقولوا : هو ابن آخر. فإن زعمتم أن ذلك كان بدعاء وتضرع فمثله أمر غيره. مع ما يحكى عن عيسى أنه كان يقول ليلة الأخذ : «اللهم إن كان من مشيئتك أن تصرف هذه الكأس المرة عن أحد فاصرفها عني . فإن قيل : كان من عيسى البكاء والتضرع ليعلم الناس قيل مثله من موسى. وبعد، فإنه وموسى كانا يصليان نحو بيت المقدس ويتضرعان. ثم البكاء والتضرع فعل الطباع لا تمتنع عنهما، فما معنى التعليم؟ ثم إن استحق هو ذلك بالعمل لزم ذلك في موسى وغيره. فإن قيل : استحق ذلك بإحياء الموتى لا غير، قيل : قد أحيا حزقيل إنسانًا . فإن عارض بالكثرة، قيل : اليهود يقولون موسى كان أكثر منه . قال الفقيه رحمه الله : وله إحياء عصا ميتة ثعبانا غير مرة، فهو أعظم. وإن احتج بإطعامه البشر الكثير من طعام يسير عورض بنبينا ، إنه أحدث في إناء دقيقاً لم يكن فيه فإن قيل : صير الماء خمرا، قيل : اليسع أحدثه بلا عدة في آنية لامرأة ثم صيره زيتاً وإن احتج بالمشي على الماء فهم يقرون بذلك ليوشع بن نون ولإيليا واليسع . وإن استدلوا بالرفع إلى السماء فهم يقرون بذلك لإيليا ، وقالوا : ارتفع إلى السماء بمشهد من جماعة. وإن احتجوا بإبراء الأكمه والأبرص ونحو ذلك فإحياء الميت أعظم منه، وقد أقروا به لإيليا واليسع . مع ما عليهم في إقرارهم أن اليهود صلبوه وهزؤا به . فإن كان الأول يدل على التعظيم فهذا يدل على التصغير . وهلا صنع كصنيع إيليا حيث أتوه أن أرسل عليهم نارا فأكلتهم، أكرمه الله به. وإن رجعوا إلى إظهار العجائب في تحقيق التخصيص عورضوا بمن ذكرت .
He said: Because God showed from him wonders. It is said: And He showed from Moses, so say: he is another son. If you claim that that was by supplication and entreaty, then likewise is the matter of other than him. Along with what is narrated from Jesus that he used to say on the night of the taking: 'O God, if it is Your will to turn this bitter cup from anyone, then turn it from me.' If it is said: From Jesus there was weeping and entreaty to teach people, the like of it is said from Moses. Moreover, he and Moses used to pray towards Jerusalem and entreat. Then weeping and entreaty are actions of nature that are not impossible from them, so what is the meaning of teaching? Then if he deserved that by action, it would be necessary for Moses and others. If it is said: He deserved that by raising the dead alone, it is said: Ezekiel revived a human. If he objects by quantity, it is said: The Jews say Moses was more than him. The jurist (may God have mercy on him) said: And he (Moses) revived a dead stick as a snake more than once, so it is greater. And if he argues by his feeding a great multitude from little food, he is opposed by our Prophet (Muhammad), who produced flour in a vessel that was not in it. If it is said: He turned water into wine, it is said: Elisha produced it without a vessel for a woman, then turned it into oil. And if he argues by walking on water, they acknowledge that for Joshua son of Nun, and for Elijah and Elisha. And if they argue by the ascension to heaven, they acknowledge that for Elijah, and they said: He ascended to heaven in the sight of a group. And if they argue by healing the blind and leper and the like, raising the dead is greater than that, and they have acknowledged it for Elijah and Elisha. Along with what is incumbent upon them in their acknowledgment that the Jews crucified him and mocked him. So if the first indicates honor, this indicates belittlement. And why did he not act as Elijah acted when they came to him, that he sent down fire upon them and it consumed them, God honored him with it? And if they return to the manifestation of wonders in achieving specification, they are opposed by those I mentioned.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Christian argument that Jesus' miracles prove his divine sonship. The author (Maturidi) begins by noting that if wonders prove sonship, then Moses should also be considered a son since God showed wonders through him. The Christian might object that Jesus' wonders were through his own power, not supplication, but the author counters that Jesus also prayed and wept, showing human weakness. Furthermore, Jesus' tears and prayers are natural human actions, not exclusive to him. If the Christian argues that Jesus deserved sonship by his deeds, then Moses and others would also deserve it. The author then systematically compares Jesus' specific miracles with those of other prophets: raising the dead (Ezekiel, Moses), feeding multitudes (Muhammad, Elisha), walking on water (Joshua, Elijah, Elisha), ascension (Elijah), healing (Moses? but here he says raising the dead is greater and performed by Elijah and Elisha). He also points out that Jesus was crucified and mocked, which dishonors him, unlike Elijah who was honored by fire from heaven. The conclusion is that miracles do not prove special status or sonship, as many prophets performed similar or greater wonders.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Christian belief that Jesus' miracles (such as raising the dead, walking on water, ascension, etc.) are unique and prove that he is the Son of God in a special sense (divine sonship).
Correction: Maturidi shows that every miracle attributed to Jesus has a parallel or greater miracle performed by other prophets (Moses, Elijah, Elisha, Ezekiel, Joshua, Muhammad). Therefore miracles cannot be evidence of a unique divine nature.

Key Takeaway
Miracles performed by prophets are not proofs of divine sonship, because many prophets performed wonders equal to or greater than those of Jesus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a success or a 'miracle' in your life, do you ever feel that you deserve special honor or status because of it? How does the lesson that many prophets performed miracles humble that pride? Will you consciously remind yourself that your gifts are from God and not a mark of superiority?
?
The Christian argument used Jesus' miracles to claim he was more than human. In your own life, do you ever use your talents or achievements to claim a position above others, forgetting that others have similar or greater gifts? How can you actively combat this tendency starting today?
?
Jesus wept and prayed in his moment of trial, showing complete reliance on God. Do you find it difficult to show vulnerability and dependence on God, preferring to appear strong? How can the example of prophets—who both performed miracles and wept—free you to be honest about your weakness before Allah?

A Tu Quoque Defense of Divine Wisdom
Maturidi rebuts an objection by invoking dualist counter-argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses a tu quoque argument to defend divine wisdom against the objection that God killing prophets while sparing enemies is unwise.

Core Idea
Maturidi turns the opponent's logic against them: the same reasoning proves too much.

In this passage, Maturidi faces a common objection: if God can slay His prophets yet spare His enemies, then His actions seem inconsistent with wisdom. Rather than directly defending the consistency, Maturidi points out that the opponent's own argument can be used by dualists to attack the opponent's position. This is a classic 'you too' argument, showing that the objection is not unique to Maturidi's theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the concise style of Maturidi's argument.
The phrase 'أنابهم' may be a variant of 'أصابهم' meaning 'killed'.
2
Identify the objection and response
The opponent claims God's slaying prophets and sparing enemies is unwise. Maturidi's response is not a direct defense but a reversal: he points out that dualists make the same argument against the opponent.
3
Understand the tu quoque structure
Maturidi argues that if the opponent's logic is valid, then the dualists' parallel argument against the opponent is also valid, making the opponent's objection self-defeating. This defuses the criticism without needing to prove divine wisdom is consistent.
This is a logical move: showing the criticism applies equally to the critic's own position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قال من قتل أنابهم الأنبياء، فهو فيما أنا بهم موجود، وفيما أبقى أعداءه قائم؛ ثم لم يخرج ذلك من الحكمة. بل استدل إخوانه أن الذي يفعل هذا غير حكيم، فما الذي يجيبهم فهو في الأول جواب.
And what he said about the killing of the prophets—that He [God] is present with them, and He remains [in] what He leaves of His enemies; then that does not remove it from wisdom. Rather, his brothers [the dualists] argued that the one who does this is not wise, so what answer they give is the same as the first [your] answer.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding God's justice. The opponent (al-Ka'bi) apparently argued that if God could kill prophets and yet spare His enemies (i.e., disbelievers), that would be inconsistent with divine wisdom. Maturidi turns the argument around: he says that the dualists (مجوس) argue that the one who does this (i.e., the opponent's God) is not wise. The point is that the same logic the opponent uses to object to the Ash'ari/Maturidi position can be used by the dualists against the Mu'tazili. So if the opponent cannot answer the dualist, his objection against Maturidi fails. In other words, it is a tu quoque argument: the opponent's own position faces an identical difficulty, so his objection is not valid.

Key Terms
قتل (killing)
أنبياء (prophets)
أعداء (enemies)
حكمة (wisdom)
استدل (argue)
إخوان (brothers, here dualists)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Maturidi is endorsing that God acts unwisely or that the objection is valid.
Correction: Maturidi is not admitting the objection; he is showing that the opponent's argument is self-defeating because it can be turned against them by dualists.

Checkpoint
What is Maturidi's response to the claim that God killing prophets while sparing enemies is unwise?
A) He admits it is unwise but argues God can do as He wills
B) He says the dualists would use the same reasoning against the Mu'tazili, thus the objection is invalid
C) He denies that God kills prophets
D) He says the enemies are not really spared

Key Takeaway
Maturidi defends divine wisdom by showing that the opponent's objection, if valid, would also prove too much against their own position when used by dualists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are criticized for your beliefs, do you ever use arguments that you would reject if someone else used them against you? This passage shows that inconsistency can unravel your position. Where in your own life have you been inconsistent in defending God's wisdom?
?
The dualists used the same logic against the opponent. Have you ever accepted a argument only because it supported your view, but dismissed it when it was turned against you? How would you feel if your own reasoning was used to attack what you hold most dear?
?
Maturidi's tu quoque forces the opponent to apply their own criterion consistently. Can you think of a belief or action in your life that you defend with a reasoning you would not accept from someone else? What would it cost you to be consistent?

Willing an Act vs. Willing the Sin
Maturidi explains the distinction between willing a sinful act and willing the sin itself, using the example of the Prophet and a Quranic verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between willing an act that results in sin and willing the sin itself, and why this distinction is important for understanding the Prophet's and God's will.

Core Idea
Willing a sinful act for a good purpose is not the same as willing the sin.

This passage from Imam Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid addresses a subtle but critical point: can a prophet or God will something that is sinful? The answer requires distinguishing between the act itself and the sin inherent in it. By examining the Prophet's will for the defeat of polytheists, Maturidi clarifies a principle that preserves divine justice and prophetic infallibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then the literal translation. Let the argument flow naturally before dissecting it.
The passage includes a question, analysis, evidence, and a counter-objection.
2
Identify the key distinction
Focus on where Maturidi states that willing an act that is a sin is not like willing the sin. Find the sentence and note the example from the Quran.
The distinction is central to the whole passage.
3
Examine the counter-objection
Notice the objection about the Prophet being pleased with the defeat. How does Maturidi refute it?
This reinforces the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم سئل عن إرادة رسول الله انهزام المشركين، فزعم أنه أراد لينظروا فيما دعاهم إليه. قال الفقيه رحمه الله : فالانهزام طاعة أو معصية، وكذلك الحال إلى وقت النظر وفي ذلك دوام على المعصية؟ لا بد أن يقول: معصية. فيجوز أن يراد به ـ لا على قصدها ـ لبعض المصالح. ومثله قوله: ﴿إني أريد أن تبوء بإثمي وإثمك﴾؛ إنه يجوز إرادة فعل هو معصية لا على قصد عصيان، وكذلك معاصي المؤمنين كلها كانت على أفعال من عاصيهم، وإن لم يريدوا معصية الله، بل لو أرادوا الكفروا. فهذا يبيّن أن إرادة فعل يكون من فاعله معصية لا يكون كإرادة المعصية. فمثله إرادة الله فعل الكافر ليكون منه معصية، أو فعله الشتم ليكون شتماً قبيحاً لا يكون كإرادة الشتم والمعصية. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم عارض بأن رسول الله رضي منهم الانهزام وذلك فاسد، لأن الانهزام منهم لم يكن الرسول الله أو الله فيتكلم فيه بالرضا وغير الرضا. ولا قوة إلا بالله. يقول الله تعالى: ﴿وقال الذي آمن يا قوم إني أخاف عليكم مثل يوم الأحزاب مثل دأب قوم نوح وعاد وثمود والذين من بعدهم وما الله يريد ظلماً للعباد﴾
Then he was asked about the will of the Messenger of God for the defeat of the polytheists, and he claimed that he willed so that they would look into what he called them to. The Faqih (may God have mercy on him) said: Is the defeat an act of obedience or disobedience? And likewise the situation until the time of consideration, and in that is persistence in disobedience? He must say: disobedience. So it is permissible to will it — not intending it — for some benefits. And similarly His saying: 'Indeed I want you to bear my sin and your sin'; it is permissible to will an act that is disobedience without intending the sin, and likewise all the sins of the believers were according to the actions of those who disobeyed them, even if they did not intend disobedience to God, rather if they intended, they would have disbelieved. This shows that willing an act that from its doer is disobedience is not like willing the disobedience. Similarly, God's willing the act of the disbeliever to be disobedience from him, or His willing the act of insult to be an ugly insult, is not like willing the insult and disobedience. There is no power except with God. Then he objected that the Messenger of God was pleased with their defeat, and that is corrupt, because the defeat from them was not the Messenger of God or God, so that one speaks of being pleased or not pleased. There is no power except with God. God Almighty says: 'And the one who believed said, O my people, indeed I fear for you the like of the day of the allies, like the behavior of the people of Noah, 'Ad, and Thamud and those after them, and God does not will injustice for the servants.'
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage is a question-and-answer about the Prophet's will for the defeat of the polytheists. Someone claimed the Prophet willed their defeat so they would consider his call. The Faqih asks: is defeat an act of obedience or disobedience? It is disobedience. But it is permissible to will it for some benefit without intending the sin itself. He cites the Quranic verse 'I want you to bear my sin and your sin' (al-Ma'idah 5:29) as an example of willing an act of disobedience without intending the sin. The same applies to believers' sins: they are actions of those who disobey, but the believers do not intend disobedience to God. The passage concludes that willing an act that results in disobedience is not the same as willing the disobedience. Then a counter-objection states that the Prophet was pleased with their defeat, but the author dismisses this because the defeat is not from the Prophet or God, so one cannot speak of pleasure or displeasure. The passage ends with a verse from al-Mu'min (40:30-31) to reinforce that God does not will injustice.

Key Terms
إرادة
انهزام
طاعة
معصية
قصد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that willing an act that is inherently sinful makes one a sinner.
Correction: The passage distinguishes between willing the act itself and willing the sin; the former can be permissible if done for a beneficial purpose without intending disobedience.

Checkpoint
According to the Faqih, why is it permissible for the Prophet to will the defeat of the polytheists even though defeat is disobedience?
A) Because the defeat was not actually disobedience.
B) Because he willed it for a beneficial purpose without intending the sin.
C) Because the defeat was an act of obedience.
D) Because the Prophet did not actually will it.

Key Takeaway
Willing an act that results in sin for a beneficial purpose is not equivalent to intending disobedience; this preserves divine justice and prophetic integrity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Prophet willed the polytheists' defeat so they would reflect. Think of a time you wanted something that involved another's wrongdoing. Did you examine your intention to ensure you weren't complicit in the sin itself? How will you purify your intentions going forward?
?
The son of Adam says, 'I want you to bear my sin and your sin'—a will for an act, not the sin. Are there desires in your heart that involve sin for what you think is a good reason? Will you now commit to distinguishing between willing the act and willing the sin before God?
?
God wills acts that result in disobedience from the disbeliever, but He does not will the disobedience. How does this change your view of God's role in your own mistakes? Will you stop blaming God for your sins and take full responsibility, while trusting His ultimate wisdom?

First Response: The Polytheists' Claim of Divine Command
Maturidi gives the first answer: the polytheists claimed a command, analogous to past deviants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's first refutation: that the polytheists' argument is actually a claim that God commanded their shirk, similar to earlier nations who justified immorality by alleging divine command.

Core Idea
The polytheists' 'God willed it' is a disguised false claim of command.

Maturidi begins by analyzing the polytheists' statement not as a theological query but as a pretext. He compares it to previous groups who claimed divine authorization for their sins. This first response exposes the logical fallacy by pointing to analogous Quranic condemnations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Maturidi directly links the polytheists's argument to earlier Quranic examples.
2
Identify the analogy
Maturidi compares the polytheists' claim to two Quranic verses. Find them and see how they condemn the same reasoning.
The verses are 7:28 and 3:78.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why calling something 'God's will' does not make it a command. How does this answer the polytheists?
This shows the polytheists are not making a valid argument but rather a false excuse, similar to previous rejecters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من احتج منهم بقوله : سيقول الذين أشركوا لو شاء الله ما أشركنا، الآية ، فالجواب لذلك من أوجه ثلاثة أحدها أنهم ادعوا به الأمر، كقوله: وإذا فعلوا فاحشة قالوا وجدنا عليها آباءنا والله أمرنا بها، وكذلك قوله : وإن منهم لفريقا يلوون ألسنتهم بالكتاب، الآية . والثاني أنهم لَمّا أُوعِدوا في ذلك قد أُمهلوا، فلما أمهلوا ظنوا كذب الرسل وحسبوا أن ذلك مما لله فيه الرضا، وإلا لم يكن يمهلهم، وكذلك ظن أصحاب السبت . وذلك كقوله تعالى: حتى إذا استيأس الرسل، الآية . والثالث أن يكونوا قالوه على الاستهزاء بالمؤمنين بما يدعون أن كل شيء بمشيئة الله، كقول الإنسان: أإذا ما مت لسوف أخرج حيّا، إنه قال ذلك على الاستهزاء بالمؤمنين، وإن كان ذلك حقاً؛ وكذلك قول المنافقين: نشهد إنك لرسول الله، ولكن ذلك لما كان الهزوء طعنوا به، فمثله الأول. والله أعلم . وأيّد ذلك آخر الآية : قل فلله الحجة البالغة فلو شاء لهداكم أجمعين، وغير ذلك، ولا يحتمل لما مر بيانه.
Then whoever among them argues with His saying: 'Those who associated partners will say: If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners'—the verse—so the answer to that is from three aspects: First, that they claimed thereby a command, like His saying: 'And when they commit an immorality, they say: We found our fathers upon it, and Allah has commanded us to it,' and likewise His saying: 'And indeed among them is a party who twist their tongues with the Book'—the verse. Second, that when they were threatened in that, they were given respite, so when they were given respite, they thought the messengers were lying and supposed that He is pleased with that, otherwise He would not have given them respite, and likewise the opinion of the companions of the Sabbath. And that is like His saying: 'Until when the messengers despaired'— the verse. Third, that they said it in mockery of the believers, because they claim that everything is by the will of Allah, like the saying of man: 'What! When I am dead, I will be brought forth alive?' He said that in mockery of the believers, even though that is true; and likewise the saying of the hypocrites: 'We bear witness that you are the messenger of Allah,' but that, because it was mockery, they attacked with it. So the first is like that. And Allah knows best. And the end of the verse supports that: 'Say: To Allah belongs the conclusive argument; if He willed, He would have guided you all,' and other than that, and it is not possible due to what has been explained.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the polytheists' argument based on the Quranic verse 'Those who associated partners will say: If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners' (Quran 6:148). The author (Maturidi) provides three distinct responses. First, the polytheists claimed their actions were commanded by God, but this is analogous to others who justified their sins by claiming divine command, as in Quran 7:28 and 3:78— thus they misinterpret divine will as a command. Second, the polytheists misinterpreted God's granting them respite as approval, thinking that if God were displeased, He would punish immediately. This is similar to the people of the Sabbath who assumed God's forbearance meant acceptance. The Quranic verse 'Until when the messengers despaired' (12:110) shows that respite does not imply approval. Third, the polytheists said this in mockery of the believers, similar to the one who scoffs at resurrection (Quran 19:66) or the hypocrites who mockingly testify to prophethood (63:1). Even if the statement is true in itself, they intended it as ridicule. The verse's conclusion 'Say: To Allah belongs the conclusive argument; if He willed, He would have guided you all' (6:149) supports that God's will is not a justification for shirk, because His will is just and He has provided guidance. The passage asserts that the polytheists' argument does not stand after these explanations.

Key Terms
الأمر
مشيئة الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that if God wills something, it must be good or commanded.
Correction: The passage shows that the polytheists used this reasoning as an excuse; God's will of permissive decree does not imply His approval or command.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi claim the polytheists' argument actually represents?
A) A true understanding of divine will
B) A claim that God commanded their shirk
C) A request for guidance
D) A prophecy

Key Takeaway
The polytheists' 'God willed it' is a disguised claim of divine command, analogous to earlier excuses condemned in the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever catch yourself thinking 'If Allah had not willed, I wouldn't have done it'? How does Maturidi's point about claiming command expose the hidden arrogance in that thought? Will you repent from using God's will as an excuse today?
?
What are the 'commands' you claim from Allah to justify your own desires or negligence? Be specific about one area of your life where you have said 'God wills it' to avoid responsibility. Are you ready to change that now?
?
How often do you twist religious language to make your actions seem divinely sanctioned? Reflect on a recent conversation where you used 'Alhamdulillah' or 'Masha'Allah' to cover a poor choice. Will you commit to honest self-accountability from today?

Second Response: The Misinterpretation of Divine Respite
Maturidi's second answer: the polytheists misconstrued divine respite as approval.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's second refutation: that the polytheists misinterpreted God's granting them respite as a sign of approval, similar to the people of the Sabbath.

Core Idea
Delayed punishment does not mean divine approval.

Maturidi's second response targets a common misconception: that if God were angry at shirk, He would punish immediately. The polytheists took God's forbearance as endorsement. Maturidi draws a parallel to the people of the Sabbath who also mistook God's patience for acceptance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the cause-and-effect: they were threatened, then given respite, then assumed approval.
2
Understand the logic
Maturidi says: because God gave them time, they thought He was pleased. This is a false inference. Why is it false?
God may give respite to test or provide opportunity for repentance, not because He is pleased.
3
Connect to the Sabbath analogy
The people of the Sabbath also thought God's tolerance meant approval. Find the Quranic reference (though not quoted fully) and understand the lesson.
This analogy strengthens the refutation: similar thinking led to severe punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Then whoever among them argues with His saying: 'Those who associated partners will say: If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners'—the verse—so the answer to that is from three aspects: First, that they claimed thereby a command, like His saying: 'And when they commit an immorality, they say: We found our fathers upon it, and Allah has commanded us to it,' and likewise His saying: 'And indeed among them is a party who twist their tongues with the Book'—the verse. And that is like His saying: 'Until when the messengers despaired'— the verse. Third, that they said it in mockery of the believers, because they claim that everything is by the will of Allah, like the saying of man: 'What! When I am dead, I will be brought forth alive?' He said that in mockery of the believers, even though that is true; and likewise the saying of the hypocrites: 'We bear witness that you are the messenger of Allah,' but that, because it was mockery, they attacked with it. So the first is like that. And Allah knows best. And the end of the verse supports that: 'Say: To Allah belongs the conclusive argument; if He willed, He would have guided you all,' and other than that, and it is not possible due to what has been explained.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the polytheists' argument based on the Quranic verse 'Those who associated partners will say: If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners' (Quran 6:148). The author (Maturidi) provides three distinct responses. First, the polytheists claimed their actions were commanded by God, but this is analogous to others who justified their sins by claiming divine command, as in Quran 7:28 and 3:78— thus they misinterpret divine will as a command. Misconception: If God does not punish immediately, He is pleased with the action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If God were to judge you right now based on your attitude towards His commands, would you be found thinking like the polytheists or like the patient believers? What is one immediate step you can take to align your heart with the truth that respite is not approval?

Third Response: The Mockery of the Believers
Maturidi's third answer: the polytheists spoke in mockery, similar to past mockers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's third refutation: that the polytheists' statement was often made in mockery, not as a sincere theological claim, similar to scoffers at resurrection and hypocrites.

Core Idea
The polytheists' argument was sometimes mockery, not genuine theology.

Maturidi's third response addresses the possibility that the polytheists were not seriously arguing but rather mocking the believers. He compares their statement to those who mock the resurrection and to hypocrites who outwardly acknowledge prophethood while inwardly ridiculing. This response reveals the disingenuous intent behind the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how Maturidi classifies this as mockery (استهزاء).
2
Examine the analogies
Maturidi gives two examples: the one who mocks resurrection (19:66) and the hypocrites (63:1). How are they similar to the polytheists?
In both cases, the speakers utter something true but intend to mock.
3
Understand the concluding support
The verse's end ('Say: To Allah belongs the conclusive argument...') reinforces that God's will is not a valid excuse; mockery does not make the argument sound.

And that is like His saying: 'Until when the messengers despaired'— the verse. He said that in mockery of the believers, even though that is true; and likewise the saying of the hypocrites: 'We bear witness that you are the messenger of Allah,' but that, because it was mockery, they attacked with it. So the first is like that. And Allah knows best. And the end of the verse supports that: 'Say: To Allah belongs the conclusive argument; if He willed, He would have guided you all,' and other than that, and it is not possible due to what has been explained.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the polytheists' argument based on the Quranic verse 'Those who associated partners will say: If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners' (Quran 6:148). The author (Maturidi) provides three distinct responses. First, the polytheists claimed their actions were commanded by God, but this is analogous to others who justified their sins by claiming divine command, as in Quran 7:28 and 3:78— thus they misinterpret divine will as a command. Second, the polytheists misinterpreted God's granting them respite as approval, thinking that if God were displeased, He would punish immediately. This is similar to the people of the Sabbath who assumed God's forbearance meant acceptance. The Quranic verse 'Until when the messengers despaired' (12:110) shows that respite does not imply approval. Third, the polytheists said this in mockery of the believers, similar to the one who scoffs at resurrection (Quran 19:66) or the hypocrites who mockingly testify to prophethood (63:1). Even if the statement is true in itself, they intended it as ridicule. The verse's conclusion 'Say: To Allah belongs the conclusive argument; if He willed, He would have guided you all' (6:149) supports that God's will is not a justification for shirk, because His will is just and He has provided guidance. The passage asserts that the polytheists' argument does not stand after these explanations.

Key Terms
الاستهزاء
الحجة البالغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Even if the polytheists' statement is true, their argument is valid.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the same statement can be mockery, and intention matters. The verse's conclusion refutes their misuse of divine will.

Checkpoint
What is the third response to the polytheists' argument?
A) That they were ignorant
B) That they said it in mockery
C) That they were forced
D) That they misunderstood the Quran

Key Takeaway
Even if the polytheists' statement is literally true, their intent of mockery invalidates it as a sincere argument; God's conclusive argument refutes them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used a true statement about God's will to dismiss someone's feelings or to avoid taking responsibility yourself? How will you change that habit after understanding that even truth can be used as mockery?
?
When someone challenges your faith, do you ever dismiss their argument by assuming it is insincere mockery, instead of engaging with the substance? What would it take for you to respond with the 'conclusive argument' of Allah rather than suspicion?
?
The polytheists mocked the believers' belief in divine will. Is there an area of your life where you secretly mock or belittle another believer's practice or belief, even if you don't say it aloud? How can you purify your heart from such mockery starting today?

Necessity of Accepting Prophetic Reports
Maturidi proves the necessity of accepting prophetic reports based on rational necessity and miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why rational necessity compels acceptance of prophetic reports and how miracles authenticate them.

Core Idea
Prophetic reports are the most reliable because miracles make them undeniable.

In the previous section, Maturidi established that rational necessity requires accepting reports that meet certain conditions. Here, he extends that argument to prophetic reports, arguing that they are even more binding. Prophets are distinguished by clear miracles (ayat) that testify to their truthfulness, making their reports the most reassuring and their rejection an act of stubbornness.

Guided Path
1
Recall the principle of report acceptance
Remember that Maturidi argued earlier that reports are accepted by intellectual necessity when they meet certain conditions. This is the foundation for the current argument.
2
Apply the principle to prophets
Now notice how Maturidi applies this principle: prophetic reports come with clear miracles that confirm their truthfulness. This makes them even more reliable than ordinary reports.
Look for the phrase 'الآيات الموضحة' (clear miracles) in the Arabic text.
3
Understand the conclusion on denial
The passage concludes that denying prophetic reports is the height of stubbornness (ta'annut) and obstinacy (mukabara), because their truth is so evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إذ قد لزم قبول الأخبار بضرورة العقل لزم قبول أخبار الرسل؛ إذ لا من تخبرهم خبرهم بما معهم من الآيات الموضحة صدقهم؛ إذ لا يوجد خبر يطمئن إليه القلب مما بينا من المعارف التي يصير منكر ذلك متعنتا بضرورة العقل - أوضح صدقا من أخبار الرسل صلوات الله عليهم، فمن أنكر ذلك فهو أحق من يقضى عليه بالتعنت والمكابرة.
Then since the acceptance of reports is necessitated by intellectual necessity, the acceptance of the reports of the messengers is necessitated; for there is none whose report informs you of what they have of clear miracles indicating their truthfulness; for there is no report that reassures the heart, among the cognitions we have explained which make its denier stubborn by intellectual necessity, that is clearer in truthfulness than the reports of the messengers, peace be upon them. So whoever denies that deserves more to be judged as stubborn and obstinate.
Source pages p. 607

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of accepting prophetic reports. Maturidi argues that since rational necessity compels us to accept reports in general (as established in the previous lesson), then the reports of prophets are even more obligatory to accept. Why? Because prophets bring clear miracles (ayat) that demonstrate their truthfulness. These miracles make their reports the most reliable and reassuring. Therefore, denying prophetic reports is even more stubborn and obstinate than denying ordinary reports.

Key Terms
الرسل
الآيات الموضحة
ضرورة العقل
التعنت والمكابرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophetic reports are accepted solely on blind faith without rational basis.
Correction: Maturidi grounds acceptance of prophetic reports in intellectual necessity and miraculous evidence, making it a rational obligation.

Checkpoint
On what basis does Maturidi argue that prophetic reports must be accepted?
A) Prophets are infallible by nature.
B) Reports in general are accepted by necessity, and prophets' reports are supported by miracles.
C) Prophets are known through historical consensus.
D) Reason alone dictates that prophets are truthful.

Key Takeaway
Prophetic reports are the most reliable reports because they are supported by clear miracles and grounded in rational necessity, making their rejection an act of stubbornness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a prophetic hadith that contradicts your personal desires or worldview, do you find yourself rationalizing or hesitating? What does Maturidi's argument about the necessity of accepting prophetic reports expose about the state of your heart? Commit to one prophetic teaching you have been avoiding and resolve to act on it this week.
?
You rely on reports from other people every day—news, science, stories. Yet when it comes to Allah's messengers, do you give their reports the same or even greater trust? If not, what is holding you back? How can you train yourself to treat every authentic prophetic report as more certain than any other report? Write down a practical step.
?
The passage says that denying prophetic reports is 'stubbornness and obstinacy.' Is there any area of your faith where you have been stubborn—refusing to submit to a clear command from the Quran or Sunnah? What is that area, and what will you do today to replace stubbornness with surrender?

Refutation of Those Who Deny That Creation is God's Act
Maturidi refutes claims that creation is not an act of God or is from nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Maturidi argues that denying God's creative act (takwin) is more incoherent than attributing creation to natures.

Core Idea
Denying God's creative act leaves nothing to cause creation.

Imam Maturidi presents a sharp refutation of those who claim that creation occurs from God without any divine act of bringing into existence. He contrasts this with the position of materialists who attribute effects to natures, showing that even the materialist has a better claim to a cause. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the flow.
Notice the contrast between the two positions mentioned.
2
Identify the two positions being refuted
Identify the first position: creation comes from God without takwin. The second: creation is from natures. Observe how Maturidi compares them.
The first is a denial of divine attribute, the second is a naturalistic explanation.
3
Understand the comparison and conclusion
Maturidi argues that the naturalists at least posit a cause, while those denying takwin posit nothing. Thus the latter have no grounding.
This is the core of the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى ذلك مجيء الرسل بالأمر الذي لو اتبعوا وعملوا بما جاءوا به ما احتمل الخلاف ولا التفريق ولا الفساد لولا علمه أمكنة متفرقة إذ في ذلك كذلك. قول من قال: كان الله ولا خلق، ثم كان الخلق بلا تكوين هو غير الخلق، كقول من ذكر بلا غير المضاف إليه العالم، والله الموفق. على أن قول من نسب إلى الطبائع والأغذية أحق إثبات أمر كان به غيرها من قول من يجعل الخلق الله بعد أن لم يكن بلا شيء من الله سوى كون الخلق، فيكون للنسبة منهم تحقيق، وليس من هؤلاء تحقيق، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And upon that is the coming of the messengers with the command which, if they followed and acted upon what they brought, would not admit of disagreement, nor division, nor corruption, were it not for His knowledge of different places, for in that is likewise. The saying of one who said: 'There was God and no creation, then creation came to be without a bringing into existence (takwīn), and it is other than the creation'—like the saying of one who mentioned 'without other than the attribute attributed to the world'—and God is the granter of success. Indeed, the saying of one who attributes [creation] to natures and temperaments is more entitled to establish an entity by which other than them comes to be than the saying of one who makes creation [come from] God after it was not, with nothing from God other than the existence of creation; so they have some grounding in their attribution, but these have no grounding.
Source pages p. 644

Commentary
This passage refutes two positions: (1) those who say creation is not God's act but merely a happening without a divine act of bringing into existence (takwīn), and (2) those who attribute creation to natures or temperaments. Maturidi begins by noting that the messengers came with a unified command that would preclude division and corruption if followed, which shows the consistency of divine revelation. He then mentions an objection: 'There was God and no creation, then creation came to be without a divine act of bringing into existence, and this creation is other than the creation [as an entity]'—a view he finds incoherent. He compares it to another statement that separates creation from the attribute. Then he argues that even the materialist (aṣḥāb al-ṭabāʾiʿ) who attributes effects to natures has a better claim to establishing a real agent (an entity by which things come to be) than those who say creation happens from God without any divine act or attribute, because the materialist at least posits a cause, while the latter posits nothing. Thus, the position that denies God's creative act (takwīn) is baseless.

Key Terms
الرسل
الخلق
التكوين
الطبائع
الأغذية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Maturidi is refuting a specific sect; actually, he is refuting a general denial of God's causal role.
Correction: The passage targets any view that denies God's creative act (takwin), not just a named group.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say that those who attribute creation to natures have more grounding than those who deny God's creative act?
A) Because natures are more powerful
B) Because they posit a real cause, while the latter posit nothing
C) Because the latter believe in God
D) Because nature is observable

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that denying God's creative act is even more irrational than attributing creation to natures because it posits no cause at all.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself assuming that things simply 'happen' without thinking of God's direct action? This passage equates that with having no grounding at all. Will you train your mind to see God's akt (takwīn) in every event today?
?
When you see the physical world—your body, the weather, your food—do you attribute its effects to natural causes alone? Maturidi says even the materialist has more grounding than those who deny God's act. How can you shift from seeing nature as autonomous to seeing it as directly upheld by God's creative act?
?
If the messengers came with a command that, if followed, would prevent all division and corruption, why do you still find conflict in your own life? What part of that divine command are you not fully following, and what will you do this week to realign?

Refuting the Objection on Moses' Request
Maturidi refutes the objection about Moses' request and offers two interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the objection that Moses' request to see God was based on ignorance, and the two interpretations he offers for Moses' request.

Core Idea
Moses' request was a lesson for the community, not a sign of ignorance.

Maturidi addresses a key objection about the story of Moses asking to see Allah. Some might think this request implies ignorance or doubt. Instead, Maturidi shows that Moses either sought to elevate his rank or acted by divine command to teach the permissibility of vision. This passage also answers a related objection about the mountain.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the text is dense and contains an objection-answer structure.
2
Identify the two reasons for Moses' request
The author gives two possibilities for why Moses asked to see God: either to elevate his rank or by Allah's command to teach the community. Find these in the text.
The phrase 'ليجل قدره' indicates elevation of rank; the phrase 'أو أن يكون الله أمره' indicates divine command.
3
Understand the objection-answer structure
Pay attention to the objection that Moses saw the mountain, not a sentient being. The answer explains that the sign (crumbling mountain) was shown to teach a lesson, and the verse 'You will not see Me' refers to the sign.
This shows how kalam scholars address apparent conflicts in scripture.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ذكر في أمر موسى عليه السلام أن ذلك على علم الإحاطة بالآيات. وقد بينا فساد ذلك. وما ذلك بلغ العلم بالذى يسأل، وهو رسول يبعث إلى به نجاة الخلق، وذلك لا يكون بغير الممتحن؛ إذ هو تبليغ الرسالة والدعاء إلى العبادة، وهي محنة، بل سأل الرؤية ليجل قدره وليعرف عظيم محله عند الله أو أن يكون الله أمره به ليُعلم الحلق جواز ذلك، وبالله التوفيق. وليراه. ثم استدل بأنه لم ير من يعقل، إنما أرى الجبل، والجبل لا يعقل ليعلمه. فيقال له: ولو كانت آية فالجميل لا يراها ولا يعقل، واذا كان كذلك فالآية إذا صار اندكاك الجبل الا أن أراه الآية ليندك بها، وفى هذا آية، قد أرى موسى دو انيكاك الجبل، والله تعالى يقول: لن ترانى، وحملته على الآية، وقد رآها، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he mentioned concerning the matter of Moses, peace be upon him, that that (request) is based on comprehensive knowledge of the signs. And we have already explained the corruption of that. And that (request) does not reach the level of knowledge of the one who asks, and he is a messenger sent to rescue creation, and that cannot be without a test; because it is the delivery of the message and the call to worship, which is a trial. Rather, he asked for vision to elevate his rank and to know his great status with Allah, or that Allah commanded him to do so to teach creation its permissibility. And with Allah is success. And to see Him. Then he argued that he did not see one who understands; rather, he showed the mountain, and the mountain does not understand to teach him. So it is said to him: Even if it were a sign, the beautiful (?) does not see it and does not understand. And if it is so, then the sign is when the mountain crumbles, except that He showed him the sign to make it crumble with it, and in this is a sign. He showed Moses the crumbling of the mountain, while Allah Almighty said: 'You will not see Me,' and he interpreted it as the sign, and he had seen it. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 682

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer concerning the story of Moses. The author first dismisses the claim that Moses' request was based on comprehensive knowledge, as that would be out of place for a prophet. He then proposes two interpretations: either Moses asked to elevate his rank, or Allah commanded him to ask to teach the community that seeing God is possible. The text then presents an objection: Moses did not see a sentient being; he saw the mountain, which is not sentient. The answer is that even if it were a sign, the sign itself is the mountain crumbling, and Moses saw that sign. The verse 'You will not see Me' is interpreted as referring to the mountain, not literal vision of God.

Key Terms
موسى
رؤية
جبل
آية
لن تراني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Moses' request implies that God can be seen in this world, or that Moses was ignorant of God's nature.
Correction: The author shows the request was either to elevate Moses' rank or a divine command to teach the community; it does not imply literal vision in this world.

Checkpoint
What two reasons does the author give for Moses asking to see God?
A) He was ignorant and wanted to learn
B) To elevate his rank or by divine command
C) He was forced by his people
D) He wanted to test God

Key Takeaway
Moses' request was not out of ignorance but either to elevate his rank or to teach the community about the possibility of seeing God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make du'a for something specific, do you stop to consider that Allah might have a higher purpose in granting or delaying it—just as Moses' request had a purpose beyond its apparent meaning? How can you cultivate trust in Allah's wisdom even when your requests are not answered as you hoped?
?
Think of a time you asked Allah for something that, in hindsight, was not in your best interest. What did that experience teach you about your own ignorance and Allah's perfect knowledge? How has that shaped your current approach to du'a?
?
Moses asked to see Allah as a means to teach his community, not out of mere curiosity. Is there an area of your life where you can ask Allah for something not just for your own benefit, but to teach or inspire others? What would that look like in practical terms?

The Meaning of Moses' Repentance
Maturidi presents and critiques interpretations of Moses' repentance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's analysis of Moses' repentance and the three interpretations he considers.

Core Idea
Repentance in prophets can be a renewal of faith, not only for sin.

In this passage, Maturidi examines the meaning of Moses' repentance after the incident of asking to see God. He presents three possible explanations, critically evaluating each. This lesson guides you through his reasoning and the theological implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Moses' repentance
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the full context of the discussion.
The passage contains dense theological reasoning.
2
Identify the two initial reasons
The passage states two possible reasons for Moses' repentance: a minor sin or a habitual renewal during terror. Find and note them.
3
Examine Abu Mansur's critique
Abu Mansur argues against the minor sin interpretation. Analyze his reasoning about consciousness and duty.
He considers it more fitting for a prophet to avoid sin than to learn through evidence.
4
Understand the third interpretation
Maturidi offers a probable interpretation: Moses renewed his faith after hearing 'You will not see Me.' Compare this to believers renewing faith with each revelation.
This interpretation preserves prophetic infallibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم سأل نفسه عن معنى توبته، ولا يسأل عنه، فزعم أنه لوجهين: أحدهما علم بما أراه من الأدلة أن ذلك صغيرة تاب عنها، والثاني على العادة في اخلاق من تجديدها عند الأهوال بلا حدوث ذنب. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: ولو كان صغيرة لكان أولى من تركه إلى أن يعلم بالأدلة، وهي في الأعلام في غير حال الإغماء أحق منها في حال الإغماء. لا عند سكونه وإبداله بالأمن والإفاقة ذلك تمناء معاينته الهول. والثاني: يصح وذلك وقمت معاينته عناه تهتز، فولی مديرا أحق، والله الموفق. لكنه يحتمل أن يكون إذ قال له لن ترانى، وكان عنده جواز الرؤية في الشاهد واحتمال وسعه ذلك بما وعد الله له في الآخرة - رجع عما كان عنده وآمن بالذي قال: لن ترانی، وإن كان فى أصل إيمانه داخلا، على نحو إحداث المؤمنين الإيمان بكل آية ينزل وبكل فريضة تتجدد، وإن كانوا في الجملة مؤمنين بالكل، والله الموفق. وقد بيتنا ما قال في قوله: وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة. قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله: والأصل في الكلام أنه إذا كان على أمر معهود أو يقرن به المقصود إليه صُرف عن حقيقته وإلا لا، نحو قوله:
Then he asked himself about the meaning of his repentance, and he is not asked about it. He claimed it is for two reasons: one is knowledge of what He showed him from the proofs that it was a minor sin from which he repented, and the second is according to the habit of renewing repentance at times of terror without the occurrence of a sin. Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: If it were a minor sin, it would be more fitting for him to avoid it than to learn through proofs, while proofs are more appropriate in states of consciousness than in states of unconsciousness. Not when it is calm and replaced with security and awakening? That is the witnessing of the terror? And the second is valid? And that is when he witnessed it trembling, so it is more appropriate to turn away? And Allah is the Grantor of success. But it is probable that when He said to him 'You will not see Me,' and he had the possibility of vision in the witnessed world and the possibility of its scope with what Allah promised him in the Hereafter, he retracted from what he had and believed in what He said: 'You will not see Me,' even though it was included in the core of his faith, similar to the renewal of faith by the believers with every revealed verse and every new obligation, even though they are collectively believers in all. And Allah is the Grantor of success. And we have already explained what He said in His saying: 'Faces on that Day will be radiant, looking at their Lord.' The jurist Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The principle in speech is that if it is based on a known matter or is accompanied by the intended meaning, it is taken away from its literal meaning, otherwise not, such as His saying:
Source pages p. 683

Commentary
This passage is a self-directed question about the meaning of Moses' repentance. The author considers two explanations: either it was a minor sin or a habitual renewal of repentance during terrifying events. He then critiques the first explanation, saying if it were a minor sin, Moses would have avoided it, and the proofs would be more appropriate when conscious. He then suggests a third possibility: that when Moses heard 'You will not see Me,' he accepted it as true, similar to how believers renew their faith with each new revelation. The passage ends with a general principle: when the context indicates a known matter or intention, speech should be interpreted figuratively; otherwise, it is literal. This principle is used to interpret the verse about looking at God in the afterlife.

Key Terms
توبة
صغيرة
لن تراني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Repentance always implies a sin was committed.
Correction: This passage shows that repentance can also be a habitual renewal without sin, as in the case of prophets during awe-inspiring events.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one possible reason for Moses' repentance?
A) He committed a major sin
B) It was a renewal of faith
C) He forgot God's command
D) It was a punishment for asking to see God

Key Takeaway
Moses' repentance can be understood as a renewal of faith rather than a confession of sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When has God's clear word contradicted a deeply held expectation of yours? Did you, like Moses, surrender and renew your belief, or did you persist?
?
Do you approach each new verse or command from Allah as if it were the first time, renewing your faith, or do you treat your faith as a completed checklist?
?
What does the fact that even a prophet like Moses had to repent (without sin) teach you about the humility required before God's absolute authority?

A Principle for Interpreting Figurative Language
Maturidi states a principle for interpreting figurative language.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's principle that speech is interpreted figuratively when context indicates a known matter or intention.

Core Idea
Context determines whether to interpret speech literally or figuratively.

Maturidi concludes his discussion with a general hermeneutic principle: when speech is based on a known matter or accompanied by an intended meaning, it should be taken figuratively. This principle is crucial for understanding verses about divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the principle passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation to grasp the principle.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the condition.
2
Identify the condition for figurative interpretation
Find the two situations that trigger figurative interpretation: 'based on a known matter' or 'accompanied by the intended meaning.'
3
Apply the principle to an example
Consider how this principle might apply to the verse about seeing God on the Day of Judgment.
Maturidi refers to a previous explanation of the verse 'Faces on that Day will be radiant, looking at their Lord.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم سأل نفسه عن معنى توبته، ولا يسأل عنه، فزعم أنه لوجهين: أحدهما علم بما أراه من الأدلة أن ذلك صغيرة تاب عنها، والثاني على العادة في اخلاق من تجديدها عند الأهوال بلا حدوث ذنب. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: ولو كان صغيرة لكان أولى من تركه إلى أن يعلم بالأدلة، وهي في الأعلام في غير حال الإغماء أحق منها في حال الإغماء. لا عند سكونه وإبداله بالأمن والإفاقة ذلك تمناء معاينته الهول. والثاني: يصح وذلك وقمت معاينته عناه تهتز، فولی مديرا أحق، والله الموفق. لكنه يحتمل أن يكون إذ قال له لن ترانى، وكان عنده جواز الرؤية في الشاهد واحتمال وسعه ذلك بما وعد الله له في الآخرة - رجع عما كان عنده وآمن بالذي قال: لن ترانی، وإن كان فى أصل إيمانه داخلا، على نحو إحداث المؤمنين الإيمان بكل آية ينزل وبكل فريضة تتجدد، وإن كانوا في الجملة مؤمنين بالكل، والله الموفق. وقد بيتنا ما قال في قوله: وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة. قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله: والأصل في الكلام أنه إذا كان على أمر معهود أو يقرن به المقصود إليه صُرف عن حقيقته وإلا لا، نحو قوله:
Then he asked himself about the meaning of his repentance, and he is not asked about it. He claimed it is for two reasons: one is knowledge of what He showed him from the proofs that it was a minor sin from which he repented, and the second is according to the habit of renewing repentance at times of terror without the occurrence of a sin. Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: If it were a minor sin, it would be more fitting for him to avoid it than to learn through proofs, while proofs are more appropriate in states of consciousness than in states of unconsciousness. Not when it is calm and replaced with security and awakening? That is the witnessing of the terror? And the second is valid? And that is when he witnessed it trembling, so it is more appropriate to turn away? And Allah is the Grantor of success. But it is probable that when He said to him 'You will not see Me,' and he had the possibility of vision in the witnessed world and the possibility of its scope with what Allah promised him in the Hereafter, he retracted from what he had and believed in what He said: 'You will not see Me,' even though it was included in the core of his faith, similar to the renewal of faith by the believers with every revealed verse and every new obligation, even though they are collectively believers in all. And Allah is the Grantor of success. And we have already explained what He said in His saying: 'Faces on that Day will be radiant, looking at their Lord.' The jurist Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The principle in speech is that if it is based on a known matter or is accompanied by the intended meaning, it is taken away from its literal meaning, otherwise not, such as His saying:
Source pages p. 683

Commentary
This passage is a self-directed question about the meaning of Moses' repentance. The author considers two explanations: either it was a minor sin or a habitual renewal of repentance during terrifying events. He then critiques the first explanation, saying if it were a minor sin, Moses would have avoided it, and the proofs would be more appropriate when conscious. He then suggests a third possibility: that when Moses heard 'You will not see Me,' he accepted it as true, similar to how believers renew their faith with each new revelation. The passage ends with a general principle: when the context indicates a known matter or intention, speech should be interpreted figuratively; otherwise, it is literal. This principle is used to interpret the verse about looking at God in the afterlife.

Key Terms
أصل في الكلام
مجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All religious texts must be interpreted literally.
Correction: Maturidi's principle shows that context can require figurative interpretation, especially for divine attributes.

Checkpoint

A) Always
B) When it is based on a known matter or accompanied by the intended meaning
C) Only in poetic language
D) Only when the literal meaning is impossible

Key Takeaway
Speech is interpreted figuratively when context indicates a known matter or intended meaning.
