The Argument of Messengers and the Proof Against Anthropomorphism
Maturidi argues that divine names brought by messengers prove tawhid, not tashbih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses the fact that messengers brought divine names to prove that these names do not imply any likeness to creation.

Core Idea
Messengers of tawhid cannot be the source of tashbih.

This passage presents a key argument from Imam Maturidi. He observes that all messengers came with divine names like 'Allah' and 'al-Rahman'. If those names implied any similarity to created beings, the very ones calling people to the oneness of God would be undermining that oneness. Maturidi shows that the names are accompanied by a negation that prevents any misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the central argument.
Read the Arabic and translation, noting that Maturidi begins with 'Then the proof for what we said is...' Identify what he is proving.
The passage builds on a previous point about names.
2
Analyze the conditional reasoning.
Maturidi uses a conditional: if the names implied tashbih, then messengers would break tawhid. But that is impossible. Understand this logic.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Examine the explanation: why the names are permissible.
Maturidi explains that the names allow the one named (God) to be beyond the known ones. Also, the verse 'There is nothing like Him' negates any 'thingness'.
The names are used with negation.
4
Reflect on the conclusion.
Maturidi concludes that the names do not establish number or likeness. They are tools for affirming God's transcendence.
This is a key principle of Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الدليل على ما قلنا مجىء الرسل والكتب السماوية بها ، ولو كان في التسمية بما جاءت به الرسل تشبيه لكانوا سبب نقض التوحيد ، وهم الواحد وإلى معرفة وحدانية البارى ، لم يجز أن يكون ذلك مما يحقق العدد ويثبت الموافقة للخلق ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . جميعا دعوا إلى عبادة ولكن لما احتملت تلك الأسماء خروج المسمى بها عن المعروفين من المسمين بها جاز مجيهم بها مع قوله : لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ لينفى به شيئية الأشياء من الإدراكات البسيطة وهى الأعراض والصفات والأعيان المركبة وهى الأجسام ، وبالله المعونة .
Then the proof for what we said is the coming of the messengers and the heavenly scriptures with them (the names). And if there were in the naming with what the messengers brought any tashbih (anthropomorphism), they would be the cause of breaking tawhid, while they are the ones who call to the knowledge of the Oneness of the Creator. It is not permissible that this (naming) should be something that establishes number and confirms likeness to creation. And there is no power except with Allah. All of them called to worship. But because those names allow the one named by them to be beyond the known ones who are named by them, it was permissible for them to come with them, along with His saying: 'There is nothing like Him' (42:11) to negate by it the 'thingness' of things from the simple perceptions—which are accidents and attributes—and the composite entities—which are bodies. And with Allah is assistance.
Source pages p. 693

Commentary
This passage is a proof (dalīl) for the previous claim that divine names are used with a negation to avoid tashbih. Maturidi argues: The fact that all messengers and scriptures came with divine names (like 'Allah', 'al-Rahman', etc.) proves that these names do not imply tashbih (likening God to creation). For if they did, then the messengers would be the cause of breaking tawhid (oneness), which is impossible because they are precisely the ones who call to the knowledge of God's oneness. Moreover, those names cannot establish multiplicity in God or affirm similarity to created beings. The reason these names are permissible is that they allow the one named (God) to be understood as beyond any known being that bears that name. God coupled them with His statement 'There is nothing like Him' to negate any 'thingness' (shay'iyya) of created things—both simple entities like accidents and attributes, and composite entities like bodies—from applying to Him. Thus the names are used but with the safeguard of negation.

Key Terms
تشبيه (tashbīh)
نقض التوحيد (naqḍ al-tawḥīd)
شيئية (shay'iyya)
أعراض (aʿrāḍ)
أجسام (ajsām)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Using names common to creation implies that God shares similarities with created beings.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the names are used in a way that emphasizes God's transcendence, and they are accompanied by the negation of likeness.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi argue that the coming of messengers with divine names proves the names do not imply tashbih?
A) Because messengers were polytheists
B) Because if they did, messengers would be breaking tawhid, but they call to it
C) Because the names are only metaphorical
D) Because the names were invented by messengers

Key Takeaway
The coming of messengers with divine names proves that these names do not entail tashbih because the names are used with a negation that safeguards God's transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah using names like 'The All-Hearing' or 'The All-Seeing', do you ever unconsciously imagine Him as a being with ears and eyes like yours? What does this lesson teach you about the safeguard that prevents such imagining? How will you actively remind yourself of the negation 'There is nothing like Him' when you use these names?
?
The messengers called to tawhid and brought names that could potentially lead to tashbih, but Allah safeguarded them with the negation. In your own life, do you ever use words or concepts about Allah that, without the proper safeguard, could lead you into anthropomorphism? What practical steps can you take to ensure your understanding remains pure?
?
Maturidi argues that messengers cannot be the cause of breaking tawhid. Reflect on your own sources of knowledge about God. Are you relying on authentic messengers (the prophets) or on human speculation? When you think about Allah's attributes, do you let the Quran and Sunnah guide you, or do you impose your own mental images? This lesson calls you to trust the messengers' teaching that Allah is beyond comparison. How will you commit to that trust?

The Need for Prophethood and Its Deniers
The jurist presents the issue of prophethood and the deniers' objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why prophethood is necessary and how some people have denied it based on ignorance or claims of intellectual sufficiency.

Core Idea
Prophethood is necessary; three groups deny it: those ignorant of God, those ignoring His commands, and those claiming intellect suffices.

This lesson introduces the debate about prophethood as presented by the jurist (al-Faqih). It explains why prophethood is affirmed by the righteous and denied by others, including those who think reason alone is enough. You will encounter an important objection: that miracles might be confused with magic, which sets the stage for Maturidi's response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the first part of the source Arabic and its literal translation. Note how the jurist sets up the issue and lists the groups denying prophethood.
The passage is dense; reading it twice may help.
2
Identify the three denying groups
Find in the text the three categories: those ignorant of the Maker, those who affirm Him but ignore His commands, and those who claim intellect is sufficient. Write them down.
3
Understand the objection about miracles
Notice that the jurist raises a challenge: the miracles of claimants could be countered by soothsayers, magicians, and jugglers. Moreover, it is possible that observers did not test everyone's abilities. This is a serious objection that Maturidi will later answer.
This sets the stage for the proof in the next lesson.
4
Reflect on the role of intellect
Consider why some people claimed intellect is sufficient. How does this passage show that even those who affirmed God might still deny the need for prophets?
This connects to the broader theme of the necessity of revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة إثبات الرسالة وبيان الحاجة إليها قال الفقيه رحمه الله : تكلم الناس في الرسالة فأثبتها أئمة الهدى وقادة الخير وحكماء البشر ، وأنكرها من جهل صانعه ، ومن أقرَّ ممن جهل أمره ونهيه ، ومن أقر بذلك ممن زعم أن فى العقل الغنى عن الرسالة أن في العقل الغنى عن الرسالة ، مع ما أمكن مقابلة آيات من ادعى الرسالة بصنيع الكهنة والسحرة والمشعبذة وبعد ، فإنه يحتمل ظهور عجز من حضرهم بما لم يكن في ذلك النوع تكلف واجتهاد ، ولم يكونوا امتحنوا قوى الجميع. قال الشيخ : فنناظر من أنكر الصانع فى اثباته ؛ إذ التنازع في إرساله لا يتمكن إلا بعد لزوم القول بهستيته وثباته ، مع ما أمكن الأمران جميعا بآيات الرسل ، إذ هم قوم نشأوا بين قوم عرفوا أحوالهم ، وقد كانوا أدركوا منتهى وسعهم ، فلما جاءوا بالآيات التي قهرت عقولهم - مع علمهم بأن وسعهم لا يحتمل إنشاء مثلها - لزم العلم بصدقه فيما أخبر من مرسله ، وأن تلك الآيات مما أنشأها من يكون رسالته من عليم حكيم قادر على إنشاء الأدلة على اثباته ؛ ليعلموه بها وإن لم يشهدوه ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Issue: Establishing Prophethood and Clarifying the Need for It. The jurist (al-Faqih), may God have mercy on him, said: People have spoken about prophethood; the imams of guidance, leaders of good, and sages of mankind affirmed it, and those who were ignorant of its Maker denied it, as well as those who affirmed [the Maker] but were ignorant of His command and prohibition, and those who affirmed that yet claimed that the intellect has sufficiency from prophethood—that the intellect has sufficiency from prophethood—despite the fact that it was possible to counter the miracles (āyāt) of those who claimed prophethood with the works of soothsayers, magicians, and tricksters. Moreover, it is possible that the incapacity of those who presented them appeared, because there was no toil and effort in that type, and they had not tested the powers of everyone. The Shaykh said: We will debate the one who denies the Maker concerning affirming Him; for disagreement about His sending [messengers] cannot be achieved except after establishing the affirmation of His existence and permanence, along with the possibility of both matters through the miracles of the messengers, for they are a people who grew up among people who knew their circumstances, and they had perceived the utmost of their capacity. When they brought miracles that overwhelmed their intellects—while knowing that their capacity could not produce the like—knowledge necessarily followed that he is truthful in what he reported from his sender, and that those miracles were originated by one whose mission is from a Knowing, Wise, Powerful being capable of producing proofs for His existence, so that they may know Him thereby even if they do not witness Him, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 775

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the necessity of prophethood (al-risāla) and its proof. It begins by stating the issue: establishing prophethood and clarifying the need for it. The jurist (al-Faqih) — likely a reference to Maturidi's teacher or a figure — presents the views: the righteous imams and sages affirmed prophethood, while three groups denied it: those ignorant of God (the Maker), those who affirmed God but ignored His commands and prohibitions, and those who affirmed God and His commands but claimed that intellect is sufficient for guidance without prophethood. The jurist then raises a potential objection: the miracles of claimants of prophethood could be countered by the works of soothsayers, magicians, and tricksters; moreover, it is possible that the incapacity of those who presented miracles became evident only because the observers did not exert themselves to produce similar things and had not tested everyone's abilities. The Shaykh (Maturidi) responds: first, the debate must focus on affirming God's existence; disagreement about sending messengers cannot be resolved without first establishing God's existence and attributes. Then he presents the core proof: the messengers grew up among their people who knew their entire background and capabilities. When the messengers brought miracles that overwhelmed the intellects of their people—while those people knew from experience that their own capacity could not produce anything similar—it becomes necessary knowledge that the messenger is truthful in his report from his Sender. Furthermore, those miracles must have been originated by a Being who is Knowing, Wise, and Powerful, who is capable of producing proofs for His own existence. Thus, through the miracles, people come to know God even without direct vision. The argument emphasizes that miracles are the decisive proof because they clearly exceed human capability and are witnessed by people who knew the messenger's limitations.

Key Terms
الرسالة
العقل
الكهنة
السحرة
المشعبذة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that Maturidi rejects the role of intellect entirely in knowing God.
Correction: Maturidi does not reject intellect; he only rejects the claim that intellect is completely sufficient without divine guidance. He uses rational arguments to affirm prophethood.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which group denies prophethood while still affirming the existence of God?
A) Those ignorant of the Maker.
B) Those who affirm the Maker but are ignorant of His command and prohibition.
C) Those who claim that intellect is sufficient from prophethood.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
Prophethood is a reality; deniers fall into three categories, and even miracles can be doubted if not properly distinguished from magic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You read that some deny prophethood because they claim intellect suffices. In your daily life, do you ever rely solely on your own reasoning and ignore divine commands when they conflict with your logic? What would it take for you to submit your intellect to revelation?
?
The passage mentions those who affirm God but ignore His command and prohibition. Reflect on an area of your life where you know God's command but choose to ignore it. What is holding you back from obedience?
?
The passage raises the possibility of confusing miracles with magic. Have you ever doubted the uniqueness of the Prophet’s miracles? How does this lesson challenge you to reaffirm your belief in his prophethood?

The Proof of Miracles: Maturidi's Response
Maturidi responds with a logical proof from miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's decisive argument for prophethood based on miracles that exceed human capacity and come from those known among their people.

Core Idea
Miracles of messengers, witnessed by their own people, prove prophethood because they surpass all human ability.

In this lesson, Maturidi directly responds to the objection raised earlier. He first insists that the debate must be grounded in affirming God's existence. Then he presents the core proof: the messengers grew up among their people, who knew their full capacity, yet the messengers brought wonders that overwhelmed them. This establishes their truthfulness and divine origin.

Guided Path
1
Read Maturidi's response and translation
Read the second part of the source Arabic and its literal translation. Note how Maturidi begins by refocusing the debate on God's existence.
This is the heart of the argument.
2
Understand the refocus on God's existence
Maturidi states that disagreement about sending messengers can only be resolved after affirming God's existence and attributes. Why is this important?
This is a foundational step.
3
Analyze the proof from known background
The messengers grew up among their people, who knew their entire life and capabilities. When they brought miracles that overwhelmed their intellects, and people knew they could not produce such things, it leads to necessary knowledge of truthfulness.
The key is that the witnesses knew the messenger's limitations.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The miracles must have been originated by a Knowing, Wise, Powerful being (God) to serve as proofs for His existence. Thus, through miracles, people come to know God even without seeing Him directly.
This completes the argument.
5
Reflect on how this refutes the objection
Explain why the miracle cannot be mistaken for magic or trickery: because the witnesses knew the messenger's capacities from birth, and the miracles exceeded all human potential.
This is the decisive refutation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness undeniable signs of truth in your life—whether through a person, an event, or a verse—do you immediately submit to the One who sent them, or do you rationalize them away to avoid the accountability they demand? What would it cost you to accept that these signs are from God, not coincidence?
?
The people knew the prophet's full life story and still could not deny the miracle. Think of someone you know well—perhaps a family member or close friend—whose character you fully understand. If they were to bring something that clearly transcends human ability, would your intimate knowledge of them make it easier or harder to accept it as divine? What does that say about your own openness to truth?
?
Maturidi says miracles lead to knowledge of God even without seeing Him. Where in your life are you demanding to 'see' before you believe, even though miracles of creation and revelation are already overwhelming your intellect? What one step can you take today to move from intellectual acknowledgment to heartfelt certainty?

The Proof of Prophethood Through Miracles
Maturidi presents the proof of prophethood via the context of the messenger's life and the inimitability of his signs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi proves the authenticity of a prophet's miracles by referencing the prophet's known history and the people's recognition of their own limitations.

Core Idea
A prophet's miracles are proven by his context and his people's inability to match them.

This passage from Imam Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid addresses one of the most important proofs of prophethood: that the messenger's life history and the people's knowledge of their own capacity confirm that his miracles are truly from God. Maturidi first describes the different views on prophethood, then tackles a common objection about sorcery, and finally presents his argument that the context of the messenger's upbringing and the people's inability to produce similar signs make it undeniable that he is sent by Allah. Let's explore this foundational argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument. The passage is dense but contains a clear logical flow.
2
Identify the groups mentioned
Notice that Maturidi lists several groups regarding prophethood: those who affirm it, those who deny the Maker, those who acknowledge the Maker but reject commands, and those who claim intellect suffices. Think about why each group is mentioned.
3
Understand the objection and response
The passage presents a potential objection that miracles could be mere sorcery. Find how Maturidi counters this by pointing to the context of the messenger's known history and the people's own awareness of their limitations.
This is the key insight.
4
Trace the logical conclusion
The passage concludes that when a person known for honesty brings signs beyond human capacity, it necessarily confirms his truthfulness and his mission from God. Reflect on how this argument differs from a simple claim of miracle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة إثبات الرسالة وبيان الحاجة إليها قال الفقيه رحمه الله تكلم الناس في الرسالة فأثبتها أئمة الهدى وقادة الخير وحكماء البشر وأنكرها من جهل صانعه ومن أقر ممن جهل أمره ونهيه ومن أقر بذلك ممن زعم أن في العقل الغنى عن الرسالة مع ما أمكن مقابلة آيات من ادعى الرسالة بصنيع الكهنة والسحرة والمشعبذة وبعد فإنه يحتمل ظهور عجز من حضرهم بما لم يكن في ذلك النوع تكلف واجتهاد ولم يكونوا امتحنوا قوى الجميع قال الشيخ فنناظر من أنكر الصانع في إثباته إذ التنازع في إرساله لا يتمكن إلا بعد لزوم القول بهستيته وثباته مع ما أمكن الأمران جميعا بآيات الرسل إذ هم قوم نشأوا بين قوم عرفوا أحوالهم وقد كانوا أدركوا منتهى وسعهم فلما جاءوا بالآيات التي قهرت عقولهم مع علمهم بأن وسعهم لا يحتمل إنشاء مثلها لزم العلم بصدقه فيما أخبر من مرسله وأن تلك الآيات مما أنشأها من يكون رسالته من عليم حكيم قادر على إنشاء الأدلة على إثباته ليعلموه بها وإن لم يشهدوه ولا قوة إلا بالله
Issue: Establishing prophethood and explaining the need for it. The faqih (may God have mercy on him) said: People spoke about prophethood. The imams of guidance, leaders of good, and wise men of humanity affirmed it. Those ignorant of its Maker denied it, and some who acknowledged the Maker denied His command and prohibition, and some acknowledged that but claimed that the intellect is sufficient without prophethood, along with the possibility of countering the signs of one who claims prophethood with the deeds of soothsayers, sorcerers, and jugglers. Furthermore, it is possible that the incapacity of those who were present [at the miracles] appeared due to something they had not exerted effort and striving in that type, and they had not tested the powers of all. The Shaykh said: So we will argue with the one who denies the Maker regarding establishing Him, because disputing about His sending [messengers] is only possible after affirming His existence and permanence. Along with the possibility that both matters [the existence of the Maker and the truth of the messenger] are possible through the signs of the messengers, for they are people who grew up among people who knew their conditions, and they had reached the utmost of their capacity. When they brought signs that overwhelmed their intellects, with the knowledge that their capacity could not produce the like of them, knowledge of his truthfulness necessarily follows in what he informed of his sender, and that those signs are created by one whose message comes from an All-Knowing, Wise, Powerful One capable of creating proofs for His existence, so that they may know Him through them even if they do not see Him. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 775

Commentary
This passage begins by defining the issue: establishing prophethood and explaining the need for it. The faqih (a student or earlier authority) summarizes the positions: the righteous leaders affirm prophethood, while some deny it out of ignorance of the Creator, some acknowledge the Creator but deny His commands, and some claim that reason is sufficient without revelation. The text then notes a potential objection: the miracles of prophets might be countered by the tricks of sorcerers and jugglers. But the author (al-Shaykh, i.e., Imam Maturidi) responds by arguing that the context of the messengers—they were known among their people from childhood, and their people knew their limits—makes it impossible that their signs could be fabricated. The people knew that their own capacity could not produce such signs, so they necessarily recognize the truthfulness of the messenger and that the signs come from a wise, powerful God. Thus, the argument uses the principle that when a person known for honesty brings something beyond human power, it confirms his divine mission.

الآيات
المعجزة
الصانع
الهوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles of prophets are indistinguishable from sorcery.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the known background of the messenger and the people's inability to produce similar signs distinguish prophetic miracles from sorcery.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do the people know that the messenger's signs are from God?
A) Because the messenger claims to be from God.
B) Because the messenger grew up among them and they know his character and their own limitations.
C) Because the signs are beautiful.
D) Because the messenger is wealthy and powerful.

Key Takeaway
The reliability of a prophet's miracle is established by his known character and the people's awareness of their own inability to replicate the sign.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This lesson shows that knowledge of the prophet's truth comes from recognizing your own limits. Where in your life are you pretending that Allah's commands are not possible for you, when in reality you are simply unwilling to push yourself? What would change if you admitted that the barrier is your own lack of effort, not Allah's demand being too high?
?
The passage says the people knew the prophet's background and still denied. Who are you denying the message of? Perhaps a family member or a teacher who calls you to Allah? Are you dismissing their call because you think you know them too well? What if that familiarity is a test of your humility?

Why We Need Prophets: Reason’s Limits in Knowing Benefit and Harm
Maturidi proves the necessity of prophethood through reason’s inability to discern true benefit and harm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi’s rational argument that human reason alone cannot discern all beneficial and harmful things, thus necessitating divine guidance through prophets.

Core Idea
Human reason is blind to the true consequences of actions — we need prophets to see.

Maturidi presents a compelling argument for the necessity of prophethood grounded in human experience. He observes that even the most rational people struggle to consistently choose what truly benefits them, because they are ignorant of the ultimate outcomes of their actions. This leads him to a logical conclusion: if we cannot reliably know what is harmful or beneficial in our own lives, we must rely on someone who does — a prophet informed by God. In this passage, Maturidi builds this case step by step, using concrete examples from nature and daily life.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the flow of Maturidi's argument. Notice how he moves from a general observation to a specific example.
The Arabic cuts off at the end, so the argument is incomplete.
2
Identify the two parts of the argument
First, Maturidi describes the human condition: people are ignorant and need a guide. Second, he gives a concrete proof: reason cannot distinguish nourishments from poisons, so a prophetic teacher is needed.
3
Reflect on the example of nourishments and poisons
Maturidi points out that God created both beneficial and harmful substances from the same earth, but reason alone cannot tell them apart. This shows the necessity of revelation.
This is a powerful analogy for the moral and spiritual realm.
4
Consider the significance of the incomplete ending
The text cuts off while discussing agriculture, hinting at another example. Reflect on how this supports the overall argument even though it's unfinished.
You can imagine how the argument would conclude: just as we need experts to know how to use plants, we need prophets to guide our lives.

And after, there is no rational person in the witnessed world who is satisfied with neglecting himself from attention, immersing in desires. Rather, everyone strives to rectify himself in a way that does not harm him and that ends with praise, despite the ignorance that destroys him by that with which he hopes for salvation, and harms him by that with which he hopes for benefit. So that requires him to have someone who knows the consequences of affairs, until he trains his soul according to his indications, without abandoning it to its desires. And there is no power except with God. Then we return to the debate of one who denied prophethood for the existence that he mentioned, after his affirmation of tawheed and his belief in command and prohibition, while what we mentioned of proofs of command and prohibition coupled with the need for prophethood is sufficient for whoever purifies his soul. Then we say: the affirmation of prophethood is necessary by necessity of the intellect in establishing the need for it in religion and world, then in establishing the favors from God if the intellect is self-sufficient, then the matter of the world which also upholds religion. For example, God created humans and made them subject to trial, and caused to grow from the earth with the water sent from heaven nourishments and medicines, then caused to grow from it ailments and lethal poisons, and granted in their intellects testing themselves to know the harmful from the beneficial, even though there may be destruction of the tested one. Commentary
This passage provides a rational proof for the necessity of prophethood based on human ignorance of consequences and the inability of reason to distinguish all beneficial and harmful things. Maturidi begins by observing that even rational people neglect self-discipline and fall into desires, and their efforts to act beneficially are undermined by ignorance. This shows they need someone who knows the true outcomes of actions to guide them. He then returns to a debate with a denier of prophethood who accepts tawheed and divine commands, stating that the earlier proofs are sufficient. He asserts that prophethood is a rational necessity for both religious and worldly life. As an example, he notes that God created humans for trial and provided both nourishments and lethal poisons from the earth, yet reason alone cannot distinguish them. Therefore, God must send someone to reveal the true essence of each thing so that humans can sustain their bodies and practice religion. He further points out that from the start, reason cannot understand agricultural processes and management, so a divinely guided teacher is needed.

Correction: Maturidi argues that while intellect can recognize some harms and benefits, it cannot know the true consequences of actions, especially in matters of trial. Prophets are necessary to reveal what reason alone cannot grasp.

Key Takeaway
Human reason alone is insufficient to navigate life’s trials; prophets provide the necessary guidance to distinguish true benefit from harm.

The author says that even in managing agriculture, reason is insufficient without divine instruction. What is one concrete step you will take today to align that area with what Allah has commanded?

The Rational Necessity of Prophethood
Maturidi states the rational necessity of prophethood for guidance in religion and world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's core argument that prophethood is rationally necessary for human guidance in religious and worldly matters.

Core Idea
Prophethood is rationally necessary because reason alone cannot fulfill all human needs.

Maturidi begins by asserting that no rational person neglects self-improvement; everyone needs a guide who knows the outcomes of actions. He then states the thesis: prophethood is obligatory by rational necessity. This lesson focuses on that foundational claim before the detailed evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening argument
Carefully read the Arabic passage and translation to grasp Maturidi's initial reasoning.
2
Identify the core thesis
Find the phrase 'يجب القول بالرسالة بضرورة العقل' and note its meaning.
This is the central claim.
3
Understand the context
Notice that the argument is presented as a debate with someone who accepts tawhid but denies prophethood.

وفيما ذكرنا لزوم القول بالرسل ليدلوهم على معالم العدل والصدق ومضار ضدهما، على الإشارة إلى كل شيء أشكلت مائيته؛ ليكون أمر الأحوال للحمد موافقا، والله الموفق. وبعد، فإنه لا عاقل فى الشاهد يرضى إهمال بأنفسهم ليعرفوا المؤذى من المغذى، لما لعل [فيه] عطب الممتحن، وليس في العقول سبيل يعرف ذلك، لزم القول بمن يطلعه الله على كل جوهر منها ليحيى بما يأكلون أبدانهم ويقيموا به دينهم. ثم فى الابتداء ليس في العقول [سبيل] يعرف الوجوه التي تنبت من الزراعة وما فيها من التدبير، ثم بعد التمام والعلم بجوهر لا بد ممن يعلم كيف يستعمله حتى يصلح للاغتذاء، على اختلاف ما جعل لصلاح ذلك. ثم جعل في الطعام أنواع الأذى مما يدفع إليه المنتفع إذا لم يحفظ حده؛ لأنه ممن يعلم حد ذلك، ثم دواه إن ضره بالقدر الذى به يدفع ضرره. ثم علوم الطب مع تفاوت الطبائع واستعمال السموم القاتلة؛ ليعرفوا قدر النافع مما يقوم معه البدن. ثم في أنواع الحرف التي بها قوام سترهم وكنتهم والوقاية لهم من الحر والبرد. ثم فيما خلقت لهم من الدواب الصعبة مما ليس يعلم المتأهل فيها أنها لأى منفعة خلقت، ولا أنها لمنافعه خلقت أو لا، ولا كيف يروضها؛ إذ طبع كل منها النفار عما شي له، حتى تنقاد وتخضع. ثم فى أنواع التجارات التي لا يقوم لهم دين ولا دنيا إلا بها. ثم ما فرق حوائجهم في البلدان، الذي ليس في طبعهم ولا في عقولهم ما يدلهم أو يبين لهم في كل حاجة أنها أين تطلب، ثم فى معرفة طرقها؛ إذ ليس في العقل ما يدل على مكانها ولا على طرقها. ثم فى تعرف الألسن التي بها قام النفس للمعاش وفهم المعاد، ثم تعرف الأسماء التى لولا هى ما فهمت حاجة ولا أمكن أحدا معرفة موضعها. ثم في وجوه أسباب التناسل، وفى معرفة تربية الصغار. ثم فى العلم بتدبير أغذية ما ليس يتطرق، ثم بظهور بعلم الخلق بعضهم من بعض – جميع ما ذكرت من الألسن والأسماء والحرف والطب والصناعات كلها وطرق البلدان ورياضة الدواب وكينية استعمالها وجميع ما ذكرت هو الدليل البين أن أصولها تعليم وإشارة لا استخراج العقول، والله الموفق. فهذا مع المعروف الموجود من فزع بعض إلى بعض عند النوائب وما يحزنهم من الأمور المهمة للاستعانة برأيهم والصدور عن مشورتهم بما عندهم فضل في العالم. ثم تعليم فنون الآداب، ثم قيام كل بما يستفيد أنواع العلم من درس الكتب والاستماع إلى الحكماء، فدل ذلك أنهم لم يروا بعقولهم كفاية عن الاستعانة وآداء حاجاتهم جميعا، لزم فى العقل الفزع إلى ناصح صدوق.
And in what we have mentioned, the necessity of affirming the messengers to guide them to the landmarks of justice and truth and the harms of their opposites, indicating everything whose essence is problematic; so that the state of affairs may be in accordance with praise, and God is the granter of success. Afterwards, no rational person in the witnessed world is satisfied with neglecting himself, indulging in desires; rather, everyone strives to rectify them in a way that does not harm him and that has praiseworthy consequences, despite the ignorance that destroys him by that through which he hopes for his salvation, and harms him by that through which he seeks benefit. So that necessitates for him someone who knows the outcomes of affairs, so that he may discipline his soul according to his instructions, without abandoning it to its desires. And there is no power except with God. Then we return to debating the one who denies the prophethood for existence which we mentioned, after his affirmation of tawhid and his belief in command and prohibition, along with what we mentioned of the proofs of command and prohibition coupled with the need for the prophethood—sufficient for one who corrects his soul. Then we say: It is obligatory to affirm prophethood by rational necessity on account of the requirement of need for it in religion and the world. Then [it is also obligatory] in establishing the graces from God, even if reason is independent of it, for the matter of this world is also through it the support of religion. For instance, He created human beings and made them people of trial, and brought forth from the earth by what He sent down from the sky water, nourishment and medicine for them, then brought forth from it ailments and deadly poisons, and granted in their intellects the test through themselves to know the harmful from the beneficial, because of the possible destruction of the one tested. And there is no way in intellects to know that, so it is necessary to affirm one whom God informs about every essence of them, so that they may live by what they eat for their bodies and establish their religion through it. Then at the beginning, there is no way in intellects to know the modes by which things grow from agriculture and the planning involved therein. Then after completion and knowledge of the essence, there must be someone who knows how to use it until it becomes suitable for nourishment, according to the differences made for its rectification. Then He placed in food types of harm that impel the beneficiary if he does not preserve its limit, because he is among those who know its limit, then its remedy if it harms him, to the extent by which its harm is repelled. Then the sciences of medicine, despite the diversity of temperaments and the use of deadly poisons, so that they may know the measure of benefit that sustains the body. Then the types of crafts by which their covering and shelter are established, and protection from heat and cold. Then regarding the intractable animals created for them, which one who reflects upon does not know for what benefit they were created, nor whether they were created for their benefits or not, nor how to tame them, since the nature of each is aversion to that which is for it, until they become obedient and submissive. Then the types of trades without which neither religion nor worldly life can be established for them. Then the distribution of their needs across lands, which is not in their nature nor in their intellects to guide them or clarify for them in every need where it is sought. Then the knowledge of its routes, since there is nothing in the intellect that indicates its location or its routes. Then the recognition of languages by which the soul established livelihood and understanding of the afterlife, then the recognition of names without which no need would be understood nor would anyone be able to know its location. Then the aspects of the causes of reproduction, and the knowledge of raising children. Then the knowledge of managing nourishment for what cannot be reached, then the manifestation, through the knowledge of some creatures from others—all that we mentioned of languages, names, crafts, medicine, industries, routes of lands, taming of animals, and the mode of their use, and all that we mentioned—is the clear evidence that their origins are teaching and indication, not extraction by intellects. And God is the granter of success. This, along with the known actual phenomenon of some people seeking refuge with others at calamities and what grieves them from important matters, to seek help from their opinion and to defer to their counsel, because of the extra knowledge they possess in the world. Then the teaching of the arts of etiquette, then the undertaking of each to acquire types of knowledge from studying books and listening to the wise. So that indicates that they did not consider their intellects sufficient for seeking help and fulfilling all their needs; thus it is rationally necessary to seek refuge with a truthful advisor.
Source pages p. 778

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of prophethood, building on the earlier recognition of tawhid and the need for divine commands. The argument proceeds in several stages. First, the author points out that no rational person neglects self-improvement; everyone seeks guidance on the consequences of actions, which requires someone who knows outcomes—a prophet. Then he returns to debating a hypothetical opponent who denies prophethood after accepting tawhid. The core argument is that prophethood is rationally necessary (بضرورة العقل) due to the universal need for it in both religious and worldly affairs. Even if reason were sufficient for some things, the worldly order itself sustains religion through divine instruction. To prove the insufficiency of reason, the author presents a long list of essential human activities where unaided intellect cannot determine the correct course: agriculture (what grows from the earth, how to use it), medicine (distinguishing beneficial from harmful, using poisons), crafts (making clothes and shelter), taming intractable animals, trade (finding goods across lands), languages and names (without which communication and understanding needs is impossible), reproduction and child-rearing, and management of diverse foods. All these domains require knowledge that is not innate to reason but must be taught. The conclusion is that the origins of these sciences and practices are not human invention but divine teaching and indication (تعليم وإشارة). Additionally, the observed human behavior of seeking counsel from the wise and studying books confirms that people recognize their own intellectual insufficiency. Therefore, it is rationally necessary to turn to a truthful advisor, i.e., a prophet.

Key Terms
رسالة
ضرورة العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Prophethood is rationally necessary because human reason cannot independently guide all religious and worldly affairs.

Evidence from Human Needs for Prophethood
Maturudi lists examples of human needs to demonstrate reason's insufficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses detailed examples from agriculture, medicine, crafts, and other domains to prove that reason alone is insufficient and requires prophetic guidance.

Core Idea
Reason's insufficiency in numerous life domains proves the need for divine teaching.

Maturidi now provides a sweeping survey of human activities—from farming to trade to language—showing that reason cannot independently master any of them. Each example reinforces that the origins of these sciences must be divine instruction, not human invention. This lesson examines the evidence and its conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of examples
Go through the Arabic and translation, noting each domain Maturidi mentions: agriculture, medicine, crafts, animals, trade, etc.
The list is long; consider grouping them.
2
Identify the repeated refrain
Notice the phrase 'ليس في العقول سبيل' and its variations; it emphasizes reason's limitations.
3
Understand the conclusion
Find where Maturidi says 'جميع ما ذكرت هو الدليل البين أن أصولها تعليم وإشارة' and the final shift to seeking a truthful advisor.
This is the argument's climax.
4
Reflect on the practical reasoning
Consider how the common practice of seeking counsel supports the argument.

وفيما ذكرنا لزوم القول بالرسل ليدلوهم على معالم العدل والصدق ومضار ضدهما، على الإشارة إلى كل شيء أشكلت مائيته؛ ليكون أمر الأحوال للحمد موافقا، والله الموفق. وبعد، فإنه لا عاقل فى الشاهد يرضى إهمال نفسه عن التعاهد، ينهمك في الشهوات، بل كل يجتهد على تسويتها على ما لا يضرها، وعلى ما يحمد عواقبها، على ما فيها من الجهل الذى يعطبه بما به يرجو نجاته، ويضره فما به يطمع نفعه، فذلك يحوجه إلى من يعلم عواقب الأمور، حتى يروض نفسه على إشاراته، دون أن يهملها لشهواتها، ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم نرجع إلى مناظرة من أنكر الرسالة للوجود التى ذكر [ها]، بعد إقراره بالتوحيد وايمانه بالأمر والنهي، مع ما فيما ذكرت من أدلة الأمر والنهي مقرونة بالحاجة إلى الرسالة كفاية لمن تصيح نفسه. ثم نقول: يجب القول بالرسالة بضرورة العقل فى إيجاب الحاجة إليها دينا ودنيا، ثم فى إثبات الأفضال من الله إن كان في العقل منه غنى، فأمر الدنيا فيها به أيضا قوام الدين، ونحو أن خلق البشر وجعلهم أهل المحنة، وأنبط طم من الأرض بما أنزل من ماء السماء أغذية لهم وأدوية، ثم أنبت منها الأدواء والسموم القاتلة، ومنح في عقولهم الامتحان بأنفسهم ليعرفوا المؤذى من المغذى، لما لعل [فيه] عطب الممتحن، وليس في العقول سبيل يعرف ذلك، لزم القول بمن يطلعه الله على كل جوهر منها ليحيى بما يأكلون أبدانهم ويقيموا به دينهم. ثم فى الابتداء ليس في العقول [سبيل] يعرف الوجوه التي تنبت من الزراعة وما فيها من التدبير، ثم بعد التمام والعلم بجوهر لا بد ممن يعلم كيف يستعمله حتى يصلح للاغتذاء، على اختلاف ما جعل لصلاح ذلك. ثم جعل في الطعام أنواع الأذى مما يدفع إليه المنتفع إذا لم يحفظ حده؛ لأنه ممن يعلم حد ذلك، ثم دواه إن ضره بالقدر الذى به يدفع ضرره. ثم علوم الطب مع تفاوت الطبائع واستعمال السموم القاتلة؛ ليعرفوا قدر النافع مما يقوم معه البدن. ثم في أنواع الحرف التي بها قوام سترهم وكنتهم والوقاية لهم من الحر والبرد. ثم فيما خلقت لهم من الدواب الصعبة مما ليس يعلم المتأهل فيها أنها لأى منفعة خلقت، ولا أنها لمنافعه خلقت أو لا، ولا كيف يروضها؛ إذ طبع كل منها النفار عما شي له، حتى تنقاد وتخضع. ثم فى أنواع التجارات التي لا يقوم لهم دين ولا دنيا إلا بها. ثم ما فرق حوائجهم في البلدان، الذي ليس في طبعهم ولا في عقولهم ما يدلهم أو يبين لهم في كل حاجة أنها أين تطلب، ثم فى معرفة طرقها؛ إذ ليس في العقل ما يدل على مكانها ولا على طرقها. ثم فى تعرف الألسن التي بها قام النفس للمعاش وفهم المعاد، ثم تعرف الأسماء التى لولا هى ما فهمت حاجة ولا أمكن أحدا معرفة موضعها. ثم في وجوه أسباب التناسل، وفى معرفة تربية الصغار. ثم فى العلم بتدبير أغذية ما ليس يتطرق، ثم بظهور بعلم الخلق بعضهم من بعض – جميع ما ذكرت من الألسن والأسماء والحرف والطب والصناعات كلها وطرق البلدان ورياضة الدواب وكينية استعمالها وجميع ما ذكرت هو الدليل البين أن أصولها تعليم وإشارة لا استخراج العقول، والله الموفق. فهذا مع المعروف الموجود من فزع بعض إلى بعض عند النوائب وما يحزنهم من الأمور المهمة للاستعانة برأيهم والصدور عن مشورتهم بما عندهم فضل في العالم. ثم تعليم فنون الآداب، ثم قيام كل بما يستفيد أنواع العلم من درس الكتب والاستماع إلى الحكماء، فدل ذلك أنهم لم يروا بعقولهم كفاية عن الاستعانة وآداء حاجاتهم جميعا، لزم فى العقل الفزع إلى ناصح صدوق.
And in what we have mentioned, the necessity of affirming the messengers to guide them to the landmarks of justice and truth and the harms of their opposites, indicating everything whose essence is problematic; so that the state of affairs may be in accordance with praise, and God is the granter of success. Afterwards, no rational person in the witnessed world is satisfied with neglecting himself, indulging in desires; rather, everyone strives to rectify them in a way that does not harm him and that has praiseworthy consequences, despite the ignorance that destroys him by that through which he hopes for his salvation, and harms him by that through which he seeks benefit. So that necessitates for him someone who knows the outcomes of affairs, so that he may discipline his soul according to his instructions, without abandoning it to its desires. And there is no power except with God. Then we return to debating the one who denies the prophethood for existence which we mentioned, after his affirmation of tawhid and his belief in command and prohibition, along with what we mentioned of the proofs of command and prohibition coupled with the need for the prophethood—sufficient for one who corrects his soul. Then we say: It is obligatory to affirm prophethood by rational necessity on account of the requirement of need for it in religion and the world. Then [it is also obligatory] in establishing the graces from God, even if reason is independent of it, for the matter of this world is also through it the support of religion. For instance, He created human beings and made them people of trial, and brought forth from the earth by what He sent down from the sky water, nourishment and medicine for them, then brought forth from it ailments and deadly poisons, and granted in their intellects the test through themselves to know the harmful from the beneficial, because of the possible destruction of the one tested. And there is no way in intellects to know that, so it is necessary to affirm one whom God informs about every essence of them, so that they may live by what they eat for their bodies and establish their religion through it. Then at the beginning, there is no way in intellects to know the modes by which things grow from agriculture and the planning involved therein. Then after completion and knowledge of the essence, there must be someone who knows how to use it until it becomes suitable for nourishment, according to the differences made for its rectification. Then He placed in food types of harm that impel the beneficiary if he does not preserve its limit, because he is among those who know its limit, then its remedy if it harms him, to the extent by which its harm is repelled. Then the sciences of medicine, despite the diversity of temperaments and the use of deadly poisons, so that they may know the measure of benefit that sustains the body. Then the types of crafts by which their covering and shelter are established, and protection from heat and cold. Then regarding the intractable animals created for them, which one who reflects upon does not know for what benefit they were created, nor whether they were created for their benefits or not, nor how to tame them, since the nature of each is aversion to that which is for it, until they become obedient and submissive. Then the types of trades without which neither religion nor worldly life can be established for them. Then the distribution of their needs across lands, which is not in their nature nor in their intellects to guide them or clarify for them in every need where it is sought. Then the knowledge of its routes, since there is nothing in the intellect that indicates its location or its routes. Then the recognition of languages by which the soul established livelihood and understanding of the afterlife, then the recognition of names without which no need would be understood nor would anyone be able to know its location. Then the aspects of the causes of reproduction, and the knowledge of raising children. Then the knowledge of managing nourishment for what cannot be reached, then the manifestation, through the knowledge of some creatures from others—all that we mentioned of languages, names, crafts, medicine, industries, routes of lands, taming of animals, and the mode of their use, and all that we mentioned—is the clear evidence that their origins are teaching and indication, not extraction by intellects. And God is the granter of success. This, along with the known actual phenomenon of some people seeking refuge with others at calamities and what grieves them from important matters, to seek help from their opinion and to defer to their counsel, because of the extra knowledge they possess in the world. Then the teaching of the arts of etiquette, then the undertaking of each to acquire types of knowledge from studying books and listening to the wise. So that indicates that they did not consider their intellects sufficient for seeking help and fulfilling all their needs; thus it is rationally necessary to seek refuge with a truthful advisor.

The Duty of Gratitude Necessitates Prophets
Maturidi's rational proof of prophethood based on gratitude's requirements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Maturidi's rational proof that the duty of gratitude to God necessitates the sending of messengers to inform humanity of how to properly thank Him.

Core Idea
The duty of gratitude requires a divine informer, because reason alone cannot specify how to thank God for all blessings.

Imam Maturidi presents a profound argument: reason itself tells us that knowing and thanking the Benefactor is good. Yet, reason also recognizes that it cannot determine the complete way to thank God for every blessing, especially those affecting each sense and limb. This gap between the duty and its fulfillment demands a messenger from God. In this passage, Maturidi unpacks this reasoning with two key observations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once. Notice the logical flow from moral duty to the necessity of a messenger.
The argument is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the foundational premise
Maturidi begins by affirming that reason deems gratitude to God as good and ingratitude as evil. This is the starting point for the entire proof.
This premise is taken as axiomatic in kalam.
3
Examine the two arguments
The passage presents two statements (عبارتان) explaining why reason cannot fulfill gratitude on its own. Find each and understand how they demonstrate the need for a messenger.
The first focuses on the variance in blessings; the second on their distribution on limbs.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Both arguments lead to the same conclusion: reason necessitates a messenger (مخبر) to inform about God and the proper way to thank Him.
This conclusion is a key doctrine in Maturidi theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم مما يلزم القول بالرسالة بضرورة العقل هو أنه قد ثبت حسن معرفة المنعم والشكر له فى العقل ، وقبح الجحود له والكفران بنعمته ، ثم ما من شيء تقع عليه حاسة من حواسه إلا والله عليه في سلامة حاسته وما أدرك نعم يعجز عن الإحاطة بنهايتها ، ثم بعد هذا له عبارتان : إحداهما تفاوت استحقاق المنعمين الشكر ، وتفاضل أقدار النعم مما لا يبلغ علم أحد نهايتها إلا علم من أنشأها : فعلى هذا لا يبلغ عقل بما به تمام شكرها إلا هو ، فيلزم العقل من يخبر عمن منه تلك النعم والأخرى أن تلك النعم إذ هي تفرقت على الحواس وأصابت كل جارحة منها ، فلزم استعمال كل جارحة في شكر ما له عليها من النعم . مع ما إذا أردت أن تعرف قدرها اعتبر بالمبتلى بالآفة بها لعلة يخف عليه بذل الدنيا ، ثم كان ما بكل جارحة تؤدى من الشكر لا يعرف بالعقل ، فيلزم القول بمخبر يخبر عن الله
Then among what necessitates affirming prophethood by rational necessity is that it is established in the intellect that knowing the Benefactor and thanking Him is good, and that denying Him and ingratitude for His blessings is evil. Then there is nothing that a sense perceives except that God has [bestowed] upon him the integrity of that sense and that he has grasped blessings whose limit he cannot encompass. Then after this, there are two statements: one of them is the variance in the due gratitude to benefactors and the disparity in the degrees of blessings, whose limit no one's knowledge reaches except the knowledge of the One who originated them. Thus, the intellect does not attain the means to fulfill complete gratitude except [through] Him. So the intellect necessitates one who informs about whom these blessings come from. And the other [statement] is that since those blessings are distributed among the senses and affect each limb, then it is necessary to employ each limb in gratitude for the blessings upon it. Moreover, when you want to know the extent of them, consider one afflicted with a defect in that [limb] for a reason that makes the sacrifice of this world light to him. Then what each limb performs of gratitude is not known by the intellect. So it necessitates affirming an informer who informs about God.
Source pages p. 780

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof for the necessity of messengers (رسالة). Maturidi begins with a foundational ethical truth: reason deems knowledge of the Benefactor and gratitude to Him as good, and ingratitude as evil. Then he notes that every sense is a blessing from God, and their limits are beyond human comprehension. He then presents two argumentative statements (عبارتان): First, gratitude owed to different benefactors varies, and the degrees of blessings are known fully only to God. Therefore, human intellect cannot determine the complete way to thank God; only He can inform us. Thus, reason demands an informant (مخبر) from God. Second, blessings are distributed across all senses and limbs, so each limb must be used in gratitude appropriate to it. However, the specific form of gratitude for each limb is not rationally knowable (e.g., prayer, fasting). This again necessitates a messenger to teach the proper modes of worship. The proof is based on the insufficiency of reason to fulfill the rational duty of gratitude.

Key Terms
الرسالة
الشكر
المنعم
المخبر
العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi denies reason's role in knowing God.
Correction: Maturidi affirms reason's duty to know God and be grateful, but shows its limits in specifying the complete method of gratitude, thus requiring revelation.

Checkpoint

A) Because reason cannot know God at all
B) Because reason knows gratitude is due but cannot specify its complete performance
C) Because people always disagree
D) Because God is hidden from reason

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that reason's recognition of its own limits in fulfilling gratitude necessitates accepting divine messengers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Which prayer or fasting duty do you avoid because you think it is 'irrational'? Repent of that arrogance and commit to following the sunnah today.

The second argument says each limb requires a gratitude not known by reason. Think of a blessing you enjoy daily—like your eyesight or hearing. What specific gratitude for that blessing have you omitted because you thought your general thankfulness was enough? How will you now employ that limb in worship as informed by revelation?

Human Flexibility Points to Worship and a Revealer
Maturidi argues that human bodily flexibility proves creation for worship, requiring a revealer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the unique flexibility of human limbs to argue that humans were created for worship and why this necessitates a divine messenger to explain the specific acts of worship.

Core Idea
Human limbs are too flexible for survival alone; they reveal a higher purpose: worship.

Maturidi presents a profound argument from human anatomy. He observes that our limbs are capable of an extraordinary range of motions—far more than needed for basic survival. This suggests a purpose beyond worldly benefit. But reason alone cannot tell us exactly how each limb should be used in worship. That is why we need a messenger from God. Let's explore this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation to grasp the core argument. Notice the key terms: خلقة, العبادة, المبين, مائية.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the evidence: flexibility and actions
Maturidi lists specific actions made possible by joint flexibility: grip, extend, give, take, turn over, spread. These go beyond mere survival needs.
Contrast with animals: they have limited capacity for work and benefit only.
3
Understand the conditional argument
The passage uses a conditional: 'had He not created him for the use of all that... He would have made him have capacity for work and benefit only.' This logic eliminates other explanations.
If the purpose was worldly, limited capacity would suffice.
4
Draw the conclusion: created for worship
The extensive capabilities indicate a higher purpose—worship ('ibadah). But worship requires specific actions that reason alone cannot determine.
Hence the need for a 'mubayyin' (explainer) for each limb.
5
Reflect on the implication for prophethood
This argument supports the necessity of messengers: they reveal the specific worship (ma'iyyah) for every limb.
Connects to broader topic of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن الله إذ خلق البشر خلقا أمكنه استعمال كل جارحة منه بما جعل من اللين بالمفاصل يقبض بها ويبسط ويعطى ويأخذ ويتقلب على مختلف الأحوال وينتشر في مفترق الأفعال مما لو لم يكن خلقه لاستعمال جميع ذلك في العادة لجعل فيه وسع العمل والنفع خاصة كالدواب والطير ، فثبت أنه خلق للعبادة . فلا بد من مبين مائيةها في كل جارحة
And also, since God created humans as a creation that makes it possible for him to use each of his limbs, due to what He placed of flexibility in the joints, so that he can grip and extend, give and take, turn over in various states, and spread out in diverse actions—had He not created him for the use of all that in the customary way, He would have made him have capacity for work and benefit only, like beasts and birds. So it is established that he was created for worship. Therefore, there must be an explainer of the quiddity of that in each limb.
Source pages p. 780

Commentary
This is a supplementary argument for the necessity of messengers based on human anatomy. Maturidi observes that humans have uniquely flexible limbs and joints that allow a wide range of actions (gripping, extending, giving, taking, turning, moving). This is unlike animals and birds, which are limited to specific functions for survival. If humans were created only for worldly benefit, they would have been given limited capacity like animals. But the extensive physical capabilities indicate a higher purpose: worship (عبادة). However, reason alone does not specify what acts of worship each limb should perform (e.g., bowing, prostrating, donating). Therefore, a messenger (مبين) is needed to explain the specific worship (مائية) for each limb. This completes the previous argument.

Key Terms
خلقة
العبادة
المبين
مائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument merely proves that the human body is designed or complex.
Correction: Maturidi's argument goes further: the specific flexibility points to a purpose (worship) that is not self-evident, thus requiring a messenger to reveal it.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does the flexibility of human limbs necessitate a messenger?
A) Because humans are stronger than animals
B) Because the limbs are capable of many actions, indicating creation for worship, but the specific worship is not known by reason
C) Because humans need to work for a living
D) Because joints are complex

Key Takeaway
The remarkable flexibility of human limbs indicates that humans were created for worship, which requires a divinely revealed explanation for each limb's role in worship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have hands that can type, build, and heal. But what if your hands are meant primarily to worship? Which daily action of your hands does not fulfill that purpose, and how will you change that starting now?
?
The passage says animals are limited to work and benefit, but you are not. Are you living like an animal, using your limbs only for worldly gain? What is one limb you have neglected in worship, and what act of worship will you perform with it today?
?
God placed flexibility in your joints so you could bow, prostrate, and give. When did you last bow in prayer with full presence? If your answer shames you, commit to praying one prayer today with total awareness that your body is fulfilling its purpose.

Prophethood

Rational Necessity of Prophethood from Gratitude
Maturidi argues that reason requires a messenger to explain how to thank God for blessings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that reason requires a messenger to fulfill the obligation of gratitude for divine blessings.

Core Idea
Reason knows gratitude is due, not how to pay it fully.

Maturidi builds a rational proof for prophethood by starting with a universal moral judgment: it is good to thank the Benefactor and evil to be ungrateful. But this judgment only tells us that gratitude is obligatory; it cannot tell us how to thank God for each specific blessing in the right measure. Therefore, reason itself points to the need for a messenger who conveys God's teachings on how to be grateful.

Guided Path
1
Identify the foundational moral judgment
Read the opening line where Maturidi asserts that reason recognizes the goodness of knowing the Benefactor and thanking Him, and the evil of denial and ingratitude.
This is a rational axiom in kalam.
2
Notice the vastness of blessings
Maturidi points out that every sensory perception involves a blessing from God, and humans cannot enumerate them.
This sets up the need for divine guidance.
3
Examine the two expressions (عبارتان)
Maturidi gives two reasons why reason cannot fully determine gratitude: the varying degrees of blessings known only to God, and the need to use each limb appropriately for its specific blessing.
Pay attention to the second expression's analogy of the afflicted person.
4
Understand the conclusion
Conclude that reason necessitates a messenger (مخبر) to inform about God and how to thank Him properly.
This is the core of the necessity of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم مما يلزم القول بالرسالة بضرورة العقل هو أنه قد ثبت حسن معرفة المنعم والشكر له في العقل، وقبح الجحود له والكفران بنعمته، ثم ما من شيء تقع عليه حاسة من حواسه إلا والله عليه في سلامة حاسته وما أدرك نعم يعجز عن الإحاطة بنهايتها على أخذ، ثم بعد هذا له عبارتان: إحداهما تفاوت استحقاق المنعمين الشكر، وتفاضل أقدار النعم مما لا يبلغ علم أحد نهايتها إلا علم من أنشأها: فعلى هذا لا يبلغ عقل بما به تمام شكرها إلا هو، فيلزم العقل من يخبر عمن منه تلك النعم والأخرى أن تلك النعم إذ هي تفرقت على الحواس وأصابت كل جارحة منها، فلزم استعمال كل جارحة في شكر ما له عليها من النعم. مع ما إذا أردت أن تعرف قدرها اعتبر بالمبتلى بالآفة بها لعلة يخف عليه بذل الدنيا، ثم كان ما بكل جارحة تؤدي من الشكر لا يعرف بالعقل، فيلزم القول بمخبر يخبر عن الله
Then among what necessitates the assertion of prophethood by rational necessity is that it has been established in reason the goodness of knowing the Benefactor and thanking Him, and the evil of denying Him and ingratitude for His blessings. Then there is no thing which one of his senses perceives except that God has a favor upon him in the soundness of his sense and what he perceives of blessings whose limit he is incapable of encompassing upon taking? Then after this he has two expressions: one is the variation in the deserving of gratitude to the benefactors, and the difference in the measures of blessings whose limit the knowledge of no one reaches except the knowledge of the One who originated them: thus on this basis, reason cannot attain the complete gratitude for them except Him, so reason necessitates one who informs about whom those blessings come from. And the other [expression] is that those blessings, since they are distributed among the senses and each limb receives its share, it becomes necessary to employ each limb in gratitude for what blessings it has upon it. With this, when you wish to know its measure, consider the one afflicted with a defect in it for a reason that makes the giving of the world light to him. Then what each limb performs of gratitude is not known by reason, so the assertion of an informant who informs about God is necessitated.
Source pages p. 780

Commentary
This passage provides a rational proof for the necessity of prophethood. It begins by stating a foundational principle: reason affirms the goodness of knowing the Benefactor (al-mun'im) and thanking Him, and the evil of denying Him and being ungrateful. This is a basic moral judgment known to reason. Then Maturidi notes that every blessing one perceives through the senses entails a favor from God in the soundness of that sense and the blessing itself, and humans are incapable of encompassing the limit of these blessings. After this, he presents two observations (عبارتان): First, the degrees of gratitude owed to benefactors and the measures of blessings vary so greatly that no human can know their full extent except the One who originated them. Therefore, reason alone cannot achieve complete gratitude; it needs someone to inform it about the true source of blessings (God). Second, since blessings are distributed across different senses and limbs, each limb must be used in gratitude for its specific blessings. The magnitude of these blessings can be appreciated by considering a person afflicted with a loss of that limb, who would sacrifice the world to regain it. But the specific form of gratitude owed by each limb cannot be known by reason alone; thus, a messenger (مخبر) from God is necessary to teach this.

Key Terms
الرسالة
ضرورة العقل
المنعم
الشكر
النعم
مخبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reason alone can fully determine how to thank God for every blessing.
Correction: Maturidi argues that while reason knows the obligation of gratitude, it cannot know the specific measure or manner appropriate for each blessing, necessitating a messenger.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does reason necessitate a messenger?
A) Because reason cannot know the existence of God.
B) Because the full measure and manner of gratitude for blessings cannot be known by reason alone.
C) Because messengers prove their own truth through miracles.
D) Because humans are naturally lazy in worship.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi proves that reason alone cannot achieve complete gratitude for God's blessings, so a messenger is rationally necessary to teach the proper way to thank God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that every blessing you have is from God, but can you honestly say you have thanked Him for each sense you possess? What would it take for you to start employing your limbs in gratitude as the lesson demands?
?
If reason cannot tell you how to thank God for the eyes you use to read this, where do you turn? Are you truly relying on the messenger, or have you invented your own ways of showing gratitude?
?
The affliction of a loss of a limb would make the world seem light to regain it. When was the last time you felt that weight of gratitude for what you have? What are you taking for granted right now that you would sacrifice everything to keep?

Humans Created for Worship Need a Guide
Maturidi argues from human anatomy that worship requires a prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the flexibility and range of human limbs indicate a purpose of worship, and why that necessitates a prophetic guide to explain the proper use of each limb.

Core Idea
Our flexible limbs are evidence that we are made for worship, not just survival.

Maturidi presents a creative argument from human anatomy to prove that humans are created for a higher purpose than animals. Since we have extra dexterity beyond survival needs, we must be intended for worship—and that worship requires a divinely appointed guide to teach us how to use each limb.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the list of actions: grasping, spreading, giving, taking, turning, and diverse actions.
Let the descriptive details sink in before interpreting.
2
Contrast humans with animals
Maturidi says if humans were only for survival, they would have limited capacities like beasts and birds. Identify this contrast and see how it sets up his argument.
Animals have just enough ability for work and benefit.
3
Understand the inference
The extra capacity beyond survival points to a higher purpose: worship (العبادة). Ask yourself: why does extra flexibility imply worship?
Think about what worship requires: diverse bodily actions with intention.
4
Realize the need for a guide
Since worship is specific and detailed, reason alone cannot deduce how to use each limb properly. Therefore, there must be an explainer (مبين) – a prophet – to teach the function (مائية) of each limb in worship.
This connects to the broader topic of prophethood.
5
Reflect on the role of reason
Consider that Maturidi is showing the limitation of reason in matters of worship. Reason can know there is a Creator, but not the detailed acts of devotion. Revelation is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن الله إذ خلق البشر خلقا أمكنه استعمال كل جارحة منه بما جعل من اللين بالمفاصل يقبض بها ويبسط ويعطى ويأخذ ويتقلب على مختلف الأحوال وينتشر في مفترق الأفعال مما لو لم يكن خلقه لاستعمال جميع ذلك في العادة لجعل فيه وسع العمل والنفع خاصة كالدواب والطيور، فثبت أنه خلق للعبادة. فلا بد من مبين مائيتها في كل جارحة
And also, when God created humans, He created them with the ability to use each limb, with the suppleness He placed in the joints, to grasp and spread out, to give and take, to turn over in various states, and to spread out in diverse actions—such that if He had not created them for the use of all that in the normal way, He would have placed in them capacity for work and benefit only, like beasts and birds. Thus it is established that he was created for worship. So there must be an explainer of the function of each limb.
Source pages p. 780

Commentary
This passage presents another argument for the necessity of prophets. Maturidi observes that God created humans with a unique physical structure: each limb has flexibility and joints allowing a wide range of movements—grasping, spreading, giving, taking, turning, and diverse actions. If humans were created merely for survival and utility, like animals (دواب) and birds, they would have been given only sufficient capacity for work and benefit. The fact that humans have this extra capacity and dexterity indicates that they were created for a higher purpose: worship (العبادة). Therefore, there must be someone (a prophet) to explain (مبين) the proper function (مائية) of each limb in worship, i.e., how to use them in acts of devotion. This is because reason alone cannot deduce the specific acts of worship required for each body part.

The Need for an Arbiter
First proof: conflict requires divine arbiter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's first rational proof for the necessity of prophets, based on the existence of conflict among people and the need for a divine arbiter.

Core Idea
Human conflict shows reason's limits—only divine guidance can unite.

Maturidi begins his argument for prophethood with a simple observation: people constantly disagree and claim to be right, yet no one among them can resolve disputes impartially. This leads to corruption, which reason recognizes as evil. Reason thus points to the need for a messenger from the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof passage
Read the Arabic passage describing conflict and the lack of an arbiter. Let the argument unfold.
Pay attention to the phrase 'ضرورة العقل'.
2
Understand conflict as corruption
Maturidi states that conflict is the root of all corruption and destruction, which reason deems evil. Accept this premise.
3
Identify reason's limit
Reason needs aid to return to goodness and knowledge. That aid must come from the Creator, who knows best.
This is a key move: reason recognizing its own limitation.
4
Conclude the necessity of a messenger
The only way for reason to receive that aid is through a messenger sent by the Creator.
This establishes the first proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل في ذلك. مما يوجب ضرورة العقل الحاجة إلى الرسل وجوه: أحدها وجود التنازع الظاهر بين الخلق على ادعاء كل منهم أحق بالحق وأولى به بالإصابة، واتفاق أن ليس فيهم من يفزع إليه ليحكم بينهم، ويريهم يتألف قلوبهم وتجتمع كلمتهم، ومعلوم أن التنازع هو أصل كل فساد ومقدمة كل فناء، وذلك كله قبيح في العقول، فقد انتهت عاقبة العقول إلى من يعينها ويردها إلى ما جعلت له من الصلاح والمعرفة، ومعلوم أن لا أحد أعلم بذلك ممن خلقها وأنشأها، وفي ذلك لزوم القول برسول نعلم أنه من عنده جاء، ودليل آخر: إنه معلوم أن العلماء يتفاضلون في إدراك ما به مصالحهم في أمر الدين والدنيا، يكون عند واحد من ذلك ما ليس عند غيره، وإذا ثبت ذلك فلا ندفع أن يكون عند الله مما به صلاح عباده مما ليس عند خلقه، فيوصلها إليهم برسله
Then the foundation in that—from what necessitates the need for messengers by rational necessity—are aspects: one of them is the existence of manifest conflict among creation, each claiming that he is most entitled to truth and most deserving of hitting it; and it is agreed that there is none among them to whom recourse is had to judge between them, and to show them so that their hearts are united and their word is gathered. And it is known that conflict is the root of all corruption and the prelude of all destruction, and all that is evil in intellects. So the outcome of intellects comes to an end at one who aids them and returns them to the goodness and knowledge for which they were made. And it is known that no one is more knowledgeable of that than the One who created them and originated them. In that is the necessity of asserting a messenger whom we know has come from Him. And another proof: it is known that scholars vary in their perception of the means of their interests in matters of religion and world; one may have something of that which another does not. And when that is established, we do not deny that there is with God what provides the well-being of His servants which is not with His creation, so He conveys it to them through His messengers.
Source pages p. 780

Commentary
This passage introduces the first two of several rational proofs (وجوه) for the necessity of prophets. The first proof is based on the existence of manifest conflict (التنازع الظاهر) among people. Everyone claims to be correct, but there is no one among them to arbitrate and unite them. Conflict is the source of all corruption and destruction, which is recognized as evil by reason. Reason thus reaches its limit and requires an external guide who can return it to the good and knowledge for which it was created. That guide must be the Creator, who knows best, and He sends a messenger. The second proof is the observable inequality among scholars (تفاضل العلماء): some know things others do not, in both religious and worldly matters. If such differences exist among humans, it is plausible that God has knowledge essential for the well-being of His servants that He has not given to any creature, and He conveys it through messengers. Both proofs rely on rational necessity (ضرورة العقل).

Key Terms
التنازع
الفساد
ضرورة العقل
الرسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reason alone can resolve all human conflicts and determine truth.
Correction: Maturidi argues that reason itself recognizes its inability to resolve conflict without divine guidance, and thus points to the necessity of a messenger.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi identify as the root of all corruption?
A) Ignorance
B) Conflict
C) Lack of knowledge
D) Human selfishness

Key Takeaway
Human conflict reveals reason's limitation, necessitating a messenger from the Creator to guide humanity back to goodness and knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are in a conflict with another believer, do you honestly seek the judgment of God's revelation, or do you insist on your own reasoning and desire to win? What would it cost you to submit fully to the truth even if it means admitting you are wrong?
?
Think of a recurring dispute in your life—whether with a family member, friend, or colleague. Are you clinging to the idea that you are 'more entitled to the truth'? How can you actively turn to divine guidance instead of your own pride to bring about reconciliation today?
?
The passage teaches that unresolved conflict leads to corruption and destruction. Is there a relationship in your life that is slowly being destroyed because you refuse to let go of your claim? What is one concrete step you can take this week to seek a divinely-guided resolution rather than feeding the conflict?
