The Inequality of Knowledge
Second proof: inequality of knowledge among scholars suggests divine knowledge via messengers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's second rational proof for prophethood, based on the observable differences in knowledge among scholars.

Core Idea
If scholars differ, God may have knowledge beyond human reach.

The second proof also begins with an observable fact: scholars vary greatly in their understanding of religious and worldly affairs. Some know what others do not. If such inequality exists among humans, it is plausible that God possesses knowledge essential for human well-being that He has not disclosed to any creature, and He conveys it through messengers.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof passage
Read the Arabic passage describing the variation among scholars.
Notice the phrase 'التفاضل' (inequality).
2
Understand the premise: scholars vary
It is known that scholars differ in their perception of what benefits them in religion and world. This is an empirical claim.
3
Accept the possibility of divine knowledge
If scholars vary, we cannot deny that God may have knowledge that is not available to any human, which He conveys through messengers.
This is an argument from analogy and plausibility.
4
Conclude the necessity of messengers
Since God conveys this special knowledge through messengers, the existence of messengers is necessary.
This completes the second proof.

Maturidi's Third Proof: Reason vs Following Others
Maturidi's third rational proof for the necessity of a messenger

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational proof that reliance on reason alone leads to contradiction, and that following a superior intellect implies the need for a divinely appointed messenger.

Core Idea
Your reason can't be the final guide; someone must be sent from God.

Maturidi presents a powerful rational argument that cuts to the heart of why human reason alone is insufficient for guidance. He poses a dilemma: either each person follows their own reason, or they follow someone intellectually superior. The first leads to irreconcilable contradictions; the second forces us to admit that the guide is effectively a messenger from God, whose identity must be confirmed by a sign.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the structure of the dilemma.
This is a dense logical argument; take your time.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The passage presents two possibilities: either a person relies on his own reason (which may be distracted) or he follows someone with superior reason. Identify each horn and notice how they are introduced.
The first horn is introduced with 'يرجع إلى ما يدعوه إليه غفلة' and the second with 'يلزم على بعض الصدور عما أراه غيره'.
3
Analyze the first horn: reliance on own reason
If everyone's own reason were correct, then all would be right, but their opinions contradict. Therefore, this is impossible. Understand why Maturidi finds this unacceptable.
Contradiction in opinions (تناقض الآراء) is key.
4
Analyze the second horn: following a superior intellect
If one follows a superior reason, that person's reason functions like a messenger. But then we need a proof (دليل) to identify that person and confirm his truthfulness. Maturidi argues this proof is no different from a prophet's sign.
The equivalence between a prophet's truthfulness and a sage's insight is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليل آخر: إنه لا يخلو الأمر من أن يرجع إلى ما يدعوه إليه غفلة، أو يلزم على بعض الصدور عما أراه غيره ممن هو أرجح منه عقلا، فإن كان الحق الأول ليجب الجمع بين العقول والقول لكل بالإصابة إذا قال بما أراه عقله، وفى ذلك شهادة بإصابة كل ذي دين اعتمد على عقله، وذلك محال لتناقض الآراء والقول، وإن كان الوجه الثاني فيصير عقله كرسول يأنيهم من عند الله، فيحتاج ذلك إلى دليل يُعلمنا شخصه، ثم لا فصل بين دليل يقوم بصدقه فيما يخبر عن الله أو بإصابة الحق فى كل ما ينطق به عن عقله
And another proof: the matter is not free from either [a person] reverting to distraction regarding what he is called to, or the obligation on some to refrain from what his own reason shows him in favor of someone else who is more sound in reason. For if the first is true, then it would be necessary to join all intellects and say that every one is correct when he says what his reason shows him; and in that is a testimony to the correctness of every possessor of religion who relies on his reason—and that is impossible due to the contradiction of opinions and statements. And if the second is the case, then his reason becomes like a messenger coming to them from God, and that requires a proof that makes known to us his identity. Then there is no distinction between a proof that establishes his truthfulness in what he informs about God, or [a proof] that establishes the correctness of truth in everything he speaks from his reason.
Source pages p. 781

Commentary
This passage presents a third rational proof for the necessity of a messenger. The argument poses a dilemma: either a person relies on his own reason (which may be distracted 'غفلة' or follow his own opinion) or he follows someone else who is intellectually superior. If the first option were correct, then all rational beings would be correct in their varying opinions, which is impossible because they contradict each other. If the second option is adopted, then the superior intellect effectively becomes a 'messenger' (رسول) for others, guiding them with his reason. But then we need a proof (دليل) to identify that person and confirm that his reason is truly guided by God. There is no difference between proving a prophet's truthfulness in his reports about God and proving the correctness of what he says from his own reason. In other words, if we accept that a person's reasoned conclusions are authoritative, we need a divine sign to distinguish the true guide from others. This leads to the necessity of an identifiable messenger with a proof.

Key Terms
الغفلة
الآراء
الراجح عقلا
الرسول
الدليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think following a scholar without divine appointment is sufficient.
Correction: Maturidi argues that even when following a superior intellect, we need a divine proof to identify that person as a true guide—essentially a prophet.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about the second possibility (following a superior intellect)?
A) It is always correct.
B) It makes that person's reason like a messenger, requiring a proof of his identity.
C) It is unnecessary because everyone has equal reason.
D) It leads to total reliance on reason without need for prophets.

Key Takeaway
Human reason alone cannot guide to truth without contradiction; true guidance requires a divinely confirmed messenger.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Whose intellect are you following in matters of faith? Is it the intellect of a scholar, your own, or a divinely guided messenger? Have you ever asked for a proof from God that the person you follow is truly sent by Him?
?
When your own reasoning tells you something that contradicts what a prophet taught, which do you trust? Are you willing to submit your reason to a messenger with a clear proof, even if it goes against your desires?
?
If you rely on your own reason alone, how do you resolve the fact that your conclusions might contradict those of another sincere believer? Can you live with that contradiction, or does it push you to seek a higher authority?

First proof for the necessity of prophets: conflict and need for a judge
Maturidi presents the first rational argument for the necessity of prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the reality of human conflict to demonstrate the rational necessity of divine messengers.

Core Idea
Reason itself acknowledges its limits and points to the need for a prophet.

Maturidi begins by grounding the need for prophets in reason itself, not blind faith. He presents a logical proof that starts with an undeniable human experience: conflict. As you read, notice how he moves from a social observation to a rational conclusion about divine guidance.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without pausing. Let the rhythm of the argument sink in. Then read the literal translation to grasp the meaning.
Pay attention to the phrase 'ضرورة العقل'—this is the key concept.
2
Identify the logical steps
Break down the argument into its premises: conflict exists, no one can judge, conflict leads to corruption, reason deems this evil, reason needs external aid, the Creator knows best.
Write down each step in your own words.
3
Reflect on the role of reason
Notice that Maturidi does not deny reason but shows its limitation. Reason recognizes its own inability to resolve fundamental disputes and thus points to a higher source.
This is a key point in Islamic theology: reason and revelation are complementary.
4
Connect to prophethood
Understand how this proof leads to the necessity of a messenger from God. The argument is not just about social order but about the very purpose of human intellect.
This proof is the first of several aspects Maturidi will present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل في ذلك. مما يوجب ضرورة العقل الحاجة إلى الرسل وجوه : أحدها وجود التنازع الظاهر بين الخلق على ادعاء كل منهم أحق بالحق وأولى به بالإصابة ، واتفاق أن ليس فيهم من يفزع إليه ليحكم بينهم ، ويريهم يتألف قلوبهم وتجتمع كلمتهم ، ومعلوم أن التنازع هو أصل كل فساد ومقدمة كل فناء ، وذلك كله قبيح في العقول ، فقد انتهت عاقبة العقول إلى من يعينها ويردها إلى ما جعلت له من الصلاح والمعرفة ، ومعلوم أن لا أحد أعلم بذلك ممن خلقها وأنشأها ، وفي ذلك لزوم القول برسول نعلم أنه من عنده جاء . وبالله التوفيق .
Then the foundation in that which necessitates by reason the need for messengers: aspects. One of them is the existence of apparent conflict among creation, each claiming that he is more entitled to the truth and more worthy of correctness, and the agreement that there is none among them to whom they can turn to judge between them and show them what unites their hearts and brings together their word. And it is known that conflict is the root of every corruption and the prelude to every destruction, and all of that is evil (qabīḥ) in the intellects. Thus the outcome of the intellects leads to one who aids them and returns them to what they were created for—of goodness and knowledge. And it is known that no one is more knowledgeable of that than He who created and originated them. And in that is the necessity of affirming a messenger whom we know came from Him. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 781

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (dalīl) for the necessity of prophets. Maturidi states that reason itself necessitates the need for messengers, and he begins with the first aspect (wajh): the observable fact of conflict (tanāzuʿ) among people, each claiming exclusive correctness. There is no one among them to act as an impartial judge to unite them. Conflict is known to be the root of all corruption and destruction—this is deemed evil by sound intellects. Therefore, the natural conclusion of reason is to seek someone who can assist it and guide it back to its intended purpose of goodness and knowledge. The one best qualified for this is the Creator Himself, who alone has full knowledge. Hence, this argument compels the affirmation of a messenger sent from God.

Key Terms
ضرورة العقل
التنازع
قبيح
لزوم القول برسول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is only about social order and conflict resolution.
Correction: Maturidi's argument is deeper: it shows that reason itself, when properly used, recognizes its own limitation and points to the need for divine guidance, not just a human arbitrator.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why does the existence of conflict necessitate the need for a prophet?
A) Because conflict is the root of corruption and reason cannot resolve it alone
B) Because people are naturally evil
C) Because prophets enjoy conflict
D) Because reason is unnecessary

Key Takeaway
The first proof for prophethood is based on the rational observation that human conflict and the absence of an impartial judge lead reason to seek divine guidance through a messenger.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been in a conflict where you were absolutely certain you were right? How does Maturidi's observation that each person claims to be more entitled to the truth challenge your own certainty? Will you now seek God's guidance before insisting on your own view?
?
Maturidi says that conflict is the root of every corruption. Look at your own relationships: where are you contributing to conflict rather than seeking resolution? What would it mean for you to acknowledge that your intellect alone might not be enough to reach the truth?
?
If reason itself points to the need for a messenger, then rejecting divine guidance is a rejection of reason's own conclusion. Are you willing to submit your intellect to the messenger God sent? What would that require of you today?

Second Proof: Variation in Knowledge as Evidence for Prophethood
Maturidi argues from scholarly variation to the possibility of prophetic knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's second proof for prophethood, which argues from the observed inequality of knowledge among people to the logical possibility of God sending prophets for human welfare.

Core Idea
If scholars differ in knowledge, God may bestow special knowledge through prophets.

Maturidi offers a simple yet profound argument: we observe that scholars vary in what they know; one may understand something another does not. If this variation is ordinary in human affairs, why would we deny that God could have unique knowledge that He sends for human welfare through prophets? This passage presents a logical possibility grounded in everyday reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very concise style here.
2
Identify the premise
The passage starts with an observable fact: scholars differ (yatafāḍalūn) in their understanding of what benefits them in religion and this world. One has knowledge that another lacks.
3
Understand the inference
From this established variation, Maturidi argues it is not unreasonable to accept that God may have knowledge for His servants' welfare that He conveys through messengers. This is a logical possibility, not a necessity.
This proof does not force belief in prophets but opens the door to their possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليل آخر : إنه معلوم أن العلماء يتفاضلون في إدراك ما به مصالحهم في أمر الدين والدنيا ، يكون عند واحد من ذلك ما ليس عند غيره ، وإذا ثبت ذلك فلا ندفع أن يكون عند الله مما به صلاح عباده مما ليس عند خلقه، فيوصلها إليهم برسله ، والله المعين .
And another proof: It is known that scholars vary in their understanding of what concerns their interests in matters of religion and this world; one of them has that which another does not. And if that is established, then we do not deny that there could be with God something by which the welfare of His servants is achieved, which is not with His creation, and He conveys it to them through His messengers. And God is the Helper.
Source pages p. 781

Commentary
This passage presents a second proof (dalīl) for the necessity of prophets. Maturidi begins with an observable fact: scholars differ in their knowledge and understanding (yatafāḍalūn) regarding what benefits them in religious and worldly matters; what one knows, another may lack. If this variation is accepted in creation, then it is not unreasonable to affirm that God may possess knowledge that serves the welfare of His servants, which He conveys exclusively through messengers. Thus, the existence of prophets is a logical possibility grounded in the observed inequality of knowledge among people.

Key Terms
يتفاضلون
مصالحهم
صلاح عباده
برسله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The variation in knowledge among scholars implies that knowledge should be equal, or that this variation disproves the need for prophets.
Correction: Maturidi's proof is not about equality of knowledge but about the logical possibility that God may grant special knowledge to prophets for human welfare, based on the fact that people already differ in knowledge.

Checkpoint
What does the variation in knowledge among scholars imply according to this proof?
A) That all scholars are prophets
B) That it is possible God may have knowledge for people's welfare that He sends via prophets
C) That scholars do not need prophets
D) That knowledge is distributed equally

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's second proof shows that the observable variation in human knowledge opens the door to the possibility of prophetic revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Dilemma of Reason: Why Prophethood is Necessary
Maturidi presents a dilemma: either everyone is right or some need to follow those of superior intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's dilemma argument for the necessity of prophethood: either all human reasoning is correct (impossible) or some must follow a person of superior intellect, which requires a divine proof.

Core Idea
Reason alone cannot settle truth; a divine guide is logically necessary.

Maturidi presents a powerful logical dilemma to show that human reason, left to itself, cannot reach agreement on truth. This leads to the necessity of a divinely appointed messenger. In this passage, we will see how he eliminates the possibility of self-sufficiency and establishes the need for a proof from God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and the dilemma
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Note that Maturidi lays out two possibilities (a dilemma).
The passage is dense; reading it multiple times helps.
2
Understand the first horn: universal correctness leads to contradiction
If everyone follows their own mind (or heedlessness), then all contradictory opinions would be correct, which is impossible. Identify why this is rejected.
This shows that not everyone can be right.
3
Understand the second horn: following a superior intellect requires a proof
If some must follow a person of superior intellect, that person's guidance functions like a messenger from God. This requires a proof to identify that person.
The proof also establishes truthfulness in all matters.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The dilemma forces the conclusion that prophethood is necessary. Consider how this argument differs from simply trusting wise people.
The necessity of a divine proof is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليل آخر : إنه لا يخلو الأمر من أن يرجع إلى ما يدعوه إليه غفلة ، أو يلزم على بعض الصدور عما أراه غيره ممن هو أرجح منه عقلا ، فإن كان الحق الأول ليجب الجمع بين العقول والقول لكل بالإصابة إذا قال بما أراه عقله ، وفى ذلك شهادة بإصابة كل ذى دين اعتمد على عقله، وذلك محال لتناقض الآراء والقول ، وإن كان الوجه الثاني فيصير عقله كرسول يأنيهم من عند الله ، فيحتاج ذلك إلى دليل يُعلمنا شخصه ، ثم لا فصل بين دليل يقوم بصدقه فيما يخبر عن الله أو بإصابة الحق فى كل ما ينطق به عن عقله ، والله الموفق .
And another proof: The matter does not escape from either that he returns to what his heedlessness calls him to, or that he is obligated by some precedent concerning what another who is more sound in intellect than him has shown. If the first is the truth, then it would be necessary to unite the intellects and pronounce everyone correct when he says what his intellect shows him, and in that is a testimony to the correctness of every adherent of religion who relies on his intellect, and that is impossible due to the contradiction of opinions and statements. And if the second aspect, then his intellect becomes like a messenger coming to them from God, so that requires a proof that makes us know his person; then there is no distinction between a proof that establishes his truthfulness in what he reports about God or his correctness in everything he utters about his intellect. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 781

Commentary
This passage presents a third proof in the form of a dilemma. Maturidi argues that there are only two possibilities regarding human reasoning: either everyone relies on his own heedlessness (ghafla) or some are obligated to follow what a person of superior intellect (arjaḥ ʿaqlan) shows them. The first option would require that conflicting opinions are all correct, which is impossible. The second option means that the superior intellect's guidance functions like a messenger (rasūl) from God. In that case, we need a proof (dalīl) to identify that person. And once we have a proof verifying his truthfulness, there is no difference between accepting his reports about God and accepting his correctness in all he says from his intellect. Thus, the existence of a prophet is logically required.

Key Terms
غفلة
أرجح عقلا
شهادة بإصابة
تناقض الآراء
دليل يُعلمنا شخصه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims that prophets are simply intellectuals or wise people without divine appointment.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly says that following a superior intellect requires a proof (dalīl) from God to identify that person, thus it is not mere intellectual superiority but divine appointment.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi reject the first horn of the dilemma (everyone relying on their own heedlessness)?
A) Because it would lead to the impossibility of contradictory opinions all being correct
B) Because heedlessness is sinful
C) Because no one is heedless
D) Because it is a weak argument

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's dilemma shows that without divine guidance, humanity cannot achieve unanimous truth, thus prophethood is rationally necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you were to meet a person of superior intellect who claimed to have guidance from God, what proof would you demand? And would you then accept that person in all they say about God and themselves? Examine your heart: do you resist submitting to clear proofs because of pride?
?
This argument shows that without a divine messenger, humanity is trapped in contradiction. Where in your life have you been content with your own justification without seeking God's revealed standard? Will you commit today to searching for that divine proof before acting on your own opinion?

The Intellect's Limitations and the Need for Prophets
Maturidi lists obstacles to rational truth and deduces the necessity of prophecy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that worldly distractions prevent the intellect from attaining full truth, making prophetic guidance necessary.

Core Idea
Worldly distractions blind the intellect; only revelation provides clarity.

Imam Maturidi presents a practical proof for the necessity of prophethood. He observes that human beings are surrounded by occupations, desires, and pains that constantly occupy the mind. These factors prevent the intellect from reaching the truth on its own, especially in matters that are subtle or profound. Therefore, God sends messengers to guide humanity through confusion. In this passage, you will see how Maturidi builds this argument from common human experience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the repetitive structure: 'likewise' (كذلك) introduces each obstacle.
2
Identify the obstacles
List the obstacles Maturidi mentions: occupations, worries, innate dispositions, pains, desires, wishes, and pleasures. How do they occupy the intellect?
3
Understand the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that because of these obstacles, a messenger is necessary to clarify the truth when confusion arises. Reflect on why intellect alone is insufficient.
4
Connect to your own experience
Think of a time when your own desires or worries clouded your judgment. How might this show the need for guidance beyond your own reason?
This connects the proof to personal reflection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا مع ما يعلم أن الأشغال وازدحامها على العقول بلبسها ، فكذلك الهموم وأنواع ما جبل عليه البشر ، وكذلك أنواع الألم وأسباب لا تحصى مما يشغل العقول ويمنعها عن الإحاطة بالحق فى كل لطيف وجليل ، وكذلك غلبة الشهوات وكثرة الأمانى واللذات ، فلذلك لا بد من رسول الله ليبينهم ويدلهم عند الاشتباه على الحق ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
This, along with what is known that occupations and their crowding upon the intellects confuse them, likewise worries and the types of what humans are disposed to, and likewise types of pain and countless causes that occupy the intellects and prevent them from comprehending the truth in every subtle and great matter, and likewise the dominance of desires and the abundance of wishes and pleasures. Therefore, there must be a messenger of God to clarify for them and guide them to the truth in times of confusion. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 781

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (dalīl) for the necessity of a prophet based on the inherent limitations of the human intellect. Maturidi lists several factors that distract and hinder the intellect from attaining full truth: constant occupations (ashghāl), worries (humūm), innate human dispositions, various pains, countless causes that preoccupy the mind, overpowering desires (shahawāt), and abundant wishes and pleasures. Because of these obstacles, the intellect cannot independently know the truth, especially in subtle matters. Hence, a messenger from God (rasūl Allāh) is needed to clarify and guide people in times of ambiguity (ishtibāh). The lesson concludes with a reliance on God's power.

الإحاطة بالحق

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The human intellect is fully sufficient to know all truth without external guidance.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the intellect is constantly distracted by occupations, desires, and pains, preventing it from attaining full comprehension of the truth. Thus, prophetic guidance is necessary.

B) It is distracted by occupations, desires, and pains

Key Takeaway
Human intellect, though valuable, is limited by worldly distractions; hence, divine revelation through prophets is essential for true guidance.

Two Principles for the Necessity of Prophets
Maturidi explains two foundational principles: intellect's limited capacity and the two ways of knowing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two foundational principles that demonstrate why the human intellect requires prophetic revelation.

Core Idea
Intellect is limited; it needs revelation for knowledge beyond inference and report.

Imam Maturidi establishes two fundamental reasons why intellects are in need of prophets. First, the intellect, as a created instrument, has boundaries and is susceptible to error. Second, knowledge of unseen matters, including divine commands and prohibitions, can only be attained through reliable reports, not through reason alone. This passage lays the groundwork for the necessity of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note that the Arabic is corrupt in places; focus on the main ideas from the translation.
The passage cuts off incomplete, but the two principles are clear.
2
Identify the first principle
Maturidi states that intellect, like other instruments of perception, has a limit and is subject to afflictions. Thus, it cannot grasp everything on its own and needs external aid.
This principle is based on the created nature of the intellect.
3
Identify the second principle
God made perception of external things possible through two ways: inference (istidlāl) from the witnessed to the unseen, and report (khabar) from reliable sources. Many things, especially divine commands, are only knowable through report.
This shows that revelation is necessary for knowledge of the unseen.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Together, these principles demonstrate that intellect cannot independently know divine law; it needs prophets to convey reports from God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد بينا بحمد الله حاجة العقول للرسل ، والأصل فى ذلك وجهان : أحدهما أن الله تعالى جعل لكل مدرك آلة بها يدرك ، ثم هؤلاء تحيط ذاته دون أسباب تتصل به ، ثم مع تعترضه آفات يلزمه تفاديها بالمؤاذرة له من الأعوان والحفظ له من ذلك الأضداد التي في أعداء تمنع ؛ ليشهد بحق الإدراك على العلم بمنع البعد ذلك واللطافة عن العمل حق العمل ، فعلى ذلك العقل ؛ إذ هو سبب مخلوق له حد كغيره من أسباب الإدراك ، يعترضه ما يعترض غيره ، مع غموض الأشياء واستغلاقها ، ومن مادته النظر في الأسباب ، أعلى ذلك مما يسمع من كلام الحكماء ، وأحقهم من له على حكمته برهان ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وجه آخر : إن الله جل ثناؤه جعل السبب الذى به درك كل خارج عن الحسن وجهين: أحدهما الاستدلال بالذي عاين؛ إذا اتصل الغائب بالذي عاين كاتصال دخان بالنار ، وضياء الشمس بها ، وكاتصال أثر الفعل بالفاعل نحو الكتابة والبنيان ونحو ذلك . والثانى الخبر ينبئ عن حال ذلك نحو البلدان النائية والأحوال المتغيرة والأمور النازلة ، معروف ذلك عند العقلاء ، وبذلك معرفة الإنسان الأجناس والفصول والأنواع ، وأنواع الطب واللسان وعلوم الصناعات والحروب وغير ذلك . ثم نعرف الأمر والنهى ، والوعد والوعيد ، فيما ليس بمحسوس دلیله ، لا وجه لإدراكه إلا بالخبر ، وذلك نحو المباح والمحظور ، وما فيه كل
And we have explained, praise be to God, the need of intellects for messengers. The foundation in that is two aspects: One of them is that God Almighty made for every perceiver an instrument by which he perceives, then these [instruments] encompass their essence without causes connected to it, then along with that afflictions befall it that require it to avoid them by seeking assistance from helpers and preservation from those opposites that are among enemies that prevent, so that it may witness the right of perception upon knowledge by preventing distance and subtlety from working the true work. So likewise is the intellect, for it is a created cause with a limit like other causes of perception; what befalls others befalls it, along with the obscurity and closure of things, and its material is the consideration of causes, above that which is heard from the speech of the wise, and the most worthy of them is one who has a proof (burhān) for his wisdom. And there is no power except through God. Another aspect: God, exalted be His mention, made the cause by which every external thing is perceived two ways: one of them is inference (istidlāl) from what one has witnessed, when the unseen is connected to the witnessed, like the connection of smoke to fire, and the light of the sun to it, and like the connection of the effect of an act to the agent, such as writing and building and the like. And the second is report (khabar) that informs about the situation of that, such as distant lands, changing circumstances, and occurring events; that is known among rational people, and by that is the knowledge of genera, species, and types, and the types of medicine, language, crafts, wars, and others. Then we know command and prohibition, promise and threat, in what is not sensible in its proof; there is no way to perceive it except through report, and that is like the permitted (mubāḥ) and the prohibited (maḥẓūr), and everything that...
Source pages p. 782

Commentary
This passage presents two foundational principles (aṣlān) explaining why intellect needs prophets. First principle: God created instruments for perception, but these instruments have limits and are subject to afflictions (āfāt) that hinder proper functioning. Similarly, the intellect is a created instrument with a limit; it is affected by obscurity and cannot grasp everything. Hence, it needs external aid. Second principle: There are two ways of knowing external things: inference (istidlāl) from observed evidence to the unseen, and report (khabar) from reliable sources. Many things—like distant lands or historical events—can only be known through report. Similarly, knowledge of divine commands and prohibitions (al-amr wa al-nahy), promise and threat (al-waʿd wa al-waʿīd), which are not sensible, can only be known through prophetic report. Thus, prophets are necessary to convey such knowledge. The passage cuts off before completing the last enumeration.

Key Terms
آلة الإدراك
الاستدلال
الخبر
المباح والمحظور
الأمر والنهي
الوعد والوعيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi denies the role of reason and promotes blind faith in revelation.
Correction: Maturidi affirms the role of reason for matters within its scope (e.g., inference from creation to Creator) but clarifies that some knowledge, particularly of divine commands and prohibitions, is only accessible through revelation.

Checkpoint
According to the second aspect, what two methods does God provide for perceiving external things?
A) Intuition and deduction
B) Inference (istidlāl) and report (khabar)
C) Reason and faith
D) Science and religion

Key Takeaway
The intellect alone cannot grasp divine laws; it needs revelation through prophets to provide reliable reports of the unseen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a moral decision you recently made. Did you rely on your own reasoning and feelings, or did you actively seek what Allah has commanded through revelation? What does this lesson reveal about the danger of trusting your intellect alone in such matters?
?
The passage describes the intellect as a 'created cause with a limit' that is subject to 'obscurity and closure'. In what area of your faith or life have you encountered your own intellectual limits? How has revelation helped you see clearly where reason could not?
?
The lesson emphasizes that divine commands and prohibitions are known only through report (khabar). Is there any ruling or practice of Islam that you have been tempted to rationalize or adjust based on your own preferences rather than submitting to the revealed text? What specific step will you take to align your heart with the report from Allah?

The Need for Messengers and Two Ways of Knowing
Maturidi explains the need for messengers and lists two ways of knowing the unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the human intellect needs messengers and how knowledge of the unseen is acquired through inference and report.

Core Idea
The intellect is limited; messengers are needed for knowledge beyond sensory experience.

Maturidi begins by asserting that human intellects, though capable, are limited and need external guidance. He then introduces two fundamental ways to know what lies beyond sensory perception: inference from visible signs and report from trustworthy sources. This passage lays the epistemological foundation for the necessity of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the overall argument. Note the structure: the author first states the need, then presents two aspects.
The Arabic has some uncertain readings; focus on the main ideas.
2
Identify the two aspects
The passage presents two aspects (وجهان). The first discusses the nature of intellect as a limited created cause. The second gives two ways of knowing the unseen: inference and report.
The first aspect is more about why intellect needs assistance; the second focuses on how we know.
3
Understand the role of report
Maturidi argues that for non-sensible matters (like commands and prohibitions), report is the only way. This proves the necessity of messengers who deliver reports from God.
This is the core conclusion: messengerhood is required for religious knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد بينا بحمد الله حاجة العقول للرسل، والقول الإحاطة بالكل، والأصل في ذلك وجهان: أحدهما أن الله تعالى جعل لكل مدرك آلة بها يدرك، ثم هؤلاء تحيط ذاته دون أسباب تتصل به، ثم مع تعترضه آفات يلزمه تفاديها بالمؤازرة له من الأعوان والحفظ له من ذلك الأضداد التي في أعداء تمنع؛ ليشهد بحق الإدراك على العلم بمنع البعد ذلك واللطافة عن العمل حق العمل، فعلى ذلك العقل؛ إذ هو سبب مخلوق له حد كغيره من أسباب الإدراك، يعترضه ما يعترض غيره، مع غموض الأشياء واستغلاقها، ومن مادته النظر في الأسباب، أعلى ذلك مما يسمع من كلام الحكماء، وأحقهم من له على حكمته برهان، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وجه آخر: إن الله جل ثناؤه جعل السبب الذي به درك كل خارج عن الحسن وجهين: أحدهما الاستدلال بالذي عاين؛ إذا اتصل الغائب بالذي عاين كاتصال دخان بالنار، وضياء الشمس بها، وكاتصال أثر الفعل بالفاعل نحو الكتابة والبنيان ونحو ذلك. والثاني الخبر ينبئ عن حال ذلك نحو البلدان النائية والأحوال المتغيرة والأمور النازلة، معروف ذلك عند العقلاء، وبذلك معرفة الإنسان الأجناس والفصول والأنواع، وأنواع الطب واللسان وعلوم الصناعات والحروب وغير ذلك. ثم نعرف الأمر والنهى، والوعد والوعيد، فيما ليس بمحسوس دليله، لا وجه لإدراكه إلا بالخبر، وذلك نحو المباح والمحظور، وما فيه كل شيء من مختلف الأحوال، فيلزم في نحو هذا القول بالخبر، وفيه إيجاب القول بالرسالة.
And we have explained, by the praise of God, the need of intellects for messengers, and the statement of encompassing the whole. The principle in that is two aspects: One of them is that God Almighty made for every perceiver an instrument by which it perceives, then these encompass His essence without means connected to Him, then with obstacles that afflict it, it must avoid them by assistance from helpers and protection for it from those opposites that are in enemies that prevent; so that it may witness by the right of perception upon knowledge by preventing remoteness and subtlety from working the true work. So likewise the intellect, for it is a created cause having a limit like other causes of perception; what afflicts others afflicts it, along with the obscurity and complexity of things, and its material is examination of causes, that being higher than what is heard from the speech of the wise, and the most worthy of them is he who has a proof for his wisdom. And there is no power except by God. Another aspect: God, exalted be His mention, made the cause by which every external thing is perceived, apart from the senses, two aspects: One is inference by what is witnessed, when the absent connects to the witnessed like the connection of smoke to fire, and the sunlight to it, and like the connection of the effect of an act to the agent such as writing and building and the like. And the second is report that informs of the state of that, like distant countries and changing states and occurring events; that is known to the rational, and by that is a person's knowledge of genera, differentiae, and species, and types of medicine, language, sciences of crafts, wars, and other things. Then we know command and prohibition, promise and threat, in that which is not sensible, its evidence having no way to perceive it except by report, and that is like the permissible and the prohibited, and that in which there is every thing of diverse states; so in such a case report must be followed, and in that is the affirmation of the necessity of messengerhood.
Source pages p. 782

Commentary
This passage explains why human intellects need messengers and identifies the two fundamental ways to know what is beyond sensory experience. The author begins by stating that after explaining the need of intellects for messengers, the principle (al-asl) consists of two aspects. The first aspect is that God created for every faculty of perception an instrument (āla) specific to it, but these instruments are limited: they are subject to obstacles (āfāt) that must be avoided with the help of assistants and protection from opposing forces. Similarly, the intellect (ʿaql) is a created cause (sabab makhluq) with a defined limit like other causes of perception; it suffers from obscurity and complexity of things, and its material is the examination of causes – this is higher than the mere sayings of philosophers, and the most deserving of them is one who has a proof (burhān) for his wisdom. The second aspect is that God made the cause by which everything external (outside the senses) is perceived into two types: (1) inferential reasoning (istidlāl) from the witnessed to the unseen when there is a connection, like smoke indicating fire, sunlight indicating the sun, or an effect indicating an agent; (2) report (khabar) that informs about unseen matters like distant lands or past events, which is known to rational people and is the basis for knowledge of genera, species, medicine, language, crafts, etc. Then the author applies this: matters of command, prohibition, promise, and threat that are not sensible have no way of being known except through report. That covers the permissible and prohibited and all diverse states; therefore, in such matters, one must accept report, and that necessitates belief in messengers (al-risāla). The passage thus establishes two epistemological foundations – inference and report – and argues that report is indispensable for religious knowledge, proving the necessity of messengers.

Key Terms
حاجة العقول للرسل
الإدراك
الاستدلال
الخبر
العقل
الآلة
السبب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The human intellect alone is sufficient to know all truths, including religious duties.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the intellect is limited and cannot grasp non-sensible matters like divine commands. These require report, which comes through messengers.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what are the two ways of knowing what is beyond sensory experience?
A) Inference (istidlal) and report (khabar)
B) Intuition and revelation
C) Reason and emotion
D) Senses and imagination

Key Takeaway
The human intellect is limited; knowledge of non-sensible matters requires both inference and, crucially, report from messengers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a moral dilemma, do you rely more on your own reasoning or on what Allah has reported through His messengers? What would Maturidi say about the limits of your intellect in that moment?
?
Think of a time you trusted a report from someone without verifying it. Now consider how much more you trust the report of the prophets. Are you living as if that report is absolutely certain? What would change in your life if you truly acted on it?
?
You know that Allah has sent messengers with commands and prohibitions. Yet, which command do you find hardest to obey because your intellect questions it? How does Maturidi's argument challenge you to submit to report over your own reasoning?

Even If Reason Suffices, Messengers Are a Divine Favor
Maturidi argues that sending messengers is an act of divine grace even if reason were self-sufficient.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the sending of messengers is an act of divine grace that goes beyond what reason alone requires.

Core Idea
Prophethood is an extra gift of divine generosity, not a mere necessity.

Imam Maturidi addresses a potential objection: if human reason can arrive at knowledge of God, then why send prophets? He responds by shifting the frame from necessity to grace. Even if reason were self-sufficient, sending messengers would still be fitting because God is known for His abundant generosity. In this passage, Maturidi draws an analogy to the many blessings in creation that exceed what is strictly needed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let the argument unfold naturally.
This passage is a flowing argument, not a list.
2
Identify the main claim
What is Maturidi's response to the objection that reason suffices? Notice the phrase 'من طريق الأفضال' (by way of bestowing favors).
3
Examine the examples of extra blessings
The passage lists several examples of God's generosity beyond the bare minimum: bodily features (ears, eyes), proofs of tawhid and prophethood, and fruits and pleasures. How do these support the main claim?
Think of these as analogies for sending messengers.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that sufficiency of reason negates the need for prophets. How does Maturidi's argument refute this?
The key is shifting from necessity to generosity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تم لو كان فى العقل الغنى عنه لجائز إرسال الرسل من طريق الأفضال؛ إذ الله موصوف معروف الإحسان، فيه تتقلب عباده، وما من نعمة الله تعالى إلا والله تعالى على عباده فضل زينهم وجمالهم، نحو الأذنين والعينين، وكل ذى عدد في الجسد، ثم في كثرة النعم، في كثرة ما أنشأ من دلائل التوحيد والرسالة وإن كان بدون ذلك كفاية، ثم بكثرة الفواكه والملاذ، وإن كان القليل من ذلك كافيا
Then even if the intellect were independent of it, it would be permissible to send messengers by way of bestowing favors; for Allah is described, known for beneficence, in which His servants are turned about. And there is no blessing from Allah except that Allah—may He be exalted—bestows upon His servants favor in their adornment and beauty, such as the ears and eyes and everything counted in the body. Then in the abundance of blessings, in the abundance of what He has created of proofs of tawhid and prophethood, even if without that there would be sufficiency. Then in the abundance of fruits and pleasures, even if a little of that would suffice.
Source pages p. 783

Commentary
This passage presents a supplementary argument: even if reason were completely self-sufficient for religious guidance, sending messengers is still permissible and actually commendable as a form of divine beneficence (ifḍāl). Maturidi points out that God is characterized by beneficence (iḥsān), and His blessings are abundant, often beyond necessity. He gives examples: bodily features like ears and eyes are additional gifts; likewise, the numerous proofs of tawhid and prophethood in creation are more than necessary. Similarly, God provides abundant fruits and pleasures, while a little would have sufficed. Thus, sending messengers fits the pattern of God's generosity, providing more than the bare minimum. This counters the objection that if reason suffices, messengers are unnecessary; Maturidi shows they are an extra favor.

Key Terms
الأفضال
الإحسان
دلائل التوحيد والرسالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the sufficiency of reason negates the need for prophets.
Correction: Maturidi shows that even if reason suffices, prophets are an additional grace because God's nature is to give abundantly beyond necessity.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why would God send messengers even if reason were sufficient?
A) Because reason is insufficient for most people.
B) Because it is a form of divine favor and generosity.
C) Because messengers correct reason's errors.
D) Because the proofs in creation require explanation.

Key Takeaway
God sends messengers as an extra grace, not because reason fails, because His way is to give more than enough.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Maturidi says God gives proofs of tawhid and prophethood abundantly even if less would suffice. Are you satisfied with a little proof, or do you seek the abundance He has placed in creation? What are you missing by not looking more closely at His signs?

Why Messengers Are Needed: Aiding Reason Against Desires
Maturidi argues that messengers facilitate reason and counteract desires.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to Imam Maturidi, reason alone is insufficient for guidance and why messengers are necessary as helpers, reminders, and facilitators.

Core Idea
Reason faces distractions and desires; messengers are the intellect's needed helpers.

Imam Maturidi addresses an important objection: if reason is sufficient, why are prophets needed? He argues that reason has practical limitations—preoccupations, desires, and lack of reminders. Thus, messengers provide facilitation, assistance, and warnings that stimulate the intellect. This lesson examines Maturidi's argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and the literal English translation. Pay attention to Maturidi's conditional opening: 'if the intellect were sufficient, it would block the limit…'.
Notice the rhetorical structure: he sets up a hypothetical, then shows its impracticality.
2
Identify the two main obstacles
Maturidi lists two key obstacles that prevent reason from being sufficient: preoccupations (ashghāl) of the intellects and desires (ahwā') of the souls that cover the intellects. Highlight these in the text.
These are not just minor distractions; they veil the intellect altogether.
3
Analyze the analogy with desire's helpers
Maturidi argues that since desire has helpers (hopes, lusts, devils), the intellect deserves helpers too. The most deserving are the messengers. Reflect on how this analogy strengthens his argument.
This is a powerful rhetorical move: you cannot reject helpers for reason while accepting helpers for desire.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، لو كان بالعقل كفاية فهو يسده الحد فى ذلك والتعاون بأنواع واستشارة أهل النظر فيما خص الله لهم وأزاح عنهم الإشكال، ثم الاجتهاد الوافر له يبذل فيه كل مجهود، فكان فى إرسال الرسل تيسير عليهم وتخفيف، وذلك من عظيم المنن، فكفران مثله يدل على حمق الرجل وجهله بالمنن حتى عدها بلاء. مع ما للعقول أشغال وللأنفس أهواء يستر العقول، فارسال الرسل معونه لهم وإرشاد، وذلك هو الذى جبلت العقول على حبه. مع ما فيه تذكير وتنبيه وتحذير لوجه التقصير، فيكون ذلك مما يحث على النظر ويدعو إلى الفكر واستعمال العقول. وذلك معروف فى جميع أمور الدنيا وسياسات الملك مع ما جعل الهوى منازعا له، وقد جعل للهوى أعوان من الأمانى والشهوات وشياطين مزينة لها، فكيف ينكر جعل أعوان للعقول، أحقهم بذلك الرسل.
And furthermore, if the intellect were sufficient, it would block the limit in that, and cooperation by types, and consultation of people of insight concerning what Allah has specially given them and removed from them difficulty. Then abundant exertion would expend every effort in it. So in sending messengers there is facilitation for them and lightening, and that is among the great favors. So ingratitude for such indicates the foolishness of the man and his ignorance of favors until he counted them as affliction. Besides, the intellects have preoccupations and the souls have desires that cover the intellects. So sending messengers is assistance for them and guidance, and that is what the intellects are disposed to love. Besides, in it is reminding, warning, and caution against shortcomings. That thus becomes something that urges reflection and calls to thought and use of intellects. And that is well-known in all worldly affairs and governance of kings, along with what desire has made a contender against it. And desire has been given helpers from hopes, lusts, and devils that embellish them, so how can one deny making helpers for the intellects? The most deserving of that are the messengers.
Source pages p. 784

Commentary
This passage argues that even if reason could theoretically attain guidance, it faces practical obstacles. Maturidi first suggests that if reason were completely sufficient, it would set a limit, requiring cooperation and consultation with experts—even in religious matters. However, sending messengers provides facilitation (taysīr) and lightening (takhfīf), which are great favors. Denying this favor indicates foolishness. He then notes that intellects have distractions (ashghāl) and souls have desires (ahwā') that veil the intellect. Therefore, messengers serve as helpers and guides for the intellects—a role that intellects naturally love. Moreover, messengers provide reminders, warnings, and caution against negligence, which stimulates reflection and intellectual effort. Maturidi draws an analogy to worldly governance: kings have assistants; desire itself has helpers (hopes, passions, devils). So why should the intellect not have helpers? The most deserving helpers are the prophets. This refutes the objection that reason alone suffices.

Key Terms
تيسير (facilitation)
تخفيف (lightening)
المنن (favors)
الهوى (desire)
المعونه (assistance)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That human reason is entirely self-sufficient for attaining guidance without any external or divine aid.
Correction: Maturidi shows that reason faces practical obstacles (preoccupations and desires) and needs prophetic assistance to overcome them.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does desire having helpers (hopes, lusts, devils) support the need for prophets?
A) Because prophets can eliminate desires.
B) Because the intellect needs helpers to counter desire's helpers.
C) Because desires are stronger than intellects.
D) Because prophets are also affected by desires.

Key Takeaway
Messengers are essential to aid reason against desires and provide reminders that stimulate reflection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where in your life do you rely solely on your own reasoning, ignoring the guidance of the messengers? What desires are covering your intellect right now, and what step will you take to invite prophetic guidance into that area before tonight?
?
Can you honestly say you love the assistance of the prophets as much as your soul loves its desires? What would change if you started seeing prophetic teaching as a lightening of your burden rather than an imposition?
?
The passage compares messengers to assistants of kings. Do you treat the Prophet's message (peace be upon him) as a mere accessory to your life or as the essential helper for your intellect? If the former, what ingratitude (kufrān) might you be guilty of, and how will you repent today?

Prophets as Sensory Witnesses to the Unseen
Maturidi explains why prophets' sensory presence makes the afterlife tangible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that sensory witnesses (prophets) are necessary to make the realities of the afterlife as compelling as sensory desires.

Core Idea
Prophets make the unseen afterlife tangible to overcome sensory desires.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi concludes his argument for the necessity of prophecy by confronting a fundamental human challenge: our desires are driven by sensory experience, but the motivations for obedience—reward, punishment, and the afterlife—are unseen. How can we be expected to follow what we cannot see, when what we see pulls us so strongly? Maturidi’s answer points to the role of prophets as sensory witnesses who make the unseen feel concrete.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Let the argument sink in first.
Notice the contrast Maturidi sets up between sensory desires and absent truth.
2
Identify the core contrast
Maturidi contrasts two things: all impulses of desire are sensory (mushāhadah ḥissiyyah), while the causes of acting upon truth are absent (ghā'ibah). This is the problem.
The 'causes of truth' refer to reminders of reward, punishment, and command to abandon pleasures.
3
Understand the role of prophets
Prophets function as 'those who remind of the afterlife.' By seeing and hearing them, the unseen afterlife becomes like direct perception (bi-ḥaqq al-ʿiyān), easing the struggle.
This transforms a difficult command into something that feels as natural as following one's nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإن جميع نيازع الهوى مشاهدة حسية، وجميع أسباب عمل الحق غائبة؛ إذ المذكر هو ذكر الثواب والعقاب والأمر بترك الشهوات والملاذ، وذلك أمر عسير على الطبع والهوى، فيحتاج فى ذلك إلى الإستعانة برؤية من تذكر المعاد ويخبرون عن المنقلب بما فيه من اليسر والعسر؛ ليصير ذلك بحق العيان، فيسهل على الطبع سهولة ما يوافق الطبع، والله الموفق
And furthermore, all the impulses of desire are sensory observations, and all the causes of acting according to truth are absent. For the reminder is the mention of reward and punishment and the command to abandon lusts and pleasures, and that is a difficult matter upon nature and desire. So one needs in that to seek help through seeing those who remind of the afterlife and inform about the return with what it contains of ease and hardship, so that that becomes a matter of direct perception, and thus becomes easy for nature with the ease of what suits nature. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 784

Commentary
This passage concludes the argument for the necessity of prophets by addressing the challenge of desire. Maturidi points out that all impulses of desire (nawāzi' al-hawā) are observable sensory experiences, while the motivations for truth (causes of acting rightly) are absent—that is, the rewards and punishments of the afterlife are unseen. The reminder of these unseen realities (reward, punishment, and the command to abandon pleasures) is inherently difficult for human nature (ṭab') and desire. Therefore, people need the assistance of seeing those who remind of the afterlife—i.e., the prophets—who actually inform about the return (al-munqalab) with its ease and hardship. By seeing the prophets and hearing them, the unseen becomes effectively like direct perception (bi-ḥaqq al-ʿiyān), making it easy for human nature to follow, as easy as things that naturally suit it. Thus, prophets make the difficult path of obedience more manageable by providing sensory witnesses to the unseen.

Key Terms
نيازع الهوى (impulses of desire)
المعاد (the afterlife)
المنقلب (the return)
حق العيان (certainty of direct perception)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Faith in the unseen is easy without external aids; humans can overcome desires by mere reasoning.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the seen world's pull is so strong that sensory witnesses (prophets) are needed to make the unseen feel real and thus ease obedience.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say that seeing prophets helps people obey commands that are difficult for their nature?
A) Prophets make the unseen afterlife sensory by their presence and testimony.
B) Prophets physically force people to obey.
C) Prophets remove all desires from people.
D) Prophets promise worldly pleasures in exchange for obedience.

Key Takeaway
Prophets make the unseen afterlife effectively sensory, enabling humans to overcome desires and obey with ease.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe in the afterlife, yet your daily choices often favor immediate desires. If the prophets—whom you have not seen—are meant to make that unseen world real for you, how are you actively using their example to shift your desires? What concrete step will you take today to treat the afterlife as a present reality?
?
When you find obedience difficult, do you seek out those who remind you of Allah and the return? Reflect: are the people you surround yourself with making the unseen tangible, or are they strengthening your attachment to sensory desires? Name one relationship you need to adjust or prioritize.
?
Maturidi says prophets make the unseen like direct perception. In your own life, where are you failing to be that 'sensory witness' to others? When do your words and actions obscure rather than clarify the reality of the afterlife? Commit to one change today that will make the unseen more real for someone else.

Reason's Sufficiency and Prophetic Facilitation
Maturidi states the principle of reason's sufficiency and the beneficial role of prophets as facilitators.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that although reason is sufficient in principle, sending prophets is a great favor that facilitates human guidance and that ingratitude for this favor indicates foolishness.

Core Idea
Prophets are not a replacement for reason but a support for it.

Maturidi begins by acknowledging a common objection: if reason is enough, why send prophets? He argues that prophets bring facilitation and lighten the burden, making them a great favor. Ingratitude for this favor indicates foolishness and ignorance of divine favors.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening argument
Read the first part of the passage where Maturidi states that if reason were sufficient, it would still need cooperation and effort, but prophets bring facilitation.
Notice that Maturidi does not deny reason's sufficiency; he adds to it.
2
Identify the benefit of prophets
The key benefit mentioned is 'taysir' (facilitation) and 'takhfif' (lightening). Understand that prophets make the path easier, not harder.
3
Reflect on ingratitude
Maturidi says that ingratitude for this favor indicates foolishness. Consider why counting such a favor as a burden would be ignorance.
This connects to the broader theme of recognizing divine blessings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، لو كان بالعقل كفاية فهو يسده الحد فى ذلك والتعاون بأنواع واستشارة أهل النظر فيما خص الله لهم وأزاح عنهم الإشكال، ثم الاجتهاد الوافر له يبذل فيه كل مجهود، فكان فى إرسال الرسل تيسير عليهم وتخفيف، وذلك من عظيم المنن، فكفران مثله يدل على حمق الرجل وجهله بالمنن حتى عدها بلاء. مع ما للعقول أشغال وللأنفس أهواء تستر العقول، فارسال الرسل معونة لهم وإرشاد، وذلك هو الذى جبلت العقول على حبه . مع ما فيه تذكير وتنبيه وتحذير لوجه التقصير، فيكون ذلك مما يحث على النظر ويدعو إلى الفكر واستعمال العقول . وذلك معروف فى جميع أمور الدنيا وسياسات الملك مع ما جعل الهوى منازعا له، وقد جعل للهوى أعوان من الأمانى والشهوات وشياطين مزينة لها، فكيف ينكر جعل أعوان للعقول، أحقهم بذلك الرسل . وبعد، فإن جميع نيازع الهوى مشاهدة حسية، وجميع أسباب عمل الحق غائبة؛ إذ المذكر هو ذكر الثواب والعقاب والأمر بترك الشهوات والملاذ، وذلك أمر عسير على الطبع والهوى، فيحتاج فى ذلك إلى الإستعانة برؤية من تذكر المعاد ويخبرون عن المنقلب بما فيه من اليسر والعسر؛ ليصير ذلك بحق العيان، فيسهل على الطبع سهولة ما يوافق الطبع، والله الموفق .
And thereafter, if reason were sufficient, it would suffice for the limit in that, and cooperation of various kinds and consultation of the people of reflection in what God has specially given them and removed from them difficulty, then abundant effort for which one expends all exertion. So in sending messengers there was facilitation and lightening upon them, and that is among the great favors. So ingratitude for such indicates the foolishness of the man and his ignorance of favors, until he counted them as affliction. Besides, the intellects have preoccupations and the souls have desires that veil the intellects. So sending messengers is a help for them and guidance, and that is what the intellects are naturally disposed to love. Besides, in it there is reminder, warning, and caution for the aspect of shortcoming, so that becomes something that urges reflection and calls to thought and the use of intellects. And that is known in all affairs of the world and the policies of kings, despite desire being made to oppose it. And desire has been given helpers from wishes and lusts and devils that embellish them. So how can one deny the appointment of helpers for the intellects, the most deserving of whom are the messengers? And thereafter, all the conflicts of desire are sensibly witnessed, and all causes of acting upon truth are hidden. For the reminder is the mention of reward and punishment and the command to abandon lusts and pleasures, and that is a difficult matter upon nature and desire. So in that there is need for assistance from seeing those who remind of the return and inform about the final destination with what it contains of ease and difficulty, so that it becomes like the truth of direct sight, and thus becomes easy upon nature with the ease of what is compatible with nature. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 784

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument for the necessity of prophets despite the sufficiency of reason. Maturidi begins by acknowledging that reason could be sufficient in principle, but then argues that in practice, reason has obstacles: desires veil it. Prophets are sent as a mercy to help people overcome these obstacles. He emphasizes that prophets provide reminder, warning, and encouragement to use reason. He compares this to worldly affairs where helpers are needed. He then notes that the motives of desire are visible, while the motives for truth are hidden; prophets make the unseen reward and punishment tangible through reminder, making obedience easier. The passage is a continuous explanatory argument defending the wisdom of sending prophets.

Key Terms
العقل
الرسل
المنن
التيسير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That prophets are needed because reason is completely insufficient for knowing good and evil.
Correction: Maturidi argues that reason is sufficient in principle, but prophets facilitate and lighten the path; they are a support, not a replacement.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi in this passage, what is the primary benefit of sending prophets?
A) They replace reason as the source of guidance.
B) They provide facilitation and lightening of the effort.
C) They prove that reason is defective.
D) They impose burdens on people.

Key Takeaway
Prophets are sent to facilitate guidance, not to replace reason, and ingratitude for this favor is foolish.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as though the prophet's message is a convenience that lightens your burden, or do you secretly view it as an extra set of restrictions? What would change in your daily choices if you truly believed it was a facilitation?
?
When you face confusion or difficulty in religious matters, do you first turn to your own intellect or to the guidance of the prophet? How does that preference reveal what you actually believe about the sufficiency of reason?
?
Maturidi says ingratitude for the favor of prophethood is foolishness. Where in your life have you counted prophetic guidance as a burden rather than a blessing? What step can you take this week to consciously treat it as a facilitation?

Desires Veil Reason: Prophets as Helpers and Reminders
Maturidi explains the obstacles of desires and the necessity of prophets as helpers and reminders.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that desires and preoccupations veil the intellect, and prophets are needed as helpers, guides, and reminders to make the unseen tangible, thereby easing obedience.

Core Idea
Desires blind reason; prophets open our eyes.

Maturidi now turns to the practical obstacles: intellects are busy and souls have desires that veil the intellect. Prophets are sent as helpers and guides, providing reminders of reward and punishment. He draws an analogy with worldly affairs where helpers are needed, and argues that since desire has its helpers, intellects also deserve helpers—the prophets.

Guided Path
1
Identify the obstacles
Read the part about preoccupations (ashghal) of the intellects and desires (ahwa') of the souls that veil the intellect. Understand why reason alone may fail in practice.
This sets up the problem that prophets solve.
2
Understand the role of prophets as helpers
Prophets are called 'a'wan' (helpers) and 'irshad' (guidance). They support the intellect against desires.
3
Analyze the analogy with worldly affairs
Maturidi compares the need for prophets to the need for helpers in worldly governance. Since desire has helpers (wishes, lusts, devils), intellects must have helpers too.
This refutes the objection that intellect alone should suffice.
4
Grasp the function of reminders
Prophets remind of reward and punishment, making the unseen feel present (like direct sight). This eases obedience despite natural difficulty.
This is the final, most practical point.

The Inability of Deniers to Refute Prophets' Proofs
Maturidi argues that deniers cannot refute prophets' proofs, and uses the need for law to reinforce prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the prophets' manifest victory despite all opposition demonstrates divine support, and how this is linked to the universal need for divine law.

Core Idea
Prophets' manifest victory proves their truth despite all denier efforts.

This passage presents a powerful historical argument. Maturidi observes that despite the deniers' relentless efforts—using tricks, accusations of magic, and expending their resources to extinguish the prophets' message—the prophets always emerged victorious. This undeniable victory points to divine support. Then Maturidi extends the argument to show that communities cannot function without a divine law, which the prophets bring.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation to grasp the overall flow and key points.
Pay attention to the sequence: deniers' failure, then the need for law.
2
Identify the two main arguments
Notice that Maturidi makes two connected points: (1) Despite all efforts, deniers could not overcome the prophets; (2) Just as earthly kings need laws, communities need divine law—implying prophethood is necessary.
The second point is an analogy that reinforces the first.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this argument is powerful: it uses observable history (prophets' victory) to prove divine support, and then links it to a universal human need (law) to argue for the necessity of prophethood.
This is a characteristic Maturidi approach: combining evidence and reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعلم جميع منكرى الرسل أن ليس مع نفسه صفة المتعنتين، مع كثرة حيلهم في مقابلات أدلتهم، وطعنها مرة بالسحر وبوجوه [أخرى]، ثم مع بذلهم مجهودهم من دنياهم في إطفاء نورهم، فلم يروا غير الظهور والغلبة، حتى أحوج الله جميع الأنام إلى الذين يؤمنون بالرسل على تعرفهم بما علموا في الجملة أن لهم في أمورهم غنى رجاء أن يصلح أمرهم فيتفق كلمتهم، وعلى ذلك سياسات ملوك الدنيا ثم لا يقوم رعية لا تجول فيهم شريعة يلزمون القيام بها وأسا[سا] يبنون عليه، ولا بد لأمثال ذلك من تدبير ممن يعلم أنه إذا خلقهم جعل لهم وجها يصلحون عليه ولا قوة إلا بالله .
All deniers of the messengers know that they do not possess the attribute of the stubborn? Despite the abundance of their tricks in confronting their proofs, and their attacking them at times with magic and with other faces, then despite their expending their utmost effort from their worldly goods in extinguishing their light, they saw nothing but manifest victory, until God made all people need those who believe in the messengers, recognizing by what they knew in general that they have sufficiency in their affairs, hoping that their condition would be set right and their word become united. And upon that are the policies of the kings of the world. Then no community can stand that has no law circulating among them that they are obliged to uphold and a foundation upon which they build. And there is no alternative for such except management from one who knows that when He created them, He made for them a way by which they can be set right. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 785

Commentary
This passage presents an argument from the historical fact that despite the intense opposition of the deniers of prophets—using various tricks, accusations of magic, and expending great effort to extinguish the prophets' message—they never succeeded in refuting the clear proofs. Instead, the prophets always emerged victorious. This victory is a sign of divine support. Then Maturidi draws an analogy: just as worldly kings rely on laws and foundations for their policies, so too must communities have a divine law (shari'a) and a foundation. This implies that the prophets' success and the universal need for law point to the truth of their mission.

Key Terms
منكرى الرسل
أدلة
الظهور والغلبة
شريعة
سياسات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophets were simply lucky or persuasive.
Correction: Maturidi argues that their victory was divinely ordained and undeniable, as even the most determined enemies could not refute their proofs.

Checkpoint

A) The prophets compromising.
B) Only manifest victory and dominance of the prophets.
C) The prophets abandoning their mission.
D) The prophets resorting to magic.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the prophets' undeniable victory and the universal need for law prove the truth of their mission.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The passage ends with 'there is no power except through God.' In what area of your life are you relying on your own strength rather than divine power to uphold the truth? What would change if you truly internalized this?

The Failure of Deniers of Prophethood
Maturidi presents the failure of deniers as proof for prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses the failure of deniers to disprove their objections and establish the truth of prophethood.

Core Idea
Deniers exhaust every trick but only see prophets victorious—that is proof.

The passage opens with a striking observation: despite all their efforts, those who reject prophets never succeed in refuting them. Maturidi turns this failure into a rational proof for the necessity of believing in prophets. Let's read how he builds this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and notice the deniers' efforts
Pay attention to what the deniers do: they use tricks, call prophets sorcerers, and spend worldly means to extinguish their light. List these attempts.
2
Identify the outcome they fail to achieve
Despite all this, they see nothing but 'manifestation and victory' for the prophets. This is the key point: defeat is not theirs.
The phrase 'فلم يروا غير الظهور والغلبة' is crucial.
3
Understand the logical conclusion
Their failure and the prophets' success show that God makes people in need of those who believe in prophets. This is a divine arrangement.
4
Reflect on how this serves as evidence for prophethood
Ask yourself: If deniers had a valid argument, wouldn't they have succeeded? Their inability to disprove prophets indicates that prophethood is true.
This is a 'proof from failure'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعلم جميع منكرى الرسل أن ليس مع نفسه صفة المتعنتين، مع كثرة حيلهم في مقابلات أدلتهم، وطعنها مرة بالسحر وبوجوه أخرى، ثم مع بذلهم مجهودهم من دنياهم في إطفاء نورهم، فلم يروا غير الظهور والغلبة، حتى أحوج الله جميع الأنام إلى الذين يؤمنون بالرسل على تعرفهم بما علموا في الجملة أن لهم في أمورهم غنى رجاء أن يصلح أمرهم فيتفق كلمتهم، وعلى ذلك سياسات ملوك الدنيا ثم لا يقوم رعية لا تجول فيهم شريعة يلزمون القيام بها وأساسا يبنون عليه، ولا بد لأمثال ذلك من تدبير ممن يعلم أنه إذا خلقهم جعل لهم وجها يصلحون عليه ولا قوة إلا بالله.
All deniers of the messengers know that they do not have with themselves the attribute of the obstinate ones, despite the abundance of their tricks in opposing their proofs, and attacking them sometimes with sorcery and with other forms, then despite their utmost efforts from their worldly means in extinguishing their light, they saw nothing but manifestation and victory, until God made all people in need of those who believe in the messengers, upon their recognition by what they knew in summary that they have sufficiency in their affairs, hoping that their matter will be corrected and their word will be unified. And upon that are the policies of the kings of the world, and no subjects can stand without a law flowing among them that they are obliged to uphold and a foundation upon which they build. And such matters require management from one who knows that when He created them, He made for them a way upon which they can be corrected. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 785

Commentary
This passage argues for the necessity of prophethood by observing that deniers of prophets, despite their efforts to refute them, fail and see only the prophets' victory. This shows that God has made people dependent on those who believe in prophets. The passage then draws an analogy: just as worldly kings need laws to govern subjects, so humanity needs divine law from prophets. The conclusion is that such governance requires a Creator who knows how to order human affairs.

Key Terms
منكرى الرسل
المتعنتين
الظهور والغلبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think that deniers of prophets have rational arguments or that prophethood is uncertain. The passage shows they have no valid argument; their failure is evidence.
Correction: The deniers' inability to refute prophets, despite using every trick, demonstrates that prophethood is true and divinely supported.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do the deniers see despite their efforts?
A) Their own success in refuting prophets
B) Only manifestation and victory (of prophets)
C) A new religion
D) Peace and harmony

Key Takeaway
Deniers of prophets, despite all their efforts, fail to disprove them, which itself shows that prophethood is true.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The deniers used every trick and resource to oppose the prophets, yet they witnessed only victory. In what areas of your life have you been like the deniers—resisting a truth with cleverness or effort? What outcome did you see, and what does that reveal about your heart? Will you continue to resist, or will you submit?
?
You know that the deniers' failure is evidence for prophethood. But have you ever used the same excuses—'it's just magic' or 'it's just a story'—to avoid obeying a clear command from Allah? What is it in your life that you are trying to extinguish? Are you willing to let that light shine instead?
?
The deniers saw only the prophets' 'zuhoor wa ghalaba'. Think of a time when you tried to ignore or fight a truth that was evident. Did you end up seeing it prevail anyway? If so, what does that tell you about your own arguments against Allah's guidance? Will you now surrender to that truth today?
