The Necessity of Divine Law for Society
Maturidi argues for the social necessity of prophethood through an analogy with kingship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that human societies require divine law (shari'ah) from prophets, and that this law must be established by the Creator.

Core Idea
Just as kings need laws to rule, humanity needs prophets for order.

After establishing the failure of deniers, Maturidi shifts to a sociological argument: society cannot function without a law that people are obliged to follow. He draws an analogy with earthly kings to show that prophecy brings the necessary divine legislation. Let's examine this reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note God's action
The passage begins by stating that God made all people in need of believers in prophets. This is a divine decree that shows the necessity of prophethood.
2
Understand the analogy with kings
Maturidi compares this to the policies of worldly kings: they need laws for their subjects. Similarly, a community cannot stand without a 'shari'ah' (law) that they must uphold.
The word 'شريعة' here means divine law.
3
Identify the conclusion about the Creator
Such management requires a Creator who knows that when He created humans, He gave them a way to be corrected. The phrase 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' underscores reliance on God.
4
Reflect on why this proves prophethood's necessity
If society needs a law, and only God can provide the right law through prophets, then prophethood is essential for social order.
This is a teleological argument: purpose requires a guide.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

All deniers of the messengers know that they do not have with themselves the attribute of the obstinate ones, despite the abundance of their tricks in opposing their proofs, and attacking them sometimes with sorcery and with other forms, then despite their utmost efforts from their worldly means in extinguishing their light, they saw nothing but manifestation and victory, until God made all people in need of those who believe in the messengers, upon their recognition by what they knew in summary that they have sufficiency in their affairs, hoping that their matter will be corrected and their word will be unified. Correction: Prophethood provides the divine law necessary for society to function, just as earthly laws are needed for subjects.

A) The need for water

Human society cannot function without a divine law (shari'ah) provided by prophets, and this law reflects the wisdom of the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a specific rule or custom you follow that came from human culture rather than the shari'ah. In what area of your life have you trusted human wisdom over divine guidance? What concrete change can you make this week to bring that area under the foundation of prophetic law?
?
The passage says every community needs a shari'ah and a foundation. Look at the community of believers you are part of—your family, mosque, or circle. Where have you allowed that community to drift from the prophetic foundation? What is one practical step you can take to reinforce it?
?
Maturidi ends with 'ولا قوة إلا بالله'. Consider an effort you are making to live by divine law—where are you relying on your own strength? How can you turn that dependence over to Allah today?

Refuting al-Warraq's Skepticism on Miracles
Maturidi presents and refutes al-Warraq's skepticism about prophetic miracles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's multi-layered refutation of al-Warraq's claim that prophetic miracles are indistinguishable from magic or tricks.

Core Idea
Miracles are unique divine signs, not human tricks.

This passage presents a powerful objection by al-Warraq that challenges the distinctiveness of miracles, comparing them to magic and tricks. Maturidi counters with a reasoned argument that miracles are unique to prophets and beyond human imitation. This lesson unpacks the objection and the refutation step by step, showing how the claims of prophets are validated through both rational and historical evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it, then read the translation. Note the flow from objection to response.
The passage is dense; take your time to grasp the structure.
2
Identify al-Warraq's objection
Al-Warraq argues that prophets did not fully understand natural laws, so their signs could be tricks or coincidences. He uses the magnet stone as an example of a natural phenomenon mistaken for wonder.
3
Follow Maturidi's first reply
Maturidi challenges al-Warraq's own knowledge and asserts that whatever the prophets say has a valid answer. He then argues that if miracles were like ordinary natural events, they would not be unique signs.
The first reply is a rhetorical counter-question and a logical refutation.
4
Examine the magnet stone argument
The magnet stone's attraction is a specific property; miracles are similarly specific to prophets and indicate divine origin. If it were mere trickery, it would not be tied to prophethood.
5
Understand the testimony argument
Maturidi points out that every true prophet had witnesses who attested to his truthfulness, and that accepting news from others is a daily practice. The denier is inconsistent if he accepts news but rejects prophethood.
This final part uses common human experience to argue for consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر طرفا مما ذكره الوراق، فقال: فما جاء به الرسل من الآيات المعجزات التي بمثلها يثبت القول بالتوحيد: إنهم لم يمتحنوا قوى الخلق، ولا وقفوا على طبائع العالم التي يستعان بها في الأفعال، بل لم تبلغ علم أكثرهم، فكيف يعرفون بذلك مبالغ الحيل؟ وهل الذي رأوا إلا كلعب أتى العجب؟ وهل حدث السحر إلا لجذب حجر المغناطيس الحديد؟ فيقال له: أبلغت أنت الذي ذكرت لتعلم أنت الذي قلت طعن أو تمويه؟ فمهما قالوا من شيء فهو له جواب في الأول، وجواب آخر: إنه لو كان في جوهر العالم الذي ذكر، لم يحتمل ظهور ما ذكر من الحجر؛ لأن الخاص إنما يحفظ باسمه لما يخرج من الاحتمال لبعد في الآيات، وتخصيص ذلك من جوهره في الأعجوبة، فأوجب ذلك أمرا ما جاء به الرسل خصوصا لهم ليكون لهم [؟] إنه في الخروج عن جوهره بالذي يدعى، مع ما بينا فيما تقدم أنه نشأ بين قوم على طبع، علموا أن مثله لا يحتمل ذلك لجوهر بشر بالذي جاء به جوهر. وبعد: فقد كثر عنهم الآيات من أنواع ما لا يحتمل ذلك بالاطلاع على الأرض إلا أن يطلعه من علم جواهرها، وفي ذلك الذي ذكر. على أنه ما من نبي صحت نبوته إلا وقد شهد قومه منه من إعلام الصدق ما يجب قبول قوله لولا الآيات كلف دليلا. ثم يقال: أنت ممن تقبل خبرا في الدنيا؟ فإن قال: نعم، على صدقه أوضح من أدلة الرسل، وفي ذلك وجوب القول بالذي ذكرت، وإن قال: لا، يشهد عليه العقل وكل شيء جعله حجة بالكذب.
Then we mention a part of what al-Warraq mentioned, so he said: 'What the messengers brought of miraculous signs by which the statement of tawhid is established: They did not test the capabilities of creation, nor did they come to know the natures of the world that are used in actions; rather, the knowledge of most of them did not reach that, so how would they know by that the extent of tricks? And what they saw is nothing but a play that brought wonder? And did sorcery happen except for the attraction of the magnet stone to iron?' It is said to him: 'Have you reached what you mentioned so that you know whether you have spoken criticism or obfuscation? Whatever they say of something, it has an answer in the first place, and another answer: If it were in the substance of the world that he mentioned, he would not bear the manifestation of what was mentioned of the stone; because the specific is preserved by its name for what goes out of possibility due to distance in the signs, and the specification of that from its substance in the miracle, so that necessitated a matter that the messengers brought specifically for them so that for them [?] it is in the departure from its substance by what is claimed, with what we showed previously that he arose among a people upon a nature, they knew that the like of him cannot bear that for the substance of a human with what he brought of substance. And after: many signs have come from them of types that cannot bear that by coming to know the earth except that He informs him who knows its substances, and in that which he mentioned. And moreover, there is no prophet whose prophethood is valid except that his people witnessed from him of the declaration of truthfulness that necessitates acceptance of his statement, if not for the signs, he would have been required to bring a proof. Then it is said: Are you among those who accept news in this world? If he says: Yes, then the truthfulness of the prophets is more evident than the proofs of the messengers, and in that is the obligation of the statement that I mentioned. And if he says: No, then the intellect and everything that made it a proof bear witness against him by lying.'
Source pages p. 785

Commentary
This passage presents al-Warraq's objection that prophets' miracles are like magic or tricks, and people cannot judge whether they are genuine because they lack full knowledge of nature. The refutation has multiple parts: (1) The prophets' signs are unique and cannot be replicated by human tricks. (2) The miracles are tied to specific prophets and their substances, proving divine origin. (3) Throughout history, every true prophet has been supported by clear signs that compel acceptance. (4) Finally, the denier himself accepts news from others in daily life, so he should accept the prophets' testimony, which is even more evident. The passage thus uses a logical argument to show consistency in accepting testimony.

Key Terms
الوراق
المعجزات
طبائع العالم
حجر المغناطيس
السحر
التوحيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles are indistinguishable from sorcery or natural tricks.
Correction: Maturidi argues that miracles are uniquely tied to prophets, beyond human capacity, and are confirmed by historical testimony and logical consistency.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's refutation, what makes prophetic miracles distinct from mere tricks or sorcery?
A) They are performed by prophets on a large scale
B) They are unique signs that cannot be replicated by human means and are tied to the substance of the prophet
C) They are always accompanied by loud sounds
D) They are dependent on the prophets' knowledge of nature

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes al-Warraq by showing that prophetic miracles are unique signs from God, substantiated by historical testimony and logical consistency in accepting testimony.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a clear sign from God in your life as 'just coincidence' or 'natural law'? What would it take for you to recognize a miracle, or do you think you already have and ignored it?
?
You trust news from people every day. The passage says the testimony of prophets is even more evident. When you read about the miracles of the Prophet, do you accept them with that same trust, or do you hold a different standard? Why?
?
Maturidi questions al-Warraq's own investigation: 'Have you reached what you mentioned?' Think about your own doubts in matters of faith: have you genuinely examined the evidence for prophethood, or are you dismissing it from a place of ignorance? What will you do to investigate honestly?

Refutation of Ibn al-Rawandi's Objection to Supernatural Claims
Maturidi refutes Ibn al-Rawandi's argument against miracles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes Ibn al-Rawandi's argument that miracles should be rejected because they violate known natural laws, and why clear proof makes acceptance obligatory.

Core Idea
Miracles are not rejected by possibility but accepted by clear proof.

Ibn al-Rawandi argued that any claim of supernatural acts should be dismissed because they depart from known natures. Imam Maturidi responds by showing that the same reasoning would apply to prophets, but the crucial difference is that prophets bring clear proof, making acceptance necessary. In this passage, Maturidi lays out this refutation concisely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note Ibn al-Rawandi's examples and Maturidi's response.
Pay attention to the structure: objection first, then rebuttal.
2
Identify the objection and the response
Ibn al-Rawandi lists hypothetical supernatural claims. Maturidi points out that the denier has no evidence, while the claimant has apparent proof.
3
Reflect on the key difference
The denier relies on conjectures and possibilities, while the prophet's proof is clear. Understand why this shifts the burden of proof.
This is the core of Maturidi's argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعارضه ابن الروندي: إن أحدا لو ادعى طبيعة يحدث بها الكواكب أو لو نصبه مقابل الشمس يذهب ضوؤها، أو إنه إذا مس البحر لفظ البحر ما فيه، وإذا مسح به قدمه لصار في الهواء وارتفع إلى السماء ويصير سحابا يمطر، فإذا لزم تكذيب بما ادعى الخروج عن طبائع معروفة فمثله الأول، مع ما كان المكذب ليس معه شيء، ومع الآخر شيء بالظنون يرد وبالاحتمال، وما به قد يمكن عيب، والحجة ظاهرة، فلزم القول به.
And Ibn al-Rawandi objected: If someone claimed a nature by which he creates the stars, or if he stood facing the sun and its light disappeared, or if he touched the sea and the sea disgorged what was in it, and if he wiped his foot and became in the air and rose to the sky and became a cloud that rains, then it would be necessary to disbelieve him for claiming departure from known natures, and the first would be similar to that, while the denier has nothing with him, and with the other there is something that is rejected by conjectures and by possibility, and what could be criticized, and the proof is apparent, so it is necessary to accept it.
Source pages p. 786

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn al-Rawandi's objection: if someone claims to perform extraordinary feats like moving stars or changing natural phenomena, they should be rejected because they violate known natural laws. The author's response is that this reasoning would also apply to the prophets, but the key difference is that the denier has no proof against the prophet, while the prophet has clear proof. Therefore, acceptance of the prophet's claim is necessary, as the evidence is manifest.

Key Terms
ابن الروندي
طبائع معروفة
الظنون
الحجة ظاهرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any claim of supernatural acts must be automatically rejected.
Correction: The passage clarifies that when supported by clear proof, acceptance is obligatory.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the outcome for the claimant of supernatural acts if the proof is apparent?
A) He should be rejected
B) It is necessary to accept his claim
C) He must be considered a liar
D) He should be tested further

Key Takeaway
Miracles are not rejected simply because they break natural laws; they are accepted when accompanied by clear proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a claim from Allah that seems too extraordinary—like a miracle or a divine promise—do you instinctively demand proof only from the claimant while ignoring your own lack of evidence for rejection? How does this lesson expose that unfair standard in your own heart?
?
Think of a clear, undeniable truth from the Qur'an or Sunnah that you have repeatedly pushed aside with weak excuses like 'it might not apply' or 'scholars differ.' Are you treating that truth the way Ibn al-Rawandi treated miracles—rejecting it with mere conjecture instead of submitting to the clear proof? What will you do about it today?
?
The passage ends with 'falazima al-qawl bihi'—it became necessary to accept. Is there an article of faith or a duty that you know is proven but have been postponing out of comfort? What is the one step you can take right now to move from knowing to accepting?

The Proof from Universal Consensus on Death
Maturidi argues from consensus on death for prophethood and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the universal consensus on death to argue for the truth of prophethood, and how he refutes al-Warraq's objection.

Core Idea
Consensus on death proves prophethood, just as it proves the universality of death.

Maturidi here presents a clever argument from consensus: all humans agree that all people die, even though no one has witnessed every death. Similarly, the truth of prophethood can be established through consensus, not direct witness. He then addresses objections from those who demand direct observation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to how Maturidi structures his argument.
2
Identify the consensus argument
Find how Maturidi uses the consensus on death as an analogy for accepting prophethood. Note the key phrase 'فيه الإجماع'.
3
Examine the three responses
Maturidi gives three responses to al-Warraq's objection. List each one and understand how they counter the demand for direct witness.
The responses are numbered in the commentary.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why consensus is a valid source of knowledge in theology. How does this argument strengthen the case for prophethood?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج على الوراق بما أجمع على موت البشر كلهم وإن لم يشهدوا الكل بالرسل، فقال: فيه الإجماع. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: وقد علم أنه لم يشهد، بل لم يبلغ علمه شيء، والثاني أنه علق دليله في ذلك بالمحنة، وقد زال، والثالث إذ لا يبلغ التدبير ثبت أنه قيل بالرسل. وقال في قول الفلسفة: إن تركيب الحيوان تركيب يموت. تأملوا حماقته، بعد قول قوم لو أدركوه لأدركوه بالرسل، ثم ينكر قول الرسل مع البرهان. يمتحن عقول جميع الفلاسفة، ولا امتحنوا طبائع الجميع. والثالث أنه لو كان بالتركيب لما اختلف قدر الحياة. وقال بالطباع إن النفس لا تطمع [؟] لم هم.
And he argued against al-Warraq by what is unanimously agreed upon: the death of all humans, even if they did not witness all of them and the messengers. He said: There is consensus. Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: He knew that he did not witness, rather his knowledge did not reach anything, and second: he attached his proof in that to a test, which has ceased, and third: since management does not reach, it is proven that it was said by the messengers. And he said concerning the statement of the philosophers: The composition of the animal is a composition that dies. Reflect on his foolishness, after the statement of a people: if they had realized him, they would have realized him through the messengers, then he denies the statement of the messengers with the proof. He tests the intellects of all philosophers, and they did not test the natures of all. And third: if it were by composition, the measure of life would not differ. And he said by the nature that the soul does not desire [?] they did not.
Source pages p. 786

Commentary
This passage contains two distinct refutations. First, the author argues from consensus: just as all humans agree that all people die, even without witnessing every death, so too should they accept the truth of prophethood. The objection that one must witness to know is refuted because knowledge can be attained through consensus. Second, the author ridicules the philosophers' argument that animal life ends due to composition, pointing out that if life span were determined by composition alone, it would not vary. The philosophers claim to test all intellects but have not tested all natures, exposing their inconsistency.

Key Terms
الإجماع
الرسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Direct observation is necessary for all knowledge.
Correction: Consensus is a valid source of knowledge, as demonstrated by the universal agreement on death.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi use to argue for prophethood?
A) Direct observation
B) Consensus on death
C) Philosophical reasoning
D) Personal experience

Key Takeaway
Consensus, not direct observation, can serve as a proof for fundamental truths like death and prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you accept the consensus of the Muslim community on matters of faith that you have not personally verified? What does this lesson teach you about the reliability of such collective knowledge? Will you now trust more fully in what the community has agreed upon, even when you haven't seen it firsthand?
?
If you were to reject prophethood because you haven't witnessed every miracle or every messenger, what other widely accepted truths—like death itself—would you also be forced to reject? Are you willing to follow that skepticism to its logical end?
?
Al-Warraq's objection relied on a test that has ceased. What tests are you imposing on God's guidance—demanding a sign or proof beyond what has been established—that are similar? Will you surrender those conditions and accept the consensus that has already been given?

Refuting the Philosophers' Argument on Death by Composition
Maturidi refutes philosophers' claim that death is due to animal composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the philosophical claim that death results from the composition of animals, and why this argument fails.

Core Idea
Death cannot be solely due to composition, as life span varies even among similar compositions.

Maturidi turns to refute a philosophical argument that death is inevitable because animals are composed of elements that disintegrate. He points out the inconsistency in their reasoning and highlights that if composition were the sole cause, all living beings would have the same lifespan.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Note the strong language used to criticize the philosophers.
The final part about the soul is unclear due to OCR issues.
2
Identify the philosophers' claim
Find the statement that summarizes the philosophers' view: 'إن تركيب الحيوان تركيب يموت'. Understand what they mean.
3
Understand Maturidi's three counterpoints
Maturidi gives three points against the philosophers. List each one and see how they expose flaws in the philosophical argument.
The third point is the clearest: variation in lifespan.
4
Reflect on the logical flaws
Consider why the philosophers' argument is inconsistent. How does Maturidi's refutation protect the doctrine of prophethood?
This connects to the overall theme of defending revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج على الوراق بما أجمع على موت البشر كلهم وإن لم يشهدوا الكل بالرسل، فقال: فيه الإجماع. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: وقد علم أنه لم يشهد، بل لم يبلغ علمه شيء، والثاني أنه علق دليله في ذلك بالمحنة، وقد زال، والثالث إذ لا يبلغ التدبير ثبت أنه قيل بالرسل. وقال في قول الفلسفة: إن تركيب الحيوان تركيب يموت. تأملوا حماقته، بعد قول قوم لو أدركوه لأدركوه بالرسل، ثم ينكر قول الرسل مع البرهان. يمتحن عقول جميع الفلاسفة، ولا امتحنوا طبائع الجميع. والثالث أنه لو كان بالتركيب لما اختلف قدر الحياة. وقال بالطباع إن النفس لا تطمع [؟] لم هم.
And he argued against al-Warraq by what is unanimously agreed upon: the death of all humans, even if they did not witness all of them and the messengers. He said: There is consensus. Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: He knew that he did not witness, rather his knowledge did not reach anything, and second: he attached his proof in that to a test, which has ceased, and third: since management does not reach, it is proven that it was said by the messengers. And he said concerning the statement of the philosophers: The composition of the animal is a composition that dies. Reflect on his foolishness, after the statement of a people: if they had realized him, they would have realized him through the messengers, then he denies the statement of the messengers with the proof. He tests the intellects of all philosophers, and they did not test the natures of all. And third: if it were by composition, the measure of life would not differ. And he said by the nature that the soul does not desire [?] they did not.
Source pages p. 786

Commentary
This passage contains two distinct refutations. First, the author argues from consensus: just as all humans agree that all people die, even without witnessing every death, so too should they accept the truth of prophethood. The objection that one must witness to know is refuted because knowledge can be attained through consensus. Second, the author ridicules the philosophers' argument that animal life ends due to composition, pointing out that if life span were determined by composition alone, it would not vary. The philosophers claim to test all intellects but have not tested all natures, exposing their inconsistency.

Key Terms
تركيب الحيوان
قدر الحياة
الفلسفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Death is purely a physical process determined by composition.
Correction: Maturidi shows that variation in lifespan disproves this, implying a non-physical cause.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi reject the philosophers' claim that death is due to composition?
A) Because composition is irrelevant
B) Because lifespan varies
C) Because the soul is eternal
D) Because they are philosophers

Key Takeaway
Variation in lifespan disproves the idea that composition alone causes death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the different lifespans of people you have known, do you truly believe that their deaths were mere accidents of biology, or do you see the Hand of God? How does your answer affect how you live today?
?
You may have relied on scientific explanations for death, but does this passage force you to reconsider the role of the divine? What would change if you fully believed that every breath is decreed?
?
If composition alone determined death, all would have equal lifespans. Why then has God given you extra time? What account will you give for it?

The Rational Necessity of Accepting Prophetic Signs
Maturidi argues that prophetic signs compel acceptance, like reports in daily life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational argument for why prophetic signs must be accepted, based on the analogy with everyday reports.

Core Idea
If you accept reports in daily life, you must accept the clearer signs of prophets.

Maturidi presents a compelling rational argument: the same logical principle that governs acceptance of everyday news also applies to prophetic claims. He forces the reader to confront their own inconsistency if they reject prophetic signs while trusting ordinary reports.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the dialectical structure: Maturidi poses a question to a hypothetical opponent.
2
Identify the three parts of the argument
Break down the passage into: (1) the statement about signs beyond human capacity, (2) the universal claim about prophets, and (3) the dialectical challenge about reports.
Each part builds on the previous one.
3
Reflect on the analogy
Consider why Maturidi compares prophetic signs to everyday reports. How does this strengthen his argument?
This analogy makes the argument accessible to rational inquiry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد: فقد كثر عنهم الآيات من أنواع ما لا يحتمل ذلك بالاطلاع على الأرض إلا أن يطلعه من علم جواهرها، وفي ذلك الذي ذكر. على أنه ما من نبي صحت نبوته إلا وقد شهد قومه منه من إعلام الصدق ما يجب قبول قوله لولا الآيات. ثم يقال: أنت ممن تقبل خبرا في الدنيا؟ فإن قال: نعم، على صدقه أوضح من أدلة الرسل، وفي ذلك وجوب القول بالذي ذكرت، وإن قال: لا، يشهد عليه العقل وكل شيء جعله حجة بالكذب.
And after: there have been many from them signs of types that cannot be attained by looking at the earth except that He who knows its jewels reveals it. And in that which he mentioned. Indeed, there is no prophet whose prophethood is correct except that his people have witnessed from him of the signs of truthfulness that necessitate acceptance of his statement, were it not for the signs. Then it is said: Are you one who accepts a report in this world? If he says: Yes, then its truthfulness is clearer than the proofs of the messengers, and in that is the obligation of accepting what I mentioned. If he says: No, then reason and everything he made a proof testifies against him with falsehood.
Source pages p. 786

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of accepting prophetic signs. Maturidi begins by stating that prophets have brought signs that cannot be produced by mere human observation of the earth; only God who knows its hidden realities can reveal them. He then asserts that no true prophet has ever appeared without his people witnessing evident signs of truthfulness that compel acceptance, even without further miracles. He then engages a hypothetical opponent by asking whether they accept reports in everyday life. If they do, then the signs of the prophets are even more clear, so they must accept them. If they do not, then reason itself testifies against them, since they have made everything a proof of falsehood. This argument establishes the rational basis for accepting prophetic claims.

Key Terms
آيات
إعلام الصدق
خبر
دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles alone are sufficient without considering the moral character of the prophet.
Correction: Maturidi emphasizes that the signs of truthfulness (i'lam al-sidq) include both miraculous signs and the evident truthfulness of the prophet, not miracles in isolation.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about the acceptance of reports in daily life in relation to prophetic signs?
A) One must reject all reports.
B) If one accepts reports in daily life, then prophetic signs are even clearer and must be accepted.
C) Prophetic signs are not like daily reports.
D) Reason has no role in accepting reports.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that accepting prophetic signs is rationally necessary based on the same principle we use for everyday reports, and inconsistency in rejecting them is self-defeating.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You accept reports from people every day without hesitation. Why then do you hesitate to accept the clearer signs of the prophets? What does this inconsistency reveal about your heart?
?
If you were to take the signs of truthfulness that prophets displayed as seriously as you take the words of a friend, how would your relationship with the Qur'an change starting today?
?
Maturidi argues that rejecting prophetic signs is self-defeating because reason testifies against you. Are there areas of your life where you are rejecting clear signs from Allah out of stubbornness? What concrete step will you take tonight to surrender to that truth?

Refuting Ibn al-Rawandi on Miracles and Nature
Maturidi refutes Ibn al-Rawandi's objection with three arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes Ibn al-Rawandi's argument that prophetic miracles could be dismissed if someone claims a special nature producing similar feats.

Core Idea
Miracles are not refuted by analogies to unverified nature claims.

Ibn al-Rawandi raised a clever objection: if someone claims a special nature to perform extraordinary acts similar to miracles, denying him would be like denying prophets. Maturidi, however, shows that the objection collapses because it relies on unobserved claims and faulty reasoning. This passage is a masterclass in responding to skepticism about prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read Ibn al-Rawandi's hypothetical scenario and his argument that rejecting such a claimant is like rejecting a prophet.
Notice how he tries to create parallel between miracles and unverified nature claims.
2
Identify the flawed presumption
See how Maturidi points out that the denier has no evidence, while the claimant's case is full of conjectures.
The key is that the claimant's claim is not verifiable.
3
Examine the consensus argument
Ibn al-Rawandi also argues from consensus about human death. Maturidi deconstructs this consensus claim.
Consensus about death does not require witnessing every death; but to know about messengers, one needs witness.
4
Learn Maturidi's three refutations
Maturidi gives three clear points: lack of witness, reliance on a past trial, and the need for divine reporting.
These systematically dismantle the objection.
5
Reflect on the implication
Understand why the objection fails and how this defends the necessity of prophethood.
Miracles are distinct from magical nature claims because they are supported by prophetic testimony and reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعارضه ابن الروندي: إن أحدا لو ادعى طبيعة يحدث بها الكواكب أو لو نصبه مقابل الشمس يذهب ضوؤها، أو إنه إذا مس البحر لفظ البحر ما فيه، وإذا مسح به قدمه لصار في الهواء وارتفع إلى السماء ويصير سحابا يمطر، فإذا لزم تكذيب بما ادعى الخروج عن طبائع معروفة فمثله الأول، مع ما كان المكذب ليس معه شيء، ومع الآخر شيء بالظنون يرد وبالاحتمال، وما به قد يمكن عيب، والحجة ظاهرة، فلزم القول به. واحتج على الوراق بما أجمع على موت البشر كلهم وإن لم يشهدوا الكل بالرسل، فقال: فيه الإجماع. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: وقد علم أنه لم يشهد، بل لم يبلغ علمه شيء، والثاني أنه علق دليله في ذلك بالمحنة، وقد زال، والثالث إذ لا يبلغ التدبير ثبت أنه قيل بالرسل.
And Ibn al-Rawandi objected: If someone claimed a nature by which he causes the stars to appear, or if he set himself opposite the sun and it loses its light, or if when he touches the sea it expels what is in it, and when he wipes his foot with it he rises into the air and ascends to the sky and becomes a raincloud that rains, then if it is necessary to deny what he claims as a departure from known natures, then the first case is similar, while the denier has nothing with him, and the other has something that is rejected by conjectures and possibilities, and what may have a defect, yet the proof is apparent, so it is necessary to accept it. And he argued against al-Warraq by the consensus on the death of all humans even if they did not all witness the messengers, so he said: It is consensus. Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: It is known that he did not witness, rather his knowledge did not reach anything, and secondly he hung his proof in that on a trial which has passed, and thirdly since deliberation does not reach it, it is established that it is said by the messengers.
Source pages p. 786

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from Ibn al-Rawandi and Maturidi's response. Ibn al-Rawandi argues that if a person claims a special nature to produce extraordinary feats (like causing stars, blocking sunlight, making the sea expel its contents, or turning foot-wiping into a raincloud), then denying such claims is similar to denying prophetic miracles because both depart from known natures. The denier has no evidence, while the claimant has something that is rejected only by conjecture. Therefore, it is necessary to accept the claimant. He also argues against al-Warraq using the consensus that all humans die, even if no one has witnessed all messengers—so consensus suffices. Maturidi responds with three points: (1) It is known that he did not witness, and his knowledge did not reach that; (2) He based his proof on a trial that has passed; (3) Since deliberation cannot reach it, it is established that it is said by the messengers (i.e., the consensus is based on prophetic reports). This refutes the objection by showing that the analogy is flawed and the consensus argument actually supports the prophets.

Key Terms
طبيعة
طبائع معروفة
إجماع
محنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Ibn al-Rawandi's objection is logically compelling and shows that miracles cannot be distinguished from nature claims.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the objection is self-defeating because it relies on unobserved claims and a flawed analogy; the consensus argument actually supports prophethood.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Maturidi's response to Ibn al-Rawandi's objection?
A) He agrees that miracles can be produced by nature.
B) He shows that Ibn al-Rawandi's argument is based on false analogies and self-defeating.
C) He denies the possibility of miracles altogether.
D) He says that consensus is not valid.

Key Takeaway
Miracles are not analogous to magical nature claims because they are supported by prophetic testimony and reason, and Ibn al-Rawandi's objection is refuted by Maturidi's three arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever rejected a teaching from the Quran or Sunnah because it seemed to contradict your personal experience of 'how nature works'? How does Maturidi's refutation of Ibn al-Rawandi challenge you to reconsider your standard of evidence?
?
Ibn al-Rawandi demanded empirical proof for miracles. In your own life, do you sometimes require visible, tangible evidence for matters of faith? How can you cultivate trust in prophetic testimony even when it defies your expectations?
?
Maturidi points out that Ibn al-Rawandi's argument relies on a 'trial that has passed.' What personal 'trials' or experiences might be clouding your acceptance of divine truths? Are you willing to set them aside and submit to what the messengers have said?

Maturidi's Refutation of Philosophers on Death
Maturidi refutes the philosophers' claim that death results from composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's threefold refutation of the philosophers' claim that death results from the composition of living beings, and how he defends the necessity of prophetic miracles.

Core Idea
Death is not merely a result of bodily composition; it is from Allah, confirmed by miracles beyond human power.

Imam Maturidi here targets the philosophers' naturalistic explanation of death. He calls their reasoning foolish and presents three arguments to show that death cannot be solely due to the composition of living beings. This passage also introduces the role of miracles as decisive proof for prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' position
Read the opening line where Maturidi states the philosophers' claim: 'The composition of the animal is a composition that dies.' Understand what they are asserting about death and material composition.
This is the thesis Maturidi will refute.
2
Examine Maturidi's three arguments
Look for the three distinct arguments Maturidi presents. First, he criticizes their incomplete testing of natures. Second, he points to variation in lifespans. Third, he addresses their claim about the soul's incapacity and responds with three counterpoints, including the nature of miracles.
The third argument has its own internal structure.
3
Understand the role of miracles
Focus on Maturidi's final response where he distinguishes between miracles that are difficult but conceivable and those that are utterly impossible (like the splitting of the moon). Recognize how this defends prophethood against philosophical skepticism.
This shows that prophetic proof is not merely philosophical but empirical and supernatural.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال في قول الفلسفة: إن تركيب الحيوان تركيب يموت. تأملوا حماقته، بعد قول قوم لو أدركوه لأدركوه بالرسل، ثم ينكر قول الرسل مع البرهان. يمتحن عقول جميع الفلاسفة، ولا امتحنوا طبائع الجميع. والثالث أنه لو كان بالتركيب لما اختلف قدر الحياة. فقالوا بالطباع إن النفس لا تطمع في دفعه ولا ترجو الظفر به، فجوابه إنه لم يمتحن طبائع الكل، والثاني أنها سكنت إلى هذا بالتوارث من قول الرسل، والثالث كذلك آيات الرسل لم يطمع إلا بعسر إتيان مثلها، وهي بحيث تحتمل الطمع، مع ما كانت فيها ما لا يطمع مع التقريع والتحذير، وفيها ما لا يحتمل الطمع البتة نحو انشقاق القمر.
And he said regarding the statement of the philosophers: The composition of the animal is a composition that dies. Reflect on their foolishness, after the statement of a people who, if they had understood it, would have understood it by the messengers, then they deny the statement of the messengers despite the proof. He tests the intellects of all philosophers, and they have not tested the natures of all. And thirdly, if it were by composition, the duration of life would not differ. They said by natures that the soul does not hope to repel it nor hope to overcome it. The response to him is that he did not test the natures of all, and secondly that they became tranquil with this by inheritance from the statement of the messengers, and thirdly likewise the signs of the messengers are not hoped for except with difficulty of producing their like, and they are such that hope is possible, while among them are what cannot be hoped for at all with reproach and warning, and among them what does not admit hope at all like the splitting of the moon.
Source pages p. 787

Commentary
This passage refutes the philosophers' claim that death is due to the composition of living beings. Maturidi first calls attention to their foolishness: they deny the messengers despite the clear proof of miracles. He offers three arguments: (1) The philosophers test the intellects of all philosophers, but they have not tested all natures (so they cannot claim universal knowledge). (2) If death were solely due to composition, then lifespans would not differ; the fact that lifespans vary shows it is not merely a result of composition. (3) The philosophers say that the soul cannot hope to repel death, but Maturidi responds: they have not tested all natures; they inherited this view from the messengers; and prophetic miracles are of two kinds: those which people might hope to imitate with difficulty, and those which are utterly impossible (like the splitting of the moon). This shows that the philosophers' argument is weak and that miracles confirm the truth of the messengers.

Key Terms
تركيب
حيوان
موت
نفس
طبائع
انشقاق القمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Death is purely a natural result of the body's composition, and there is no need for prophets or divine intervention.
Correction: Maturidi shows that death is not merely due to composition (lifespans vary) and that prophets are confirmed by miracles that cannot be explained naturally, such as the splitting of the moon.

Checkpoint
What is the second (or third, as per the passage) argument Maturidi gives against the philosophers' claim that death is due to composition?
A) Lifespans would differ if it were due to composition.
B) Lifespans would be equal if it were due to composition.
C) Composition has no effect on death.
D) The philosophers have tested all natures.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that death is not simply a result of composition, as lifespans vary, and the most certain proof of prophethood is miracles beyond human ability, like the splitting of the moon.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about death, do you attribute it more to natural causes like age or disease, or do you truly believe it comes from Allah? How does your daily attitude reflect that belief?
?
Maturidi says the philosophers did not test all natures before making a universal claim. Where in your own life have you made sweeping judgments based on limited evidence, especially about matters of faith? Will you humble yourself and seek more before concluding?
?
The splitting of the moon is a miracle that no one could hope to imitate. If you witnessed such a miracle today, would you submit immediately? Or would you still make excuses? What does your hesitation reveal about your heart?

Refuting the Total Skeptic
Maturidi refutes the complete skeptic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that total skepticism is self-defeating and cannot be consistently maintained.

Core Idea
Total skepticism self-destructs because the skeptic must affirm a belief to argue.

Before establishing prophethood, Maturidi clears the ground by addressing the radical skeptic who denies all knowledge. He shows that such a position cannot even be stated without contradiction. This passage prepares the way for accepting the messengers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note how the argument unfolds.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two options
The skeptic is asked if he believes anything. Trace the two possible answers (no/yes) and see how each leads to a problem for the skeptic.
The 'no' option renders his denial meaningless; the 'yes' option subjects him to the same uncertainty he accuses others of.
3
Understand the final principle
Maturidi concludes that anyone who rejects all knowledge in favor of pure possibility abolishes the very basis of asserting or denying anything.
This places the total skeptic outside rational discourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال له: تعتقد شيئا البتة؟ فإن قال: لا، أقر أنه لم يعتقد تكذيب من ذكر، ولا أنه هو، ولا هو حي أو ميت، فتكلفه الأجوبة والمعارضات خطأ، وإن قال: نعم، قيل: لعلك تعتقده بما لم يبلغ قوة دركك وعلمك بالأشياء مبلغ الإحالة؛ إذ قد رأيت كثيرا من المعتقدين بطل اعتقادهم، فلعل طبيعتك أرتك ذلك الفساد، ويجوز أن يكون في الطبائع طبيعة نقية يدرك لذلك فيما اعتقدت، ويظهر جهلك. فمها قال من شيء فهو له - في جميع ما أنكر جواب. وأصله أن كل من استخار الخروج من المعارف والتفوه بغير الموجود في الطبائع بلا شيء سوى أنه لم يكن أو لعله يكون، أبطل سبيل تثبيت شيء البتة أو نفيه، ويكون في حد الشاكين في البيان كله، ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم الأصل عندنا في إعلام الرسل وجهان: أحدهما ظهور أحوالهم على جهة يدفع العقول عنهم الريبة وتأبى فيهم توهم الظنة بما صحبوهم في الصغر والكبر فوجدوهم ظاهرين أصفياء أتقياء بين أظهر قوم، ما أحتمل التسوية بينهم على ذلك، ولا تربيتهم تبلغ ذلك، على ظهور أحوالهم لهم وكونهم بينهم في القرار والانتشار، فيعلم بإحاطة أن ذلك حفظ من يعلم أنه يقيمهم مقاما.
Then it is said to him: Do you believe anything at all? If he says: No, he admits that he does not believe in the denial of those mentioned, nor that he himself is alive or dead, so burdening him with answers and objections is a mistake. If he says: Yes, it is said: Perhaps you believe it by something that has not reached the strength of your perception and your knowledge of things to the degree of impossibility; for you have seen many believers whose belief became invalid, so perhaps your nature showed you that corruption, and it is possible that among natures there is a pure nature that perceives that which you believed, and your ignorance appears. So whatever he says from anything, it is an answer to him in everything he denied. The principle is that whoever seeks to exit from knowledge and speaks of non-existent things in natures with nothing except that it was not or perhaps it will be, he abolishes the way of establishing anything at all or negating it, and he is in the category of the doubters in all clarification, and there is no power but in God. Then the principle with us regarding the signs of the messengers is two aspects: First, the manifestation of their states in a way that prevents intellects from doubting them and refuses suspicion of them, because they accompanied them in youth and old age and found them apparent, pure, pious among their people, such that equality between them and others is not conceivable, nor does their upbringing reach that, due to the manifestation of their states to them and their being among them in settlement and movement, so they know comprehensively that this is preservation from One who knows that He will establish them in a station.
Source pages p. 787

Commentary
This passage contains two parts. First, Maturidi refutes the complete skeptic by asking if they believe anything at all. If they say no, they admit that they do not even hold a belief in denial or in their own existence, so arguing with them is futile. If they say yes, then their belief is subject to the same uncertainty they ascribe to others; they might be mistaken, and a pure nature could reveal the truth to them, exposing their ignorance. This shows that skepticism is self-defeating. The principle is that those who reject all knowledge and speak of non-existent possibilities destroy the possibility of affirming or denying anything, placing them among the doubters. Then Maturidi states the two principles for establishing prophethood. The first principle is the manifest upright character of the prophets: their people observed them from childhood to adulthood and saw them as pure, pious, and above suspicion. Their states were so consistent that equality with others is inconceivable, and their upbringing could not have produced such virtue. This comprehensive observation indicates that they were preserved by God who knows that He will place them in a high station. The second principle (implied but not fully written here) is likely the miracles themselves, but the text only details the first. So the passage provides a rational and empirical basis for accepting prophets.

Key Terms
شك
طبائع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Total skepticism is a rational, consistent position.
Correction: Maturidi shows that total skepticism collapses because the skeptic must either admit no belief (making argument impossible) or admit a belief (subjecting it to critique).

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say is the result if the skeptic claims to believe nothing?
A) The skeptic is consistent and cannot be refuted.
B) The skeptic admits he does not even believe in his own denial or existence, making argument pointless.
C) The skeptic must be punished for denial of truth.
D) The skeptic is forced to accept prophethood.

Key Takeaway
Total skepticism is self-defeating because it destroys the basis for any rational assertion, including its own.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever entertained doubts about the most basic certainties (like your own existence) as an intellectual exercise? Did that exercise lead you to real clarity or only confusion? How does Maturidi’s argument force you to recognize the dead end of total skepticism?
?
When you face someone who claims that nothing can be known for sure, does your heart tremble or remain anchored? Based on this lesson, what specific truth about skepticism can you hold onto so that your faith is not shaken by such arguments? Commit to memorizing one sentence from the passage that exposes the flaw in skepticism.
?
The passage ends with 'there is no power but in Allah'—a recognition that certainty ultimately comes from Him. In what area of your life are you acting as if you can establish truth on your own, without relying on Allah’s guidance? Write down one step you will take today to reorient that area toward Him.

The First Principle of Prophethood
Maturidi gives the first principle of establishing prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's first principle for establishing the truthfulness of prophets: their manifest moral character and public witness.

Core Idea
Prophets are known by their flawless character, witnessed by their communities from youth.

After refuting skepticism, Maturidi turns to how we know the true prophets. He presents two principles, the first of which is the visible purity and piety of the prophets throughout their lives. Their own people, who knew them best, found them beyond reproach, proving divine selection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that Maturidi mentions two aspects but elaborates only the first.
The passage ends mid-thought; the second aspect is implied but not detailed here.
2
Identify the key elements of the first aspect

The phrase 'ظهور أحوالهم' is central.
3
Understand the logic
The argument is that such consistency could not be achieved by upbringing or human effort; it must be divine preservation.
This connects to the purpose of establishing a station given by God.

Then it is said to him: Do you believe anything at all? If he says: Yes, it is said: Perhaps you believe it by something that has not reached the strength of your perception and your knowledge of things to the degree of impossibility; for you have seen many believers whose belief became invalid, so perhaps your nature showed you that corruption, and it is possible that among natures there is a pure nature that perceives that which you believed, and your ignorance appears. So whatever he says from anything, it is an answer to him in everything he denied. Prophethood

Refutation of the Skeptic on Prophethood
Maturidi refutes a skeptic by exposing the self-defeating nature of his doubt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes a skeptic who denies prophethood by showing that his position is self-defeating and leads to total doubt.

Core Idea
The skeptic's doubt is self-refuting: it denies all certainty, including itself.

Maturidi confronts a skeptic who rejects prophethood on the grounds that we cannot be certain about the truth of miracles or inherited beliefs. Rather than engaging in a direct defense of prophethood, Maturidi turns the skeptic's own logic against him. He shows that the skeptic's position, if taken consistently, undermines all knowledge and leads to absurdity. This passage is a masterclass in using logical dilemmas to refute radical skepticism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very condensed style here.
2
Identify the skeptic's three objections
The passage mentions three points the skeptic might raise: not examining all natures, inherited belief, and the difficulty of miracles. Find each one and note how Maturidi responds.
These are the first three 'fā's in the Arabic.
3
Analyze the dilemma
Maturidi poses a dilemma to the skeptic: either he believes nothing at all, or he believes something. Understand the implications of each branch and why the dilemma is effective.
If the skeptic believes nothing, he cannot coherently deny anything. If he believes something, his belief is subject to the same uncertainty he casts on others.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that the skeptic's position invalidates the way of establishing or negating anything, placing him among the perpetual doubters. Consider why this is a decisive refutation.
This principle applies to any form of radical skepticism, not just about prophethood.
5
Apply the argument
Think about how Maturidi's argument could be used against other forms of skepticism. Can you think of a modern example where someone's doubt is self-refuting?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فى دفعه ولا ترجو الظفر به ، فجوابه إنه لم يمتحن طبائع الكل ، والثانى أنها سكنت إلى هذا بالتوارث من قول الرسل ، والثالث كذلك آيات الرسل لم يطمع إلا بعسر إتيان مثلها ، وهى بحيث تحتمل الطمع ، مع ما كانت فيها ما لا يطمع مع التقريع والتحذير ، وفيها ما لا يحتمل الطمع البتة نحو انشقاق القمر . ثم يقال له : تعتقد شيئا البتة ؟ فان قال : لا ، أقر أنه لم يعتقد تكذيب من ذكر ، ولا أنه هو ، ولا هو حى أو ميت ، فتكلفه الأجوبة والمعارضات خطأ ، وإن قال : نعم ، قيل : لعلك تعتقده بما لم يبلغ قوة دركك وعلمك بالأشياء مبلغ الإحالة ؛ إذ قد رأيت كثيرا من المعتقدين بطل اعتقادهم ، فلعل طبيعتك أرتك ذلك الفساد ، ويجوز أن يكون في الطبائع طبيعة نقية يدرك لذلك فيما اعتقدت ، ويظهر جهلك . فمها قال من شيء فهو له - فى جميع ما أنكر جواب . وأصله أن كل من استخار الخروج من المعارف والتفوه بغير الموجود في الطبائع بلا شيء سوى أنه لم يكن أو لعله يكون ، أبطل سبيل تثبيت شيء البتة أو نفيه ، ويكون فى حد الشاكين فى البيان كله ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
In refuting him, and do not hope to attain it, so his answer is that he has not examined the natures of all [people], and second, that they have settled on this by inheritance from the saying of the messengers, and third, likewise the signs of the messengers are not hoped for except with difficulty of producing their like, and they are such that they admit hope, while among them are what cannot be hoped for at all, like the splitting of the moon. Then it is said to him: Do you believe anything at all? If he says: No, he admits that he does not believe the denial of those mentioned, nor that he is, nor that he is alive or dead, so his undertaking of answers and objections is a mistake. If he says: Yes, it is said: Perhaps you believe it by something that has not reached the strength of your perception and your knowledge of things the level of impossibility; for you have seen many believers whose belief turned out false, so perhaps your nature showed you that corruption, and it is possible that among natures there is a pure nature that perceives that regarding what you believed, and your ignorance appears. So whatever he says of something, it is for him — in all that he denies an answer. The principle is that whoever seeks to exit from knowledge and to utter other than what is present in natures with nothing except that it was not or perhaps it will be, he invalidates the way of establishing anything at all or negating it, and he becomes among the doubters in all explanation, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 787

Commentary
This passage is a polemical refutation of a skeptic who denies the truth of prophethood. Maturidi begins by anticipating the skeptic's objection that he has not examined all natures, and that people accept prophethood only through inherited custom. He then points out that the miracles of the prophets, such as the splitting of the moon, are beyond human capacity and not subject to hope of imitation. He then poses a dilemma to the skeptic: either he believes nothing at all, in which case he cannot coherently deny anything, or he believes something, in which case his belief is subject to the same uncertainty he attributes to others, since many believers have been wrong. Maturidi concludes that the skeptic's position undermines all certainty, placing him among the perpetual doubters.

شك
آية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The skeptic thinks his doubt is a rational, consistent position.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the skeptic's doubt is self-refuting: it cannot be maintained without undermining its own basis.

Checkpoint
What dilemma does Maturidi present to the skeptic?
A) Either he believes in prophethood or he is an atheist.
B) Either he believes nothing, making his denial meaningless, or he believes something, making him subject to the same uncertainty.
C) Either he accepts miracles or he rejects reason.
D) Either he follows the prophets or he follows the sorcerers.

Key Takeaway
The skeptic who denies prophethood cannot consistently maintain his position; his doubt destroys the basis for any knowledge, including his own denial.

?
When you question the truth of Islam, do you also question the basis of your questioning? Are you willing to examine whether your doubts themselves depend on assumptions you cannot prove? What would change if you held your doubts to the same standard you hold your beliefs?

First Principle of Prophethood: Moral Character
Maturidi explains the first principle for identifying true prophets: their irreproachable character known from childhood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's first principle for identifying true prophets: their lifelong irreproachable character, publicly known, makes rejection irrational and obstinate.

Core Idea
A prophet's character, known from childhood, makes rejection a matter of obstinacy, not reason.

Maturidi begins his discussion of prophethood with a key question: How can we know someone is truly a messenger of God? He argues that one principle is the public witness of their moral excellence since childhood. This passage explains why such character makes rejection unreasonable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: Maturidi states there are two aspects, then elaborates on the first.
The passage is continuous; try to grasp the flow of argument.
2
Identify the first principle
Look for the description of the prophets' character: how they were observed from childhood to adulthood, found pure and pious, and how this public knowledge precludes suspicion.
Key phrase: 'ظهور أحوالهم على جهة يدفع العقول عنهم الريبة'
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why Maturidi says that rejection after this knowledge can only be due to obstinacy, habit, worldly honor, or desires. How does this strengthen the case for prophethood?
This connects to the learning objective about rational grounds for accepting prophets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل عندنا في إعلام الرسل وجهان : أحدهما ظهور أحوالهم على جهة يدفع العقول عنهم الريبة وتأبى فيهم توهم الظنة بما صحبوهم في الصغر والكبر فوجدوهم ظاهرين أصفياء أتقياء بين أظهر قوم ، ما أحتمل التسوية بينهم على ذلك ، ولا تربيتهم تبلغ ذلك ، على ظهور أحوالهم لهم وكونهم بينهم فى القرار والانتشار ، فيعلم باحاطة أن ذلك حفظ من يعلم أنه يقيمهم مقاما شريفا ، ويجعلهم أمناء على العيوب والأسرار ، وهذا مما يميل إلى قبوله الطبيعة ، ويستحسن جميع أمورهم العقل ، فيكون الراد عليه يرد بعد المعرفة رد تعنت له اما لألف وعادة على خلاف ذلك ، أو لشرف ونباهة فى العاجل أو لمطامع ومنال ، وإلا فما من قلب إلا ويميل إلى من دون هذا رتبته ومحله ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then the principle with us regarding the informing of the messengers is two aspects: One of them is the appearance of their states in a manner that repels suspicion from them by intellects and prevents the imagination of suspicion in them because of what they accompanied them in childhood and adulthood, and they found them apparent pure pious among their people, such that equality between them upon that is not possible, nor does their upbringing reach that, upon the appearance of their states to them and their being among them in settlement and dispersal, so it is known comprehensively that that is preservation from Him who knows that He establishes them in a noble station, and makes them trustees over faults and secrets. And this is something that nature inclines to accept, and reason approves all their affairs, so the rejecter against him rejects after knowledge a rejection of obstinacy, either due to familiarity and habit contrary to that, or for immediate honor and prominence, or for desires and gains. Otherwise, there is no heart but that it inclines to one whose rank and position are less than this, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 787

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two principles for establishing the truth of prophethood. Maturidi argues that the prophets' entire lives, from childhood to adulthood, were observed by their communities, who saw them as pure, pious, and trustworthy. This public knowledge makes it impossible to suspect them of fraud or self-interest. Reason and human nature naturally incline to accept such individuals as messengers of God. Therefore, anyone who rejects them after knowing their character does so out of obstinacy, habit, worldly ambition, or greed, not on rational grounds.

Key Takeaway

Prophethood

Second Proof: Miracles Beyond Human Capacity
Maturidi explains the second proof of prophethood: miracles are beyond human learning and capacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second principle of prophethood: that miracles are beyond human learning and capacity, and how this distinguishes them from sorcery.

Core Idea
Prophetic miracles cannot be learned; they come directly from Allah.

Maturidi now turns to the second principle that proves prophethood: the miracle itself. He explains that true miracles are outside the natural ability of experts in that field and cannot be replicated through learning. This passage also contrasts prophetic miracles with sorcery, showing that even sorcery has a heavenly origin but is transmitted through learning, whereas miracles are direct divine gifts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the key phrases about impossibility of learning and the contrast with sorcery.
The Arabic uses terms like 'خارجة عن طبائع' and 'ممتنعة عن أن يطمع فى مثلها'.
2
Identify the three features of prophetic miracles
From the passage, find: (1) miracles are outside the natures of experts, (2) impossible to replicate or reach their essence through learning, (3) even if learning could produce them, the prophets' upbringing shows they come from Allah.
These features prove the divine origin.
3
Understand the distinction from sorcery
Maturidi says sorcery origined from heaven but people forgot and transmit it by learning. Prophetic miracles are not learned. Compare and contrast.
This guards against the misunderstanding that miracles are a type of advanced sorcery.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني مجيء الآيات الخارجة عن طبائع أهل البصر في ذلك النوع الممتنعة عن أن يطمع فى مثلها أو يبلغ بكنهها التعلم ، مع ما لو احتمل أن يبلغ أحد ذلك بالتعلم والاجتهاد فإن الرسل بما نشأوا لا في ذلك ، وربوا لا به يظهر أنهم استفادوه بالله ، أكرمهم بذلك ؛ لما يجعلهم أمناء على وجيه . ولهم أيضا معانى فاقوا بها السحرة ، على أن علم السحر أصله من السماء ، لكن الناس نسوا أصله وتوارثوه بالتعلم ، وكذلك المكاسب والحرف والصناعات كلها ، فمن أكرم لا بالوجه الذي هو طريقه في المعارف عليم أن ذلك تخصيص الأمر عظيم
And the second is the coming of signs that are outside the natures of people of insight in that type, impossible to hope for their like or to attain their essence by learning, along with the fact that if it were possible that someone could attain that by learning and effort, then the messengers, by virtue of their upbringing not in that [field] and their not being raised thereby, show that they acquired it from Allah, He honored them with that, to make them trustees over His face. And they also have meanings by which they surpassed sorcerers, given that the origin of sorcery is from heaven, but people forgot its origin and inherited it by learning, and likewise all earnings, crafts, and industries. So whoever is honored by the way that is not his path in knowledge, knows that that is a great specification.
Source pages p. 788

Commentary
This passage presents the second principle for establishing prophethood: the miracles (āyāt) brought by prophets are beyond the capacity of human experts in that field, and cannot be replicated by learning. Even if learning could theoretically produce such wonders, the prophets were not trained in those arts, proving that their power comes from God. Maturidi also distinguishes prophetic miracles from sorcery: sorcery has a heavenly origin but is learned and transmitted, whereas miracles are direct divine gifts. The fact that prophets are given these powers without normal learning shows God's special selection.

Key Terms
آيات
طبائع
تعلم
سحر
تخصيص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles are a form of advanced sorcery.
Correction: Maturidi explains that sorcery is learned and transmitted, whereas prophetic miracles are not acquired through learning, and the prophets were not trained in those arts, proving divine origin.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi distinguish prophetic miracles from sorcery?
A) Miracles are more powerful than sorcery.
B) Sorcery is learned and transmitted, while prophetic miracles are not acquired through learning.
C) Sorcery is from devils, while miracles are from angels.
D) Miracles are only performed in public, while sorcery is secret.

Key Takeaway
Prophetic miracles are distinguished by being impossible to learn or replicate, proving their divine origin and the special status of prophets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the fact that prophets were not trained in the miracle they brought challenge your reliance on formal qualifications and credentials in matters of faith? Will you now trust that Allah can guide even the unlettered, or will you demand worldly proofs?
?
If you truly believe that prophetic miracles are beyond human learning, why do you still hesitate to fully submit to the prophetic message? What is holding you back from accepting that their testimony is from Allah, not from human effort?
?
The source distinguishes between sorcery (learned) and miracles (divine gift). Have you been treating your own talents and successes as if they came from your effort alone, forgetting that all true honor is a specification from Allah? Will you now acknowledge that your abilities are ultimately gifts, and live with gratitude?
